  46: ACT I. Scene I.
  47: Elsinore. A platform before the Castle.
  48: 
  49: Enter two Sentinels-[first,] Francisco, [who paces up and down
  50: at his post; then] Bernardo, [who approaches him].
  51: 
  52:   Ber. Who's there.?
  53:   Fran. Nay, answer me. Stand and unfold yourself.
  54:   Ber. Long live the King!
  55:   Fran. Bernardo?
  56:   Ber. He.
  57:   Fran. You come most carefully upon your hour.
  58:   Ber. 'Tis now struck twelve. Get thee to bed, Francisco.
  59:   Fran. For this relief much thanks. 'Tis bitter cold,
  60:     And I am sick at heart.
  61:   Ber. Have you had quiet guard?
  62:   Fran. Not a mouse stirring.
  63:   Ber. Well, good night.
  64:     If you do meet Horatio and Marcellus,
  65:     The rivals of my watch, bid them make haste.
  66: 
  67:                     Enter Horatio and Marcellus.  
  68: 
  69:   Fran. I think I hear them. Stand, ho! Who is there?
  70:   Hor. Friends to this ground.
  71:   Mar. And liegemen to the Dane.
  72:   Fran. Give you good night.
  73:   Mar. O, farewell, honest soldier.
  74:     Who hath reliev'd you?
  75:   Fran. Bernardo hath my place.
  76:     Give you good night.                                   Exit.
  77:   Mar. Holla, Bernardo!
  78:   Ber. Say-
  79:     What, is Horatio there ?
  80:   Hor. A piece of him.
  81:   Ber. Welcome, Horatio. Welcome, good Marcellus.
  82:   Mar. What, has this thing appear'd again to-night?
  83:   Ber. I have seen nothing.
  84:   Mar. Horatio says 'tis but our fantasy,
  85:     And will not let belief take hold of him
  86:     Touching this dreaded sight, twice seen of us.
  87:     Therefore I have entreated him along,  
  88:     With us to watch the minutes of this night,
  89:     That, if again this apparition come,
  90:     He may approve our eyes and speak to it.
  91:   Hor. Tush, tush, 'twill not appear.
  92:   Ber. Sit down awhile,
  93:     And let us once again assail your ears,
  94:     That are so fortified against our story,
  95:     What we two nights have seen.
  96:   Hor. Well, sit we down,
  97:     And let us hear Bernardo speak of this.
  98:   Ber. Last night of all,
  99:     When yond same star that's westward from the pole
 100:     Had made his course t' illume that part of heaven
 101:     Where now it burns, Marcellus and myself,
 102:     The bell then beating one-
 103: 
 104:                         Enter Ghost.
 105: 
 106:   Mar. Peace! break thee off! Look where it comes again!
 107:   Ber. In the same figure, like the King that's dead.  
 108:   Mar. Thou art a scholar; speak to it, Horatio.
 109:   Ber. Looks it not like the King? Mark it, Horatio.
 110:   Hor. Most like. It harrows me with fear and wonder.
 111:   Ber. It would be spoke to.
 112:   Mar. Question it, Horatio.
 113:   Hor. What art thou that usurp'st this time of night
 114:     Together with that fair and warlike form
 115:     In which the majesty of buried Denmark
 116:     Did sometimes march? By heaven I charge thee speak!
 117:   Mar. It is offended.
 118:   Ber. See, it stalks away!
 119:   Hor. Stay! Speak, speak! I charge thee speak!
 120:                                                      Exit Ghost.
 121:   Mar. 'Tis gone and will not answer.
 122:   Ber. How now, Horatio? You tremble and look pale.
 123:     Is not this something more than fantasy?
 124:     What think you on't?
 125:   Hor. Before my God, I might not this believe
 126:     Without the sensible and true avouch
 127:     Of mine own eyes.  
 128:   Mar. Is it not like the King?
 129:   Hor. As thou art to thyself.
 130:     Such was the very armour he had on
 131:     When he th' ambitious Norway combated.
 132:     So frown'd he once when, in an angry parle,
 133:     He smote the sledded Polacks on the ice.
 134:     'Tis strange.
 135:   Mar. Thus twice before, and jump at this dead hour,
 136:     With martial stalk hath he gone by our watch.
 137:   Hor. In what particular thought to work I know not;
 138:     But, in the gross and scope of my opinion,
 139:     This bodes some strange eruption to our state.
 140:   Mar. Good now, sit down, and tell me he that knows,
 141:     Why this same strict and most observant watch
 142:     So nightly toils the subject of the land,
 143:     And why such daily cast of brazen cannon
 144:     And foreign mart for implements of war;
 145:     Why such impress of shipwrights, whose sore task
 146:     Does not divide the Sunday from the week.
 147:     What might be toward, that this sweaty haste  
 148:     Doth make the night joint-labourer with the day?
 149:     Who is't that can inform me?
 150:   Hor. That can I.
 151:     At least, the whisper goes so. Our last king,
 152:     Whose image even but now appear'd to us,
 153:     Was, as you know, by Fortinbras of Norway,
 154:     Thereto prick'd on by a most emulate pride,
 155:     Dar'd to the combat; in which our valiant Hamlet
 156:     (For so this side of our known world esteem'd him)
 157:     Did slay this Fortinbras; who, by a seal'd compact,
 158:     Well ratified by law and heraldry,
 159:     Did forfeit, with his life, all those his lands
 160:     Which he stood seiz'd of, to the conqueror;
 161:     Against the which a moiety competent
 162:     Was gaged by our king; which had return'd
 163:     To the inheritance of Fortinbras,
 164:     Had he been vanquisher, as, by the same comart
 165:     And carriage of the article design'd,
 166:     His fell to Hamlet. Now, sir, young Fortinbras,
 167:     Of unimproved mettle hot and full,  
 168:     Hath in the skirts of Norway, here and there,
 169:     Shark'd up a list of lawless resolutes,
 170:     For food and diet, to some enterprise
 171:     That hath a stomach in't; which is no other,
 172:     As it doth well appear unto our state,
 173:     But to recover of us, by strong hand
 174:     And terms compulsatory, those foresaid lands
 175:     So by his father lost; and this, I take it,
 176:     Is the main motive of our preparations,
 177:     The source of this our watch, and the chief head
 178:     Of this post-haste and romage in the land.
 179:   Ber. I think it be no other but e'en so.
 180:     Well may it sort that this portentous figure
 181:     Comes armed through our watch, so like the King
 182:     That was and is the question of these wars.
 183:   Hor. A mote it is to trouble the mind's eye.
 184:     In the most high and palmy state of Rome,
 185:     A little ere the mightiest Julius fell,
 186:     The graves stood tenantless, and the sheeted dead
 187:     Did squeak and gibber in the Roman streets;  
 188:     As stars with trains of fire, and dews of blood,
 189:     Disasters in the sun; and the moist star
 190:     Upon whose influence Neptune's empire stands
 191:     Was sick almost to doomsday with eclipse.
 192:     And even the like precurse of fierce events,
 193:     As harbingers preceding still the fates
 194:     And prologue to the omen coming on,
 195:     Have heaven and earth together demonstrated
 196:     Unto our climature and countrymen.
 197: 
 198:                       Enter Ghost again.
 199: 
 200:     But soft! behold! Lo, where it comes again!
 201:     I'll cross it, though it blast me.- Stay illusion!
 202:                                                Spreads his arms.
 203:     If thou hast any sound, or use of voice,
 204:     Speak to me.
 205:     If there be any good thing to be done,
 206:     That may to thee do ease, and, race to me,
 207:     Speak to me.  
 208:     If thou art privy to thy country's fate,
 209:     Which happily foreknowing may avoid,
 210:     O, speak!
 211:     Or if thou hast uphoarded in thy life
 212:     Extorted treasure in the womb of earth
 213:     (For which, they say, you spirits oft walk in death),
 214:                                                  The cock crows.
 215:     Speak of it! Stay, and speak!- Stop it, Marcellus!
 216:   Mar. Shall I strike at it with my partisan?
 217:   Hor. Do, if it will not stand.
 218:   Ber. 'Tis here!
 219:   Hor. 'Tis here!
 220:   Mar. 'Tis gone!
 221:                                                      Exit Ghost.
 222:     We do it wrong, being so majestical,
 223:     To offer it the show of violence;
 224:     For it is as the air, invulnerable,
 225:     And our vain blows malicious mockery.
 226:   Ber. It was about to speak, when the cock crew.
 227:   Hor. And then it started, like a guilty thing  
 228:     Upon a fearful summons. I have heard
 229:     The cock, that is the trumpet to the morn,
 230:     Doth with his lofty and shrill-sounding throat
 231:     Awake the god of day; and at his warning,
 232:     Whether in sea or fire, in earth or air,
 233:     Th' extravagant and erring spirit hies
 234:     To his confine; and of the truth herein
 235:     This present object made probation.
 236:   Mar. It faded on the crowing of the cock.
 237:     Some say that ever, 'gainst that season comes
 238:     Wherein our Saviour's birth is celebrated,
 239:     The bird of dawning singeth all night long;
 240:     And then, they say, no spirit dare stir abroad,
 241:     The nights are wholesome, then no planets strike,
 242:     No fairy takes, nor witch hath power to charm,
 243:     So hallow'd and so gracious is the time.
 244:   Hor. So have I heard and do in part believe it.
 245:     But look, the morn, in russet mantle clad,
 246:     Walks o'er the dew of yon high eastward hill.
 247:     Break we our watch up; and by my advice  
 248:     Let us impart what we have seen to-night
 249:     Unto young Hamlet; for, upon my life,
 250:     This spirit, dumb to us, will speak to him.
 251:     Do you consent we shall acquaint him with it,
 252:     As needful in our loves, fitting our duty?
 253:     Let's do't, I pray; and I this morning know
 254:     Where we shall find him most conveniently.           Exeunt.
 255: 
 256: 
 257: 
 258: 
 259: Scene II.
 260: Elsinore. A room of state in the Castle.
 261: 
 262: Flourish. [Enter Claudius, King of Denmark, Gertrude the Queen, Hamlet,
 263: Polonius, Laertes and his sister Ophelia, [Voltemand, Cornelius,]
 264: Lords Attendant.
 265: 
 266:   King. Though yet of Hamlet our dear brother's death
 267:     The memory be green, and that it us befitted
 268:     To bear our hearts in grief, and our whole kingdom
 269:     To be contracted in one brow of woe,
 270:     Yet so far hath discretion fought with nature
 271:     That we with wisest sorrow think on him
 272:     Together with remembrance of ourselves.
 273:     Therefore our sometime sister, now our queen,
 274:     Th' imperial jointress to this warlike state,
 275:     Have we, as 'twere with a defeated joy,
 276:     With an auspicious, and a dropping eye,
 277:     With mirth in funeral, and with dirge in marriage,
 278:     In equal scale weighing delight and dole,
 279:     Taken to wife; nor have we herein barr'd
 280:     Your better wisdoms, which have freely gone  
 281:     With this affair along. For all, our thanks.
 282:     Now follows, that you know, young Fortinbras,
 283:     Holding a weak supposal of our worth,
 284:     Or thinking by our late dear brother's death
 285:     Our state to be disjoint and out of frame,
 286:     Colleagued with this dream of his advantage,
 287:     He hath not fail'd to pester us with message
 288:     Importing the surrender of those lands
 289:     Lost by his father, with all bands of law,
 290:     To our most valiant brother. So much for him.
 291:     Now for ourself and for this time of meeting.
 292:     Thus much the business is: we have here writ
 293:     To Norway, uncle of young Fortinbras,
 294:     Who, impotent and bedrid, scarcely hears
 295:     Of this his nephew's purpose, to suppress
 296:     His further gait herein, in that the levies,
 297:     The lists, and full proportions are all made
 298:     Out of his subject; and we here dispatch
 299:     You, good Cornelius, and you, Voltemand,
 300:     For bearers of this greeting to old Norway,  
 301:     Giving to you no further personal power
 302:     To business with the King, more than the scope
 303:     Of these dilated articles allow.            [Gives a paper.]
 304:     Farewell, and let your haste commend your duty.
 305:   Cor., Volt. In that, and all things, will we show our duty.
 306:   King. We doubt it nothing. Heartily farewell.
 307:                                  Exeunt Voltemand and Cornelius.
 308:     And now, Laertes, what's the news with you?
 309:     You told us of some suit. What is't, Laertes?
 310:     You cannot speak of reason to the Dane
 311:     And lose your voice. What wouldst thou beg, Laertes,
 312:     That shall not be my offer, not thy asking?
 313:     The head is not more native to the heart,
 314:     The hand more instrumental to the mouth,
 315:     Than is the throne of Denmark to thy father.
 316:     What wouldst thou have, Laertes?
 317:   Laer. My dread lord,
 318:     Your leave and favour to return to France;
 319:     From whence though willingly I came to Denmark
 320:     To show my duty in your coronation,  
 321:     Yet now I must confess, that duty done,
 322:     My thoughts and wishes bend again toward France
 323:     And bow them to your gracious leave and pardon.
 324:   King. Have you your father's leave? What says Polonius?
 325:   Pol. He hath, my lord, wrung from me my slow leave
 326:     By laboursome petition, and at last
 327:     Upon his will I seal'd my hard consent.
 328:     I do beseech you give him leave to go.
 329:   King. Take thy fair hour, Laertes. Time be thine,
 330:     And thy best graces spend it at thy will!
 331:     But now, my cousin Hamlet, and my son-
 332:   Ham. [aside] A little more than kin, and less than kind!
 333:   King. How is it that the clouds still hang on you?
 334:   Ham. Not so, my lord. I am too much i' th' sun.
 335:   Queen. Good Hamlet, cast thy nighted colour off,
 336:     And let thine eye look like a friend on Denmark.
 337:     Do not for ever with thy vailed lids
 338:     Seek for thy noble father in the dust.
 339:     Thou know'st 'tis common. All that lives must die,
 340:     Passing through nature to eternity.  
 341:   Ham. Ay, madam, it is common.
 342:   Queen. If it be,
 343:     Why seems it so particular with thee?
 344:   Ham. Seems, madam, Nay, it is. I know not 'seems.'
 345:     'Tis not alone my inky cloak, good mother,
 346:     Nor customary suits of solemn black,
 347:     Nor windy suspiration of forc'd breath,
 348:     No, nor the fruitful river in the eye,
 349:     Nor the dejected havior of the visage,
 350:     Together with all forms, moods, shapes of grief,
 351:     'That can denote me truly. These indeed seem,
 352:     For they are actions that a man might play;
 353:     But I have that within which passeth show-
 354:     These but the trappings and the suits of woe.
 355:   King. 'Tis sweet and commendable in your nature, Hamlet,
 356:     To give these mourning duties to your father;
 357:     But you must know, your father lost a father;
 358:     That father lost, lost his, and the survivor bound
 359:     In filial obligation for some term
 360:     To do obsequious sorrow. But to persever  
 361:     In obstinate condolement is a course
 362:     Of impious stubbornness. 'Tis unmanly grief;
 363:     It shows a will most incorrect to heaven,
 364:     A heart unfortified, a mind impatient,
 365:     An understanding simple and unschool'd;
 366:     For what we know must be, and is as common
 367:     As any the most vulgar thing to sense,
 368:     Why should we in our peevish opposition
 369:     Take it to heart? Fie! 'tis a fault to heaven,
 370:     A fault against the dead, a fault to nature,
 371:     To reason most absurd, whose common theme
 372:     Is death of fathers, and who still hath cried,
 373:     From the first corse till he that died to-day,
 374:     'This must be so.' We pray you throw to earth
 375:     This unprevailing woe, and think of us
 376:     As of a father; for let the world take note
 377:     You are the most immediate to our throne,
 378:     And with no less nobility of love
 379:     Than that which dearest father bears his son
 380:     Do I impart toward you. For your intent  
 381:     In going back to school in Wittenberg,
 382:     It is most retrograde to our desire;
 383:     And we beseech you, bend you to remain
 384:     Here in the cheer and comfort of our eye,
 385:     Our chiefest courtier, cousin, and our son.
 386:   Queen. Let not thy mother lose her prayers, Hamlet.
 387:     I pray thee stay with us, go not to Wittenberg.
 388:   Ham. I shall in all my best obey you, madam.
 389:   King. Why, 'tis a loving and a fair reply.
 390:     Be as ourself in Denmark. Madam, come.
 391:     This gentle and unforc'd accord of Hamlet
 392:     Sits smiling to my heart; in grace whereof,
 393:     No jocund health that Denmark drinks to-day
 394:     But the great cannon to the clouds shall tell,
 395:     And the King's rouse the heaven shall bruit again,
 396:     Respeaking earthly thunder. Come away.
 397:                                 Flourish. Exeunt all but Hamlet.
 398:   Ham. O that this too too solid flesh would melt,
 399:     Thaw, and resolve itself into a dew!
 400:     Or that the Everlasting had not fix'd  
 401:     His canon 'gainst self-slaughter! O God! God!
 402:     How weary, stale, flat, and unprofitable
 403:     Seem to me all the uses of this world!
 404:     Fie on't! ah, fie! 'Tis an unweeded garden
 405:     That grows to seed; things rank and gross in nature
 406:     Possess it merely. That it should come to this!
 407:     But two months dead! Nay, not so much, not two.
 408:     So excellent a king, that was to this
 409:     Hyperion to a satyr; so loving to my mother
 410:     That he might not beteem the winds of heaven
 411:     Visit her face too roughly. Heaven and earth!
 412:     Must I remember? Why, she would hang on him
 413:     As if increase of appetite had grown
 414:     By what it fed on; and yet, within a month-
 415:     Let me not think on't! Frailty, thy name is woman!-
 416:     A little month, or ere those shoes were old
 417:     With which she followed my poor father's body
 418:     Like Niobe, all tears- why she, even she
 419:     (O God! a beast that wants discourse of reason
 420:     Would have mourn'd longer) married with my uncle;  
 421:     My father's brother, but no more like my father
 422:     Than I to Hercules. Within a month,
 423:     Ere yet the salt of most unrighteous tears
 424:     Had left the flushing in her galled eyes,
 425:     She married. O, most wicked speed, to post
 426:     With such dexterity to incestuous sheets!
 427:     It is not, nor it cannot come to good.
 428:     But break my heart, for I must hold my tongue!
 429: 
 430:           Enter Horatio, Marcellus, and Bernardo.
 431: 
 432:   Hor. Hail to your lordship!
 433:   Ham. I am glad to see you well.
 434:     Horatio!- or I do forget myself.
 435:   Hor. The same, my lord, and your poor servant ever.
 436:   Ham. Sir, my good friend- I'll change that name with you.
 437:     And what make you from Wittenberg, Horatio?
 438:     Marcellus?
 439:   Mar. My good lord!
 440:   Ham. I am very glad to see you.- [To Bernardo] Good even, sir.-  
 441:     But what, in faith, make you from Wittenberg?
 442:   Hor. A truant disposition, good my lord.
 443:   Ham. I would not hear your enemy say so,
 444:     Nor shall you do my ear that violence
 445:     To make it truster of your own report
 446:     Against yourself. I know you are no truant.
 447:     But what is your affair in Elsinore?
 448:     We'll teach you to drink deep ere you depart.
 449:   Hor. My lord, I came to see your father's funeral.
 450:   Ham. I prithee do not mock me, fellow student.
 451:     I think it was to see my mother's wedding.
 452:   Hor. Indeed, my lord, it followed hard upon.
 453:   Ham. Thrift, thrift, Horatio! The funeral bak'd meats
 454:     Did coldly furnish forth the marriage tables.
 455:     Would I had met my dearest foe in heaven
 456:     Or ever I had seen that day, Horatio!
 457:     My father- methinks I see my father.
 458:   Hor. O, where, my lord?
 459:   Ham. In my mind's eye, Horatio.
 460:   Hor. I saw him once. He was a goodly king.  
 461:   Ham. He was a man, take him for all in all.
 462:     I shall not look upon his like again.
 463:   Hor. My lord, I think I saw him yesternight.
 464:   Ham. Saw? who?
 465:   Hor. My lord, the King your father.
 466:   Ham. The King my father?
 467:   Hor. Season your admiration for a while
 468:     With an attent ear, till I may deliver
 469:     Upon the witness of these gentlemen,
 470:     This marvel to you.
 471:   Ham. For God's love let me hear!
 472:   Hor. Two nights together had these gentlemen
 473:     (Marcellus and Bernardo) on their watch
 474:     In the dead vast and middle of the night
 475:     Been thus encount'red. A figure like your father,
 476:     Armed at point exactly, cap-a-pe,
 477:     Appears before them and with solemn march
 478:     Goes slow and stately by them. Thrice he walk'd
 479:     By their oppress'd and fear-surprised eyes,
 480:     Within his truncheon's length; whilst they distill'd  
 481:     Almost to jelly with the act of fear,
 482:     Stand dumb and speak not to him. This to me
 483:     In dreadful secrecy impart they did,
 484:     And I with them the third night kept the watch;
 485:     Where, as they had deliver'd, both in time,
 486:     Form of the thing, each word made true and good,
 487:     The apparition comes. I knew your father.
 488:     These hands are not more like.
 489:   Ham. But where was this?
 490:   Mar. My lord, upon the platform where we watch'd.
 491:   Ham. Did you not speak to it?
 492:   Hor. My lord, I did;
 493:     But answer made it none. Yet once methought
 494:     It lifted up it head and did address
 495:     Itself to motion, like as it would speak;
 496:     But even then the morning cock crew loud,
 497:     And at the sound it shrunk in haste away
 498:     And vanish'd from our sight.
 499:   Ham. 'Tis very strange.
 500:   Hor. As I do live, my honour'd lord, 'tis true;  
 501:     And we did think it writ down in our duty
 502:     To let you know of it.
 503:   Ham. Indeed, indeed, sirs. But this troubles me.
 504:     Hold you the watch to-night?
 505:   Both [Mar. and Ber.] We do, my lord.
 506:   Ham. Arm'd, say you?
 507:   Both. Arm'd, my lord.
 508:   Ham. From top to toe?
 509:   Both. My lord, from head to foot.
 510:   Ham. Then saw you not his face?
 511:   Hor. O, yes, my lord! He wore his beaver up.
 512:   Ham. What, look'd he frowningly.
 513:   Hor. A countenance more in sorrow than in anger.
 514:   Ham. Pale or red?
 515:   Hor. Nay, very pale.
 516:   Ham. And fix'd his eyes upon you?
 517:   Hor. Most constantly.
 518:   Ham. I would I had been there.
 519:   Hor. It would have much amaz'd you.
 520:   Ham. Very like, very like. Stay'd it long?  
 521:   Hor. While one with moderate haste might tell a hundred.
 522:   Both. Longer, longer.
 523:   Hor. Not when I saw't.
 524:   Ham. His beard was grizzled- no?
 525:   Hor. It was, as I have seen it in his life,
 526:     A sable silver'd.
 527:   Ham. I will watch to-night.
 528:     Perchance 'twill walk again.
 529:   Hor. I warr'nt it will.
 530:   Ham. If it assume my noble father's person,
 531:     I'll speak to it, though hell itself should gape
 532:     And bid me hold my peace. I pray you all,
 533:     If you have hitherto conceal'd this sight,
 534:     Let it be tenable in your silence still;
 535:     And whatsoever else shall hap to-night,
 536:     Give it an understanding but no tongue.
 537:     I will requite your loves. So, fare you well.
 538:     Upon the platform, 'twixt eleven and twelve,
 539:     I'll visit you.
 540:   All. Our duty to your honour.  
 541:   Ham. Your loves, as mine to you. Farewell.
 542:                                         Exeunt [all but Hamlet].
 543:     My father's spirit- in arms? All is not well.
 544:     I doubt some foul play. Would the night were come!
 545:     Till then sit still, my soul. Foul deeds will rise,
 546:     Though all the earth o'erwhelm them, to men's eyes.
 547: Exit.
 548: 
 549: 
 550: 
 551: 
 552: Scene III.
 553: Elsinore. A room in the house of Polonius.
 554: 
 555: Enter Laertes and Ophelia.
 556: 
 557:   Laer. My necessaries are embark'd. Farewell.
 558:     And, sister, as the winds give benefit
 559:     And convoy is assistant, do not sleep,
 560:     But let me hear from you.
 561:   Oph. Do you doubt that?
 562:   Laer. For Hamlet, and the trifling of his favour,
 563:     Hold it a fashion, and a toy in blood;
 564:     A violet in the youth of primy nature,
 565:     Forward, not permanent- sweet, not lasting;
 566:     The perfume and suppliance of a minute;
 567:     No more.
 568:   Oph. No more but so?
 569:   Laer. Think it no more.
 570:     For nature crescent does not grow alone
 571:     In thews and bulk; but as this temple waxes,
 572:     The inward service of the mind and soul
 573:     Grows wide withal. Perhaps he loves you now,  
 574:     And now no soil nor cautel doth besmirch
 575:     The virtue of his will; but you must fear,
 576:     His greatness weigh'd, his will is not his own;
 577:     For he himself is subject to his birth.
 578:     He may not, as unvalued persons do,
 579:     Carve for himself, for on his choice depends
 580:     The safety and health of this whole state,
 581:     And therefore must his choice be circumscrib'd
 582:     Unto the voice and yielding of that body
 583:     Whereof he is the head. Then if he says he loves you,
 584:     It fits your wisdom so far to believe it
 585:     As he in his particular act and place
 586:     May give his saying deed; which is no further
 587:     Than the main voice of Denmark goes withal.
 588:     Then weigh what loss your honour may sustain
 589:     If with too credent ear you list his songs,
 590:     Or lose your heart, or your chaste treasure open
 591:     To his unmast'red importunity.
 592:     Fear it, Ophelia, fear it, my dear sister,
 593:     And keep you in the rear of your affection,  
 594:     Out of the shot and danger of desire.
 595:     The chariest maid is prodigal enough
 596:     If she unmask her beauty to the moon.
 597:     Virtue itself scopes not calumnious strokes.
 598:     The canker galls the infants of the spring
 599:     Too oft before their buttons be disclos'd,
 600:     And in the morn and liquid dew of youth
 601:     Contagious blastments are most imminent.
 602:     Be wary then; best safety lies in fear.
 603:     Youth to itself rebels, though none else near.
 604:   Oph. I shall th' effect of this good lesson keep
 605:     As watchman to my heart. But, good my brother,
 606:     Do not as some ungracious pastors do,
 607:     Show me the steep and thorny way to heaven,
 608:     Whiles, like a puff'd and reckless libertine,
 609:     Himself the primrose path of dalliance treads
 610:     And recks not his own rede.
 611:   Laer. O, fear me not!
 612: 
 613:                        Enter Polonius.  
 614: 
 615:     I stay too long. But here my father comes.
 616:     A double blessing is a double grace;
 617:     Occasion smiles upon a second leave.
 618:   Pol. Yet here, Laertes? Aboard, aboard, for shame!
 619:     The wind sits in the shoulder of your sail,
 620:     And you are stay'd for. There- my blessing with thee!
 621:     And these few precepts in thy memory
 622:     Look thou character. Give thy thoughts no tongue,
 623:     Nor any unproportion'd thought his act.
 624:     Be thou familiar, but by no means vulgar:
 625:     Those friends thou hast, and their adoption tried,
 626:     Grapple them unto thy soul with hoops of steel;
 627:     But do not dull thy palm with entertainment
 628:     Of each new-hatch'd, unfledg'd comrade. Beware
 629:     Of entrance to a quarrel; but being in,
 630:     Bear't that th' opposed may beware of thee.
 631:     Give every man thine ear, but few thy voice;
 632:     Take each man's censure, but reserve thy judgment.
 633:     Costly thy habit as thy purse can buy,  
 634:     But not express'd in fancy; rich, not gaudy;
 635:     For the apparel oft proclaims the man,
 636:     And they in France of the best rank and station
 637:     Are most select and generous, chief in that.
 638:     Neither a borrower nor a lender be;
 639:     For loan oft loses both itself and friend,
 640:     And borrowing dulls the edge of husbandry.
 641:     This above all- to thine own self be true,
 642:     And it must follow, as the night the day,
 643:     Thou canst not then be false to any man.
 644:     Farewell. My blessing season this in thee!
 645:   Laer. Most humbly do I take my leave, my lord.
 646:   Pol. The time invites you. Go, your servants tend.
 647:   Laer. Farewell, Ophelia, and remember well
 648:     What I have said to you.
 649:   Oph. 'Tis in my memory lock'd,
 650:     And you yourself shall keep the key of it.
 651:   Laer. Farewell.                                          Exit.
 652:   Pol. What is't, Ophelia, he hath said to you?
 653:   Oph. So please you, something touching the Lord Hamlet.  
 654:   Pol. Marry, well bethought!
 655:     'Tis told me he hath very oft of late
 656:     Given private time to you, and you yourself
 657:     Have of your audience been most free and bounteous.
 658:     If it be so- as so 'tis put on me,
 659:     And that in way of caution- I must tell you
 660:     You do not understand yourself so clearly
 661:     As it behooves my daughter and your honour.
 662:     What is between you? Give me up the truth.
 663:   Oph. He hath, my lord, of late made many tenders
 664:     Of his affection to me.
 665:   Pol. Affection? Pooh! You speak like a green girl,
 666:     Unsifted in such perilous circumstance.
 667:     Do you believe his tenders, as you call them?
 668:   Oph. I do not know, my lord, what I should think,
 669:   Pol. Marry, I will teach you! Think yourself a baby
 670:     That you have ta'en these tenders for true pay,
 671:     Which are not sterling. Tender yourself more dearly,
 672:     Or (not to crack the wind of the poor phrase,
 673:     Running it thus) you'll tender me a fool.  
 674:   Oph. My lord, he hath importun'd me with love
 675:     In honourable fashion.
 676:   Pol. Ay, fashion you may call it. Go to, go to!
 677:   Oph. And hath given countenance to his speech, my lord,
 678:     With almost all the holy vows of heaven.
 679:   Pol. Ay, springes to catch woodcocks! I do know,
 680:     When the blood burns, how prodigal the soul
 681:     Lends the tongue vows. These blazes, daughter,
 682:     Giving more light than heat, extinct in both
 683:     Even in their promise, as it is a-making,
 684:     You must not take for fire. From this time
 685:     Be something scanter of your maiden presence.
 686:     Set your entreatments at a higher rate
 687:     Than a command to parley. For Lord Hamlet,
 688:     Believe so much in him, that he is young,
 689:     And with a larger tether may he walk
 690:     Than may be given you. In few, Ophelia,
 691:     Do not believe his vows; for they are brokers,
 692:     Not of that dye which their investments show,
 693:     But mere implorators of unholy suits,  
 694:     Breathing like sanctified and pious bawds,
 695:     The better to beguile. This is for all:
 696:     I would not, in plain terms, from this time forth
 697:     Have you so slander any moment leisure
 698:     As to give words or talk with the Lord Hamlet.
 699:     Look to't, I charge you. Come your ways.
 700:   Oph. I shall obey, my lord.
 701:                                                          Exeunt.
 702: 
 703: 
 704: 
 705: 
 706: Scene IV.
 707: Elsinore. The platform before the Castle.
 708: 
 709: Enter Hamlet, Horatio, and Marcellus.
 710: 
 711:   Ham. The air bites shrewdly; it is very cold.
 712:   Hor. It is a nipping and an eager air.
 713:   Ham. What hour now?
 714:   Hor. I think it lacks of twelve.
 715:   Mar. No, it is struck.
 716:   Hor. Indeed? I heard it not. It then draws near the season
 717:     Wherein the spirit held his wont to walk.
 718:                    A flourish of trumpets, and two pieces go off.
 719:     What does this mean, my lord?
 720:   Ham. The King doth wake to-night and takes his rouse,
 721:     Keeps wassail, and the swagg'ring upspring reels,
 722:     And, as he drains his draughts of Rhenish down,
 723:     The kettledrum and trumpet thus bray out
 724:     The triumph of his pledge.
 725:   Hor. Is it a custom?
 726:   Ham. Ay, marry, is't;
 727:     But to my mind, though I am native here  
 728:     And to the manner born, it is a custom
 729:     More honour'd in the breach than the observance.
 730:     This heavy-headed revel east and west
 731:     Makes us traduc'd and tax'd of other nations;
 732:     They clip us drunkards and with swinish phrase
 733:     Soil our addition; and indeed it takes
 734:     From our achievements, though perform'd at height,
 735:     The pith and marrow of our attribute.
 736:     So oft it chances in particular men
 737:     That, for some vicious mole of nature in them,
 738:     As in their birth,- wherein they are not guilty,
 739:     Since nature cannot choose his origin,-
 740:     By the o'ergrowth of some complexion,
 741:     Oft breaking down the pales and forts of reason,
 742:     Or by some habit that too much o'erleavens
 743:     The form of plausive manners, that these men
 744:     Carrying, I say, the stamp of one defect,
 745:     Being nature's livery, or fortune's star,
 746:     Their virtues else- be they as pure as grace,
 747:     As infinite as man may undergo-  
 748:     Shall in the general censure take corruption
 749:     From that particular fault. The dram of e'il
 750:     Doth all the noble substance often dout To his own scandal.
 751: 
 752:                          Enter Ghost.
 753: 
 754:   Hor. Look, my lord, it comes!
 755:   Ham. Angels and ministers of grace defend us!
 756:     Be thou a spirit of health or goblin damn'd,
 757:     Bring with thee airs from heaven or blasts from hell,
 758:     Be thy intents wicked or charitable,
 759:     Thou com'st in such a questionable shape
 760:     That I will speak to thee. I'll call thee Hamlet,
 761:     King, father, royal Dane. O, answer me?
 762:     Let me not burst in ignorance, but tell
 763:     Why thy canoniz'd bones, hearsed in death,
 764:     Have burst their cerements; why the sepulchre
 765:     Wherein we saw thee quietly inurn'd,
 766:     Hath op'd his ponderous and marble jaws
 767:     To cast thee up again. What may this mean  
 768:     That thou, dead corse, again in complete steel,
 769:     Revisits thus the glimpses of the moon,
 770:     Making night hideous, and we fools of nature
 771:     So horridly to shake our disposition
 772:     With thoughts beyond the reaches of our souls?
 773:     Say, why is this? wherefore? What should we do?
 774:                                            Ghost beckons Hamlet.
 775:   Hor. It beckons you to go away with it,
 776:     As if it some impartment did desire
 777:     To you alone.
 778:   Mar. Look with what courteous action
 779:     It waves you to a more removed ground.
 780:     But do not go with it!
 781:   Hor. No, by no means!
 782:   Ham. It will not speak. Then will I follow it.
 783:   Hor. Do not, my lord!
 784:   Ham. Why, what should be the fear?
 785:     I do not set my life at a pin's fee;
 786:     And for my soul, what can it do to that,
 787:     Being a thing immortal as itself?  
 788:     It waves me forth again. I'll follow it.
 789:   Hor. What if it tempt you toward the flood, my lord,
 790:     Or to the dreadful summit of the cliff
 791:     That beetles o'er his base into the sea,
 792:     And there assume some other, horrible form
 793:     Which might deprive your sovereignty of reason
 794:     And draw you into madness? Think of it.
 795:     The very place puts toys of desperation,
 796:     Without more motive, into every brain
 797:     That looks so many fadoms to the sea
 798:     And hears it roar beneath.
 799:   Ham. It waves me still.
 800:     Go on. I'll follow thee.
 801:   Mar. You shall not go, my lord.
 802:   Ham. Hold off your hands!
 803:   Hor. Be rul'd. You shall not go.
 804:   Ham. My fate cries out
 805:     And makes each petty artire in this body
 806:     As hardy as the Nemean lion's nerve.
 807:                                                 [Ghost beckons.]  
 808:     Still am I call'd. Unhand me, gentlemen.
 809:     By heaven, I'll make a ghost of him that lets me!-
 810:     I say, away!- Go on. I'll follow thee.
 811:                                         Exeunt Ghost and Hamlet.
 812:   Hor. He waxes desperate with imagination.
 813:   Mar. Let's follow. 'Tis not fit thus to obey him.
 814:   Hor. Have after. To what issue wail this come?
 815:   Mar. Something is rotten in the state of Denmark.
 816:   Hor. Heaven will direct it.
 817:   Mar. Nay, let's follow him.
 818:                                                          Exeunt.
 819: 
 820: 
 821: 
 822: 
 823: Scene V.
 824: Elsinore. The Castle. Another part of the fortifications.
 825: 
 826: Enter Ghost and Hamlet.
 827: 
 828:   Ham. Whither wilt thou lead me? Speak! I'll go no further.
 829:   Ghost. Mark me.
 830:   Ham. I will.
 831:   Ghost. My hour is almost come,
 832:     When I to sulph'rous and tormenting flames
 833:     Must render up myself.
 834:   Ham. Alas, poor ghost!
 835:   Ghost. Pity me not, but lend thy serious hearing
 836:     To what I shall unfold.
 837:   Ham. Speak. I am bound to hear.
 838:   Ghost. So art thou to revenge, when thou shalt hear.
 839:   Ham. What?
 840:   Ghost. I am thy father's spirit,
 841:     Doom'd for a certain term to walk the night,
 842:     And for the day confin'd to fast in fires,
 843:     Till the foul crimes done in my days of nature
 844:     Are burnt and purg'd away. But that I am forbid  
 845:     To tell the secrets of my prison house,
 846:     I could a tale unfold whose lightest word
 847:     Would harrow up thy soul, freeze thy young blood,
 848:     Make thy two eyes, like stars, start from their spheres,
 849:     Thy knotted and combined locks to part,
 850:     And each particular hair to stand an end
 851:     Like quills upon the fretful porpentine.
 852:     But this eternal blazon must not be
 853:     To ears of flesh and blood. List, list, O, list!
 854:     If thou didst ever thy dear father love-
 855:   Ham. O God!
 856:   Ghost. Revenge his foul and most unnatural murther.
 857:   Ham. Murther?
 858:   Ghost. Murther most foul, as in the best it is;
 859:     But this most foul, strange, and unnatural.
 860:   Ham. Haste me to know't, that I, with wings as swift
 861:     As meditation or the thoughts of love,
 862:     May sweep to my revenge.
 863:   Ghost. I find thee apt;
 864:     And duller shouldst thou be than the fat weed  
 865:     That rots itself in ease on Lethe wharf,
 866:     Wouldst thou not stir in this. Now, Hamlet, hear.
 867:     'Tis given out that, sleeping in my orchard,
 868:     A serpent stung me. So the whole ear of Denmark
 869:     Is by a forged process of my death
 870:     Rankly abus'd. But know, thou noble youth,
 871:     The serpent that did sting thy father's life
 872:     Now wears his crown.
 873:   Ham. O my prophetic soul!
 874:     My uncle?
 875:   Ghost. Ay, that incestuous, that adulterate beast,
 876:     With witchcraft of his wit, with traitorous gifts-
 877:     O wicked wit and gifts, that have the power
 878:     So to seduce!- won to his shameful lust
 879:     The will of my most seeming-virtuous queen.
 880:     O Hamlet, what a falling-off was there,
 881:     From me, whose love was of that dignity
 882:     That it went hand in hand even with the vow
 883:     I made to her in marriage, and to decline
 884:     Upon a wretch whose natural gifts were poor  
 885:     To those of mine!
 886:     But virtue, as it never will be mov'd,
 887:     Though lewdness court it in a shape of heaven,
 888:     So lust, though to a radiant angel link'd,
 889:     Will sate itself in a celestial bed
 890:     And prey on garbage.
 891:     But soft! methinks I scent the morning air.
 892:     Brief let me be. Sleeping within my orchard,
 893:     My custom always of the afternoon,
 894:     Upon my secure hour thy uncle stole,
 895:     With juice of cursed hebona in a vial,
 896:     And in the porches of my ears did pour
 897:     The leperous distilment; whose effect
 898:     Holds such an enmity with blood of man
 899:     That swift as quicksilverr it courses through
 900:     The natural gates and alleys of the body,
 901:     And with a sudden vigour it doth posset
 902:     And curd, like eager droppings into milk,
 903:     The thin and wholesome blood. So did it mine;
 904:     And a most instant tetter bark'd about,  
 905:     Most lazar-like, with vile and loathsome crust
 906:     All my smooth body.
 907:     Thus was I, sleeping, by a brother's hand
 908:     Of life, of crown, of queen, at once dispatch'd;
 909:     Cut off even in the blossoms of my sin,
 910:     Unhous'led, disappointed, unanel'd,
 911:     No reckoning made, but sent to my account
 912:     With all my imperfections on my head.
 913:   Ham. O, horrible! O, horrible! most horrible!
 914:   Ghost. If thou hast nature in thee, bear it not.
 915:     Let not the royal bed of Denmark be
 916:     A couch for luxury and damned incest.
 917:     But, howsoever thou pursuest this act,
 918:     Taint not thy mind, nor let thy soul contrive
 919:     Against thy mother aught. Leave her to heaven,
 920:     And to those thorns that in her bosom lodge
 921:     To prick and sting her. Fare thee well at once.
 922:     The glowworm shows the matin to be near
 923:     And gins to pale his uneffectual fire.
 924:     Adieu, adieu, adieu! Remember me.                      Exit.  
 925:   Ham. O all you host of heaven! O earth! What else?
 926:     And shall I couple hell? Hold, hold, my heart!
 927:     And you, my sinews, grow not instant old,
 928:     But bear me stiffly up. Remember thee?
 929:     Ay, thou poor ghost, while memory holds a seat
 930:     In this distracted globe. Remember thee?
 931:     Yea, from the table of my memory
 932:     I'll wipe away all trivial fond records,
 933:     All saws of books, all forms, all pressures past
 934:     That youth and observation copied there,
 935:     And thy commandment all alone shall live
 936:     Within the book and volume of my brain,
 937:     Unmix'd with baser matter. Yes, by heaven!
 938:     O most pernicious woman!
 939:     O villain, villain, smiling, damned villain!
 940:     My tables! Meet it is I set it down
 941:     That one may smile, and smile, and be a villain;
 942:     At least I am sure it may be so in Denmark.        [Writes.]
 943:     So, uncle, there you are. Now to my word:
 944:     It is 'Adieu, adieu! Remember me.'  
 945:     I have sworn't.
 946:   Hor. (within) My lord, my lord!
 947: 
 948:                    Enter Horatio and Marcellus.
 949: 
 950:   Mar. Lord Hamlet!
 951:   Hor. Heaven secure him!
 952:   Ham. So be it!
 953:   Mar. Illo, ho, ho, my lord!
 954:   Ham. Hillo, ho, ho, boy! Come, bird, come.
 955:   Mar. How is't, my noble lord?
 956:   Hor. What news, my lord?
 957:   Mar. O, wonderful!
 958:   Hor. Good my lord, tell it.
 959:   Ham. No, you will reveal it.
 960:   Hor. Not I, my lord, by heaven!
 961:   Mar. Nor I, my lord.
 962:   Ham. How say you then? Would heart of man once think it?
 963:     But you'll be secret?
 964:   Both. Ay, by heaven, my lord.  
 965:   Ham. There's neer a villain dwelling in all Denmark
 966:     But he's an arrant knave.
 967:   Hor. There needs no ghost, my lord, come from the grave
 968:     To tell us this.
 969:   Ham. Why, right! You are in the right!
 970:     And so, without more circumstance at all,
 971:     I hold it fit that we shake hands and part;
 972:     You, as your business and desires shall point you,
 973:     For every man hath business and desire,
 974:     Such as it is; and for my own poor part,
 975:     Look you, I'll go pray.
 976:   Hor. These are but wild and whirling words, my lord.
 977:   Ham. I am sorry they offend you, heartily;
 978:     Yes, faith, heartily.
 979:   Hor. There's no offence, my lord.
 980:   Ham. Yes, by Saint Patrick, but there is, Horatio,
 981:     And much offence too. Touching this vision here,
 982:     It is an honest ghost, that let me tell you.
 983:     For your desire to know what is between us,
 984:     O'ermaster't as you may. And now, good friends,  
 985:     As you are friends, scholars, and soldiers,
 986:     Give me one poor request.
 987:   Hor. What is't, my lord? We will.
 988:   Ham. Never make known what you have seen to-night.
 989:   Both. My lord, we will not.
 990:   Ham. Nay, but swear't.
 991:   Hor. In faith,
 992:     My lord, not I.
 993:   Mar. Nor I, my lord- in faith.
 994:   Ham. Upon my sword.
 995:   Mar. We have sworn, my lord, already.
 996:   Ham. Indeed, upon my sword, indeed.
 997: 
 998:                  Ghost cries under the stage.
 999: 
1000:   Ghost. Swear.
1001:   Ham. Aha boy, say'st thou so? Art thou there, truepenny?
1002:     Come on! You hear this fellow in the cellarage.
1003:     Consent to swear.
1004:   Hor. Propose the oath, my lord.  
1005:   Ham. Never to speak of this that you have seen.
1006:     Swear by my sword.
1007:   Ghost. [beneath] Swear.
1008:   Ham. Hic et ubique? Then we'll shift our ground.
1009:     Come hither, gentlemen,
1010:     And lay your hands again upon my sword.
1011:     Never to speak of this that you have heard:
1012:     Swear by my sword.
1013:   Ghost. [beneath] Swear by his sword.
1014:   Ham. Well said, old mole! Canst work i' th' earth so fast?
1015:     A worthy pioner! Once more remove, good friends."
1016:   Hor. O day and night, but this is wondrous strange!
1017:   Ham. And therefore as a stranger give it welcome.
1018:     There are more things in heaven and earth, Horatio,
1019:     Than are dreamt of in your philosophy.
1020:     But come!
1021:     Here, as before, never, so help you mercy,
1022:     How strange or odd soe'er I bear myself
1023:     (As I perchance hereafter shall think meet
1024:     To put an antic disposition on),  
1025:     That you, at such times seeing me, never shall,
1026:     With arms encumb'red thus, or this head-shake,
1027:     Or by pronouncing of some doubtful phrase,
1028:     As 'Well, well, we know,' or 'We could, an if we would,'
1029:     Or 'If we list to speak,' or 'There be, an if they might,'
1030:     Or such ambiguous giving out, to note
1031:     That you know aught of me- this is not to do,
1032:     So grace and mercy at your most need help you,
1033:     Swear.
1034:   Ghost. [beneath] Swear.
1035:                                                    [They swear.]
1036:   Ham. Rest, rest, perturbed spirit! So, gentlemen,
1037:     With all my love I do commend me to you;
1038:     And what so poor a man as Hamlet is
1039:     May do t' express his love and friending to you,
1040:     God willing, shall not lack. Let us go in together;
1041:     And still your fingers on your lips, I pray.
1042:     The time is out of joint. O cursed spite
1043:     That ever I was born to set it right!
1044:     Nay, come, let's go together.  
1045:                                                          Exeunt.
1046: 
1047: 
1048: 
1049: 
1050: Act II. Scene I.
1051: Elsinore. A room in the house of Polonius.
1052: 
1053: Enter Polonius and Reynaldo.
1054: 
1055:   Pol. Give him this money and these notes, Reynaldo.
1056:   Rey. I will, my lord.
1057:   Pol. You shall do marvell's wisely, good Reynaldo,
1058:     Before You visit him, to make inquire
1059:     Of his behaviour.
1060:   Rey. My lord, I did intend it.
1061:   Pol. Marry, well said, very well said. Look you, sir,
1062:     Enquire me first what Danskers are in Paris;
1063:     And how, and who, what means, and where they keep,
1064:     What company, at what expense; and finding
1065:     By this encompassment and drift of question
1066:     That they do know my son, come you more nearer
1067:     Than your particular demands will touch it.
1068:     Take you, as 'twere, some distant knowledge of him;
1069:     As thus, 'I know his father and his friends,
1070:     And in part him.' Do you mark this, Reynaldo?
1071:   Rey. Ay, very well, my lord.  
1072:   Pol. 'And in part him, but,' you may say, 'not well.
1073:     But if't be he I mean, he's very wild
1074:     Addicted so and so'; and there put on him
1075:     What forgeries you please; marry, none so rank
1076:     As may dishonour him- take heed of that;
1077:     But, sir, such wanton, wild, and usual slips
1078:     As are companions noted and most known
1079:     To youth and liberty.
1080:   Rey. As gaming, my lord.
1081:   Pol. Ay, or drinking, fencing, swearing, quarrelling,
1082:     Drabbing. You may go so far.
1083:   Rey. My lord, that would dishonour him.
1084:   Pol. Faith, no, as you may season it in the charge.
1085:     You must not put another scandal on him,
1086:     That he is open to incontinency.
1087:     That's not my meaning. But breathe his faults so quaintly
1088:     That they may seem the taints of liberty,
1089:     The flash and outbreak of a fiery mind,
1090:     A savageness in unreclaimed blood,
1091:     Of general assault.  
1092:   Rey. But, my good lord-
1093:   Pol. Wherefore should you do this?
1094:   Rey. Ay, my lord,
1095:     I would know that.
1096:   Pol. Marry, sir, here's my drift,
1097:     And I believe it is a fetch of warrant.
1098:     You laying these slight sullies on my son
1099:     As 'twere a thing a little soil'd i' th' working,
1100:     Mark you,
1101:     Your party in converse, him you would sound,
1102:     Having ever seen in the prenominate crimes
1103:     The youth you breathe of guilty, be assur'd
1104:     He closes with you in this consequence:
1105:     'Good sir,' or so, or 'friend,' or 'gentleman'-
1106:     According to the phrase or the addition
1107:     Of man and country-
1108:   Rey. Very good, my lord.
1109:   Pol. And then, sir, does 'a this- 'a does- What was I about to say?
1110:     By the mass, I was about to say something! Where did I leave?
1111:   Rey. At 'closes in the consequence,' at 'friend or so,' and  
1112:     gentleman.'
1113:   Pol. At 'closes in the consequence'- Ay, marry!
1114:     He closes thus: 'I know the gentleman.
1115:     I saw him yesterday, or t'other day,
1116:     Or then, or then, with such or such; and, as you say,
1117:     There was 'a gaming; there o'ertook in's rouse;
1118:     There falling out at tennis'; or perchance,
1119:     'I saw him enter such a house of sale,'
1120:     Videlicet, a brothel, or so forth.
1121:     See you now-
1122:     Your bait of falsehood takes this carp of truth;
1123:     And thus do we of wisdom and of reach,
1124:     With windlasses and with assays of bias,
1125:     By indirections find directions out.
1126:     So, by my former lecture and advice,
1127:     Shall you my son. You have me, have you not
1128:   Rey. My lord, I have.
1129:   Pol. God b' wi' ye, fare ye well!
1130:   Rey. Good my lord!                                    [Going.]
1131:   Pol. Observe his inclination in yourself.  
1132:   Rey. I shall, my lord.
1133:   Pol. And let him ply his music.
1134:   Rey. Well, my lord.
1135:   Pol. Farewell!
1136:                                                   Exit Reynaldo.
1137: 
1138:                        Enter Ophelia.
1139: 
1140:     How now, Ophelia? What's the matter?
1141:   Oph. O my lord, my lord, I have been so affrighted!
1142:   Pol. With what, i' th' name of God I
1143:   Oph. My lord, as I was sewing in my closet,
1144:     Lord Hamlet, with his doublet all unbrac'd,
1145:     No hat upon his head, his stockings foul'd,
1146:     Ungart'red, and down-gyved to his ankle;
1147:     Pale as his shirt, his knees knocking each other,
1148:     And with a look so piteous in purport
1149:     As if he had been loosed out of hell
1150:     To speak of horrors- he comes before me.
1151:   Pol. Mad for thy love?  
1152:   Oph. My lord, I do not know,
1153:     But truly I do fear it.
1154:   Pol. What said he?
1155:   Oph. He took me by the wrist and held me hard;
1156:     Then goes he to the length of all his arm,
1157:     And, with his other hand thus o'er his brow,
1158:     He falls to such perusal of my face
1159:     As he would draw it. Long stay'd he so.
1160:     At last, a little shaking of mine arm,
1161:     And thrice his head thus waving up and down,
1162:     He rais'd a sigh so piteous and profound
1163:     As it did seem to shatter all his bulk
1164:     And end his being. That done, he lets me go,
1165:     And with his head over his shoulder turn'd
1166:     He seem'd to find his way without his eyes,
1167:     For out o' doors he went without their help
1168:     And to the last bended their light on me.
1169:   Pol. Come, go with me. I will go seek the King.
1170:     This is the very ecstasy of love,
1171:     Whose violent property fordoes itself  
1172:     And leads the will to desperate undertakings
1173:     As oft as any passion under heaven
1174:     That does afflict our natures. I am sorry.
1175:     What, have you given him any hard words of late?
1176:   Oph. No, my good lord; but, as you did command,
1177:     I did repel his letters and denied
1178:     His access to me.
1179:   Pol. That hath made him mad.
1180:     I am sorry that with better heed and judgment
1181:     I had not quoted him. I fear'd he did but trifle
1182:     And meant to wrack thee; but beshrew my jealousy!
1183:     By heaven, it is as proper to our age
1184:     To cast beyond ourselves in our opinions
1185:     As it is common for the younger sort
1186:     To lack discretion. Come, go we to the King.
1187:     This must be known; which, being kept close, might move
1188:     More grief to hide than hate to utter love.
1189:     Come.
1190:                                                          Exeunt.
1191: 
1192: Scene II.
1193: Elsinore. A room in the Castle.
1194: 
1195: Flourish. [Enter King and Queen, Rosencrantz and Guildenstern, cum aliis.
1196: 
1197:   King. Welcome, dear Rosencrantz and Guildenstern.
1198:     Moreover that we much did long to see you,
1199:     The need we have to use you did provoke
1200:     Our hasty sending. Something have you heard
1201:     Of Hamlet's transformation. So I call it,
1202:     Sith nor th' exterior nor the inward man
1203:     Resembles that it was. What it should be,
1204:     More than his father's death, that thus hath put him
1205:     So much from th' understanding of himself,
1206:     I cannot dream of. I entreat you both
1207:     That, being of so young clays brought up with him,
1208:     And since so neighbour'd to his youth and haviour,
1209:     That you vouchsafe your rest here in our court
1210:     Some little time; so by your companies
1211:     To draw him on to pleasures, and to gather
1212:     So much as from occasion you may glean,  
1213:     Whether aught to us unknown afflicts him thus
1214:     That, open'd, lies within our remedy.
1215:   Queen. Good gentlemen, he hath much talk'd of you,
1216:     And sure I am two men there are not living
1217:     To whom he more adheres. If it will please you
1218:     To show us so much gentry and good will
1219:     As to expend your time with us awhile
1220:     For the supply and profit of our hope,
1221:     Your visitation shall receive such thanks
1222:     As fits a king's remembrance.
1223:   Ros. Both your Majesties
1224:     Might, by the sovereign power you have of us,
1225:     Put your dread pleasures more into command
1226:     Than to entreaty.
1227:   Guil. But we both obey,
1228:     And here give up ourselves, in the full bent,
1229:     To lay our service freely at your feet,
1230:     To be commanded.
1231:   King. Thanks, Rosencrantz and gentle Guildenstern.
1232:   Queen. Thanks, Guildenstern and gentle Rosencrantz.  
1233:     And I beseech you instantly to visit
1234:     My too much changed son.- Go, some of you,
1235:     And bring these gentlemen where Hamlet is.
1236:   Guil. Heavens make our presence and our practices
1237:     Pleasant and helpful to him!
1238:   Queen. Ay, amen!
1239:                  Exeunt Rosencrantz and Guildenstern, [with some
1240:                                                     Attendants].
1241: 
1242:                          Enter Polonius.
1243: 
1244:   Pol. Th' ambassadors from Norway, my good lord,
1245:     Are joyfully return'd.
1246:   King. Thou still hast been the father of good news.
1247:   Pol. Have I, my lord? Assure you, my good liege,
1248:     I hold my duty as I hold my soul,
1249:     Both to my God and to my gracious king;
1250:     And I do think- or else this brain of mine
1251:     Hunts not the trail of policy so sure
1252:     As it hath us'd to do- that I have found  
1253:     The very cause of Hamlet's lunacy.
1254:   King. O, speak of that! That do I long to hear.
1255:   Pol. Give first admittance to th' ambassadors.
1256:     My news shall be the fruit to that great feast.
1257:   King. Thyself do grace to them, and bring them in.
1258:                                                 [Exit Polonius.]
1259:     He tells me, my dear Gertrude, he hath found
1260:     The head and source of all your son's distemper.
1261:   Queen. I doubt it is no other but the main,
1262:     His father's death and our o'erhasty marriage.
1263:   King. Well, we shall sift him.
1264: 
1265:               Enter Polonius, Voltemand, and Cornelius.
1266: 
1267:     Welcome, my good friends.
1268:     Say, Voltemand, what from our brother Norway?
1269:   Volt. Most fair return of greetings and desires.
1270:     Upon our first, he sent out to suppress
1271:     His nephew's levies; which to him appear'd
1272:     To be a preparation 'gainst the Polack,  
1273:     But better look'd into, he truly found
1274:     It was against your Highness; whereat griev'd,
1275:     That so his sickness, age, and impotence
1276:     Was falsely borne in hand, sends out arrests
1277:     On Fortinbras; which he, in brief, obeys,
1278:     Receives rebuke from Norway, and, in fine,
1279:     Makes vow before his uncle never more
1280:     To give th' assay of arms against your Majesty.
1281:     Whereon old Norway, overcome with joy,
1282:     Gives him three thousand crowns in annual fee
1283:     And his commission to employ those soldiers,
1284:     So levied as before, against the Polack;
1285:     With an entreaty, herein further shown,
1286:                                                 [Gives a paper.]
1287:     That it might please you to give quiet pass
1288:     Through your dominions for this enterprise,
1289:     On such regards of safety and allowance
1290:     As therein are set down.
1291:   King. It likes us well;
1292:     And at our more consider'd time we'll read,  
1293:     Answer, and think upon this business.
1294:     Meantime we thank you for your well-took labour.
1295:     Go to your rest; at night we'll feast together.
1296:     Most welcome home!                       Exeunt Ambassadors.
1297:   Pol. This business is well ended.
1298:     My liege, and madam, to expostulate
1299:     What majesty should be, what duty is,
1300:     Why day is day, night is night, and time is time.
1301:     Were nothing but to waste night, day, and time.
1302:     Therefore, since brevity is the soul of wit,
1303:     And tediousness the limbs and outward flourishes,
1304:     I will be brief. Your noble son is mad.
1305:     Mad call I it; for, to define true madness,
1306:     What is't but to be nothing else but mad?
1307:     But let that go.
1308:   Queen. More matter, with less art.
1309:   Pol. Madam, I swear I use no art at all.
1310:     That he is mad, 'tis true: 'tis true 'tis pity;
1311:     And pity 'tis 'tis true. A foolish figure!
1312:     But farewell it, for I will use no art.  
1313:     Mad let us grant him then. And now remains
1314:     That we find out the cause of this effect-
1315:     Or rather say, the cause of this defect,
1316:     For this effect defective comes by cause.
1317:     Thus it remains, and the remainder thus.
1318:     Perpend.
1319:     I have a daughter (have while she is mine),
1320:     Who in her duty and obedience, mark,
1321:     Hath given me this. Now gather, and surmise.
1322:                                              [Reads] the letter.
1323:     'To the celestial, and my soul's idol, the most beautified
1324:       Ophelia,'-
1325: 
1326:     That's an ill phrase, a vile phrase; 'beautified' is a vile
1327:       phrase.
1328:     But you shall hear. Thus:
1329:                                                         [Reads.]
1330:     'In her excellent white bosom, these, &c.'
1331:   Queen. Came this from Hamlet to her?
1332:   Pol. Good madam, stay awhile. I will be faithful.     [Reads.]  
1333: 
1334:           'Doubt thou the stars are fire;
1335:             Doubt that the sun doth move;
1336:           Doubt truth to be a liar;
1337:             But never doubt I love.
1338:       'O dear Ophelia, I am ill at these numbers; I have not art to
1339:     reckon my groans; but that I love thee best, O most best, believe
1340:     it. Adieu.
1341:       'Thine evermore, most dear lady, whilst this machine is to him,
1342:                                                           HAMLET.'
1343: 
1344:     This, in obedience, hath my daughter shown me;
1345:     And more above, hath his solicitings,
1346:     As they fell out by time, by means, and place,
1347:     All given to mine ear.
1348:   King. But how hath she
1349:     Receiv'd his love?
1350:   Pol. What do you think of me?
1351:   King. As of a man faithful and honourable.
1352:   Pol. I would fain prove so. But what might you think,  
1353:     When I had seen this hot love on the wing
1354:     (As I perceiv'd it, I must tell you that,
1355:     Before my daughter told me), what might you,
1356:     Or my dear Majesty your queen here, think,
1357:     If I had play'd the desk or table book,
1358:     Or given my heart a winking, mute and dumb,
1359:     Or look'd upon this love with idle sight?
1360:     What might you think? No, I went round to work
1361:     And my young mistress thus I did bespeak:
1362:     'Lord Hamlet is a prince, out of thy star.
1363:     This must not be.' And then I prescripts gave her,
1364:     That she should lock herself from his resort,
1365:     Admit no messengers, receive no tokens.
1366:     Which done, she took the fruits of my advice,
1367:     And he, repulsed, a short tale to make,
1368:     Fell into a sadness, then into a fast,
1369:     Thence to a watch, thence into a weakness,
1370:     Thence to a lightness, and, by this declension,
1371:     Into the madness wherein now he raves,
1372:     And all we mourn for.  
1373:   King. Do you think 'tis this?
1374:   Queen. it may be, very like.
1375:   Pol. Hath there been such a time- I would fain know that-
1376:     That I have Positively said ''Tis so,'
1377:     When it prov'd otherwise.?
1378:   King. Not that I know.
1379:   Pol. [points to his head and shoulder] Take this from this, if this
1380:       be otherwise.
1381:     If circumstances lead me, I will find
1382:     Where truth is hid, though it were hid indeed
1383:     Within the centre.
1384:   King. How may we try it further?
1385:   Pol. You know sometimes he walks four hours together
1386:     Here in the lobby.
1387:   Queen. So he does indeed.
1388:   Pol. At such a time I'll loose my daughter to him.
1389:     Be you and I behind an arras then.
1390:     Mark the encounter. If he love her not,
1391:     And he not from his reason fall'n thereon
1392:     Let me be no assistant for a state,  
1393:     But keep a farm and carters.
1394:   King. We will try it.

1396:                  Enter Hamlet, reading on a book.
1397: 
1398:   Queen. But look where sadly the poor wretch comes reading.
1399:   Pol. Away, I do beseech you, both away
1400:     I'll board him presently. O, give me leave.
1401:                        Exeunt King and Queen, [with Attendants].
1402:     How does my good Lord Hamlet?
1403:   Ham. Well, God-a-mercy.
1404:   Pol. Do you know me, my lord?
1405:   Ham. Excellent well. You are a fishmonger.
1406:   Pol. Not I, my lord.
1407:   Ham. Then I would you were so honest a man.
1408:   Pol. Honest, my lord?
1409:   Ham. Ay, sir. To be honest, as this world goes, is to be one man
1410:     pick'd out of ten thousand.
1411:   Pol. That's very true, my lord.
1412:   Ham. For if the sun breed maggots in a dead dog, being a god  
1413:     kissing carrion- Have you a daughter?
1414:   Pol. I have, my lord.
1415:   Ham. Let her not walk i' th' sun. Conception is a blessing, but not
1416:     as your daughter may conceive. Friend, look to't.
1417:   Pol. [aside] How say you by that? Still harping on my daughter. Yet
1418:     he knew me not at first. He said I was a fishmonger. He is far
1419:     gone, far gone! And truly in my youth I suff'red much extremity
1420:     for love- very near this. I'll speak to him again.- What do you
1421:     read, my lord?
1422:   Ham. Words, words, words.
1423:   Pol. What is the matter, my lord?
1424:   Ham. Between who?
1425:   Pol. I mean, the matter that you read, my lord.
1426:   Ham. Slanders, sir; for the satirical rogue says here that old men
1427:     have grey beards; that their faces are wrinkled; their eyes
1428:     purging thick amber and plum-tree gum; and that they have a
1429:     plentiful lack of wit, together with most weak hams. All which,
1430:     sir, though I most powerfully and potently believe, yet I hold it
1431:     not honesty to have it thus set down; for you yourself, sir,
1432:     should be old as I am if, like a crab, you could go backward.  
1433:   Pol. [aside] Though this be madness, yet there is a method in't.-
1434:    Will You walk out of the air, my lord?
1435:   Ham. Into my grave?
1436:   Pol. Indeed, that is out o' th' air. [Aside] How pregnant sometimes
1437:     his replies are! a happiness that often madness hits on, which
1438:     reason and sanity could not so prosperously be delivered of. I
1439:     will leave him and suddenly contrive the means of meeting between
1440:     him and my daughter.- My honourable lord, I will most humbly take
1441:     my leave of you.
1442:   Ham. You cannot, sir, take from me anything that I will more
1443:     willingly part withal- except my life, except my life, except my
1444:     life,

1447: 
1448:   Pol. Fare you well, my lord.
1449:   Ham. These tedious old fools!
1450:   Pol. You go to seek the Lord Hamlet. There he is.
1451:   Ros. [to Polonius] God save you, sir!
1452:                                                 Exit [Polonius].  
1453:   Guil. My honour'd lord!
1454:   Ros. My most dear lord!
1455:   Ham. My excellent good friends! How dost thou, Guildenstern? Ah,
1456:     Rosencrantz! Good lads, how do ye both?
1457:   Ros. As the indifferent children of the earth.
1458:   Guil. Happy in that we are not over-happy.
1459:     On Fortune's cap we are not the very button.
1460:   Ham. Nor the soles of her shoe?
1461:   Ros. Neither, my lord.
1462:   Ham. Then you live about her waist, or in the middle of her
1463:     favours?
1464:   Guil. Faith, her privates we.
1465:   Ham. In the secret parts of Fortune? O! most true! she is a
1466:     strumpet. What news ?
1467:   Ros. None, my lord, but that the world's grown honest.
1468:   Ham. Then is doomsday near! But your news is not true. Let me
1469:     question more in particular. What have you, my good friends,
1470:     deserved at the hands of Fortune that she sends you to prison
1471:     hither?
1472:   Guil. Prison, my lord?  
1473:   Ham. Denmark's a prison.
1474:   Ros. Then is the world one.
1475:   Ham. A goodly one; in which there are many confines, wards, and
1476:     dungeons, Denmark being one o' th' worst.
1477:   Ros. We think not so, my lord.
1478:   Ham. Why, then 'tis none to you; for there is nothing either good
1479:     or bad but thinking makes it so. To me it is a prison.
1480:   Ros. Why, then your ambition makes it one. 'Tis too narrow for your
1481:     mind.
1482:   Ham. O God, I could be bounded in a nutshell and count myself a
1483:     king of infinite space, were it not that I have bad dreams.
1484:   Guil. Which dreams indeed are ambition; for the very substance of
1485:     the ambitious is merely the shadow of a dream.
1486:   Ham. A dream itself is but a shadow.
1487:   Ros. Truly, and I hold ambition of so airy and light a quality that
1488:     it is but a shadow's shadow.
1489:   Ham. Then are our beggars bodies, and our monarchs and outstretch'd
1490:     heroes the beggars' shadows. Shall we to th' court? for, by my
1491:     fay, I cannot reason.
1492:   Both. We'll wait upon you.  
1493:   Ham. No such matter! I will not sort you with the rest of my
1494:     servants; for, to speak to you like an honest man, I am most
1495:     dreadfully attended. But in the beaten way of friendship, what
1496:     make you at Elsinore?
1497:   Ros. To visit you, my lord; no other occasion.
1498:   Ham. Beggar that I am, I am even poor in thanks; but I thank you;
1499:     and sure, dear friends, my thanks are too dear a halfpenny. Were
1500:     you not sent for? Is it your own inclining? Is it a free
1501:     visitation? Come, deal justly with me. Come, come! Nay, speak.
1502:   Guil. What should we say, my lord?
1503:   Ham. Why, anything- but to th' purpose. You were sent for; and
1504:     there is a kind of confession in your looks, which your modesties
1505:     have not craft enough to colour. I know the good King and Queen
1506:     have sent for you.
1507:   Ros. To what end, my lord?
1508:   Ham. That you must teach me. But let me conjure you by the rights
1509:     of our fellowship, by the consonancy of our youth, by the
1510:     obligation of our ever-preserved love, and by what more dear a
1511:     better proposer could charge you withal, be even and direct with
1512:     me, whether you were sent for or no.  
1513:   Ros. [aside to Guildenstern] What say you?
1514:   Ham. [aside] Nay then, I have an eye of you.- If you love me, hold
1515:     not off.
1516:   Guil. My lord, we were sent for.
1517:   Ham. I will tell you why. So shall my anticipation prevent your
1518:     discovery, and your secrecy to the King and Queen moult no
1519:     feather. I have of late- but wherefore I know not- lost all my
1520:     mirth, forgone all custom of exercises; and indeed, it goes so
1521:     heavily with my disposition that this goodly frame, the earth,
1522:     seems to me a sterile promontory; this most excellent canopy, the
1523:     air, look you, this brave o'erhanging firmament, this majestical
1524:     roof fretted with golden fire- why, it appeareth no other thing
1525:     to me than a foul and pestilent congregation of vapours. What a
1526:     piece of work is a man! how noble in reason! how infinite in
1527:     faculties! in form and moving how express and admirable! in
1528:     action how like an angel! in apprehension how like a god! the
1529:     beauty of the world, the paragon of animals! And yet to me what
1530:     is this quintessence of dust? Man delights not me- no, nor woman
1531:     neither, though by your smiling you seem to say so.
1532:   Ros. My lord, there was no such stuff in my thoughts.  
1533:   Ham. Why did you laugh then, when I said 'Man delights not me'?
1534:   Ros. To think, my lord, if you delight not in man, what lenten
1535:     entertainment the players shall receive from you. We coted them
1536:     on the way, and hither are they coming to offer you service.
1537:   Ham. He that plays the king shall be welcome- his Majesty shall
1538:     have tribute of me; the adventurous knight shall use his foil and
1539:     target; the lover shall not sigh gratis; the humorous man shall
1540:     end his part in peace; the clown shall make those laugh whose
1541:     lungs are tickle o' th' sere; and the lady shall say her mind
1542:     freely, or the blank verse shall halt fort. What players are
1543:     they?
1544:   Ros. Even those you were wont to take such delight in, the
1545:     tragedians of the city.
1546:   Ham. How chances it they travel? Their residence, both in
1547:     reputation and profit, was better both ways.
1548:   Ros. I think their inhibition comes by the means of the late
1549:     innovation.
1550:   Ham. Do they hold the same estimation they did when I was in the
1551:     city? Are they so follow'd?
1552:   Ros. No indeed are they not.  
1553:   Ham. How comes it? Do they grow rusty?
1554:   Ros. Nay, their endeavour keeps in the wonted pace; but there is,
1555:     sir, an eyrie of children, little eyases, that cry out on the top
1556:     of question and are most tyrannically clapp'd fort. These are now
1557:     the fashion, and so berattle the common stages (so they call
1558:     them) that many wearing rapiers are afraid of goosequills and
1559:     dare scarce come thither.
1560:   Ham. What, are they children? Who maintains 'em? How are they
1561:     escoted? Will they pursue the quality no longer than they can
1562:     sing? Will they not say afterwards, if they should grow
1563:     themselves to common players (as it is most like, if their means
1564:     are no better), their writers do them wrong to make them exclaim
1565:     against their own succession.
1566:   Ros. Faith, there has been much to do on both sides; and the nation
1567:     holds it no sin to tarre them to controversy. There was, for a
1568:     while, no money bid for argument unless the poet and the player
1569:     went to cuffs in the question.
1570:   Ham. Is't possible?
1571:   Guil. O, there has been much throwing about of brains.
1572:   Ham. Do the boys carry it away?  
1573:   Ros. Ay, that they do, my lord- Hercules and his load too.
1574:   Ham. It is not very strange; for my uncle is King of Denmark, and
1575:     those that would make mows at him while my father lived give
1576:     twenty, forty, fifty, a hundred ducats apiece for his picture in
1577:     little. 'Sblood, there is something in this more than natural, if
1578:     philosophy could find it out.

1580:                      Flourish for the Players.

1582:   Guil. There are the players.
1583:   Ham. Gentlemen, you are welcome to Elsinore. Your hands, come! Th'
1584:     appurtenance of welcome is fashion and ceremony. Let me comply
1585:     with you in this garb, lest my extent to the players (which I
1586:     tell you must show fairly outwards) should more appear like

1600:     man is twice a child.
1601:   Ham. I will prophesy he comes to tell me of the players. Mark it.-
1602:    You say right, sir; a Monday morning; twas so indeed.

1613:     individable, or poem unlimited. Seneca cannot be too heavy, nor
1614:     Plautus too light. For the law of writ and the liberty, these are
1615:     the only men.
