1640:                      Enter four or five Players.
1641: 
1642:     You are welcome, masters; welcome, all.- I am glad to see thee
1643:     well.- Welcome, good friends.- O, my old friend? Why, thy face is
1644:     valanc'd since I saw thee last. Com'st' thou to' beard me in
1645:     Denmark?- What, my young lady and mistress? By'r Lady, your
1646:     ladyship is nearer to heaven than when I saw you last by the
1647:     altitude of a chopine. Pray God your voice, like a piece of
1648:     uncurrent gold, be not crack'd within the ring.- Masters, you are
1649:     all welcome. We'll e'en to't like French falconers, fly at
1650:     anything we see. We'll have a speech straight. Come, give us a
1651:     taste of your quality. Come, a passionate speech.
1652:   1. Play. What speech, my good lord?
1653:   Ham. I heard thee speak me a speech once, but it was never acted;
1654:     or if it was, not above once; for the play, I remember, pleas'd  
1655:     not the million, 'twas caviary to the general; but it was (as I
1656:     receiv'd it, and others, whose judgments in such matters cried in
1657:     the top of mine) an excellent play, well digested in the scenes,
1658:     set down with as much modesty as cunning. I remember one said
1659:     there were no sallets in the lines to make the matter savoury,
1660:     nor no matter in the phrase that might indict the author of
1661:     affectation; but call'd it an honest method, as wholesome as
1662:     sweet, and by very much more handsome than fine. One speech in't
1663:     I chiefly lov'd. 'Twas AEneas' tale to Dido, and thereabout of it
1664:     especially where he speaks of Priam's slaughter. If it live in
1665:     your memory, begin at this line- let me see, let me see:
1666: 
1667:          'The rugged Pyrrhus, like th' Hyrcanian beast-'
1668: 
1669:     'Tis not so; it begins with Pyrrhus:
1670: 
1671:          'The rugged Pyrrhus, he whose sable arms,
1672:          Black as his purpose, did the night resemble
1673:          When he lay couched in the ominous horse,
1674:          Hath now this dread and black complexion smear'd  
1675:          With heraldry more dismal. Head to foot
1676:          Now is be total gules, horridly trick'd
1677:          With blood of fathers, mothers, daughters, sons,
1678:          Bak'd and impasted with the parching streets,
1679:          That lend a tyrannous and a damned light
1680:          To their lord's murther. Roasted in wrath and fire,
1681:          And thus o'ersized with coagulate gore,
1682:          With eyes like carbuncles, the hellish Pyrrhus
1683:          Old grandsire Priam seeks.'
1684: 
1685:     So, proceed you.
1686:   Pol. Fore God, my lord, well spoken, with good accent and good
1687:      discretion.
1688: 
1689:   1. Play. 'Anon he finds him,
1690:       Striking too short at Greeks. His antique sword,
1691:       Rebellious to his arm, lies where it falls,
1692:       Repugnant to command. Unequal match'd,
1693:       Pyrrhus at Priam drives, in rage strikes wide;
1694:       But with the whiff and wind of his fell sword  
1695:       Th' unnerved father falls. Then senseless Ilium,
1696:       Seeming to feel this blow, with flaming top
1697:       Stoops to his base, and with a hideous crash
1698:       Takes prisoner Pyrrhus' ear. For lo! his sword,
1699:       Which was declining on the milky head
1700:       Of reverend Priam, seem'd i' th' air to stick.
1701:       So, as a painted tyrant, Pyrrhus stood,
1702:       And, like a neutral to his will and matter,
1703:       Did nothing.
1704:       But, as we often see, against some storm,
1705:       A silence in the heavens, the rack stand still,
1706:       The bold winds speechless, and the orb below
1707:       As hush as death- anon the dreadful thunder
1708:       Doth rend the region; so, after Pyrrhus' pause,
1709:       Aroused vengeance sets him new awork;
1710:       And never did the Cyclops' hammers fall
1711:       On Mars's armour, forg'd for proof eterne,
1712:       With less remorse than Pyrrhus' bleeding sword
1713:       Now falls on Priam.
1714:       Out, out, thou strumpet Fortune! All you gods,  
1715:       In general synod take away her power;
1716:       Break all the spokes and fellies from her wheel,
1717:       And bowl the round nave down the hill of heaven,
1718:       As low as to the fiends!
1719: 
1720:   Pol. This is too long.
1721:   Ham. It shall to the barber's, with your beard.- Prithee say on.
1722:     He's for a jig or a tale of bawdry, or he sleeps. Say on; come to
1723:     Hecuba.
1724: 
1725:   1. Play. 'But who, O who, had seen the mobled queen-'
1726: 
1727:   Ham. 'The mobled queen'?
1728:   Pol. That's good! 'Mobled queen' is good.
1729: 
1730:   1. Play. 'Run barefoot up and down, threat'ning the flames
1731:       With bisson rheum; a clout upon that head
1732:       Where late the diadem stood, and for a robe,
1733:       About her lank and all o'erteemed loins,
1734:       A blanket, in the alarm of fear caught up-  
1735:       Who this had seen, with tongue in venom steep'd
1736:       'Gainst Fortune's state would treason have pronounc'd.
1737:       But if the gods themselves did see her then,
1738:       When she saw Pyrrhus make malicious sport
1739:       In Mincing with his sword her husband's limbs,
1740:       The instant burst of clamour that she made
1741:       (Unless things mortal move them not at all)
1742:       Would have made milch the burning eyes of heaven
1743:       And passion in the gods.'
1744: 
1745:   Pol. Look, whe'r he has not turn'd his colour, and has tears in's
1746:     eyes. Prithee no more!
1747:   Ham. 'Tis well. I'll have thee speak out the rest of this soon.-
1748:     Good my lord, will you see the players well bestow'd? Do you
1749:     hear? Let them be well us'd; for they are the abstract and brief
1750:     chronicles of the time. After your death you were better have a
1751:     bad epitaph than their ill report while you live.
1752:   Pol. My lord, I will use them according to their desert.
1753:   Ham. God's bodykins, man, much better! Use every man after his
1754:     desert, and who should scape whipping? Use them after your own  
1755:     honour and dignity. The less they deserve, the more merit is in
1756:     your bounty. Take them in.
1757:   Pol. Come, sirs.
1758:   Ham. Follow him, friends. We'll hear a play to-morrow.
1759:                  Exeunt Polonius and Players [except the First].
1760:     Dost thou hear me, old friend? Can you play 'The Murther of
1761:     Gonzago'?
1762:   1. Play. Ay, my lord.
1763:   Ham. We'll ha't to-morrow night. You could, for a need, study a
1764:     speech of some dozen or sixteen lines which I would set down and
1765:     insert in't, could you not?
1766:   1. Play. Ay, my lord.
1767:   Ham. Very well. Follow that lord- and look you mock him not.
1768:                                             [Exit First Player.]
1769:     My good friends, I'll leave you till night. You are welcome to
1770:     Elsinore.
1771:   Ros. Good my lord!
1772:   Ham. Ay, so, God b' wi' ye!
1773:                             [Exeunt Rosencrantz and Guildenstern
1774:     Now I am alone.  
1775:     O what a rogue and peasant slave am I!
1776:     Is it not monstrous that this player here,
1777:     But in a fiction, in a dream of passion,
1778:     Could force his soul so to his own conceit
1779:     That, from her working, all his visage wann'd,
1780:     Tears in his eyes, distraction in's aspect,
1781:     A broken voice, and his whole function suiting
1782:     With forms to his conceit? And all for nothing!
1783:     For Hecuba!
1784:     What's Hecuba to him, or he to Hecuba,
1785:     That he should weep for her? What would he do,
1786:     Had he the motive and the cue for passion
1787:     That I have? He would drown the stage with tears
1788:     And cleave the general ear with horrid speech;
1789:     Make mad the guilty and appal the free,
1790:     Confound the ignorant, and amaze indeed
1791:     The very faculties of eyes and ears.
1792:     Yet I,
1793:     A dull and muddy-mettled rascal, peak
1794:     Like John-a-dreams, unpregnant of my cause,  
1795:     And can say nothing! No, not for a king,
1796:     Upon whose property and most dear life
1797:     A damn'd defeat was made. Am I a coward?
1798:     Who calls me villain? breaks my pate across?
1799:     Plucks off my beard and blows it in my face?
1800:     Tweaks me by th' nose? gives me the lie i' th' throat
1801:     As deep as to the lungs? Who does me this, ha?
1802:     'Swounds, I should take it! for it cannot be
1803:     But I am pigeon-liver'd and lack gall
1804:     To make oppression bitter, or ere this
1805:     I should have fatted all the region kites
1806:     With this slave's offal. Bloody bawdy villain!
1807:     Remorseless, treacherous, lecherous, kindless villain!
1808:     O, vengeance!
1809:     Why, what an ass am I! This is most brave,
1810:     That I, the son of a dear father murther'd,
1811:     Prompted to my revenge by heaven and hell,
1812:     Must (like a whore) unpack my heart with words
1813:     And fall a-cursing like a very drab,
1814:     A scullion!  
1815:     Fie upon't! foh! About, my brain! Hum, I have heard
1816:     That guilty creatures, sitting at a play,
1817:     Have by the very cunning of the scene
1818:     Been struck so to the soul that presently
1819:     They have proclaim'd their malefactions;
1820:     For murther, though it have no tongue, will speak
1821:     With most miraculous organ, I'll have these Players
1822:     Play something like the murther of my father
1823:     Before mine uncle. I'll observe his looks;
1824:     I'll tent him to the quick. If he but blench,
1825:     I know my course. The spirit that I have seen
1826:     May be a devil; and the devil hath power
1827:     T' assume a pleasing shape; yea, and perhaps
1828:     Out of my weakness and my melancholy,
1829:     As he is very potent with such spirits,
1830:     Abuses me to damn me. I'll have grounds
1831:     More relative than this. The play's the thing
1832:     Wherein I'll catch the conscience of the King.         Exit.

1838: ACT III. Scene I.
1839: Elsinore. A room in the Castle.
1840: 
1841: Enter King, Queen, Polonius, Ophelia, Rosencrantz, Guildenstern, and Lords.
1842: 
1843:   King. And can you by no drift of circumstance
1844:     Get from him why he puts on this confusion,
1845:     Grating so harshly all his days of quiet
1846:     With turbulent and dangerous lunacy?
1847:   Ros. He does confess he feels himself distracted,
1848:     But from what cause he will by no means speak.
1849:   Guil. Nor do we find him forward to be sounded,
1850:     But with a crafty madness keeps aloof
1851:     When we would bring him on to some confession
1852:     Of his true state.
1853:   Queen. Did he receive you well?
1854:   Ros. Most like a gentleman.
1855:   Guil. But with much forcing of his disposition.
1856:   Ros. Niggard of question, but of our demands
1857:     Most free in his reply.
1858:   Queen. Did you assay him  
1859:     To any pastime?
1860:   Ros. Madam, it so fell out that certain players
1861:     We o'erraught on the way. Of these we told him,
1862:     And there did seem in him a kind of joy
1863:     To hear of it. They are here about the court,
1864:     And, as I think, they have already order
1865:     This night to play before him.
1866:   Pol. 'Tis most true;
1867:     And he beseech'd me to entreat your Majesties
1868:     To hear and see the matter.
1869:   King. With all my heart, and it doth much content me
1870:     To hear him so inclin'd.
1871:     Good gentlemen, give him a further edge
1872:     And drive his purpose on to these delights.
1873:   Ros. We shall, my lord.
1874:                             Exeunt Rosencrantz and Guildenstern.
1875:   King. Sweet Gertrude, leave us too;
1876:     For we have closely sent for Hamlet hither,
1877:     That he, as 'twere by accident, may here
1878:     Affront Ophelia.  
1879:     Her father and myself (lawful espials)
1880:     Will so bestow ourselves that, seeing unseen,
1881:     We may of their encounter frankly judge
1882:     And gather by him, as he is behav'd,
1883:     If't be th' affliction of his love, or no,
1884:     That thus he suffers for.
1885:   Queen. I shall obey you;
1886:     And for your part, Ophelia, I do wish
1887:     That your good beauties be the happy cause
1888:     Of Hamlet's wildness. So shall I hope your virtues
1889:     Will bring him to his wonted way again,
1890:     To both your honours.
1891:   Oph. Madam, I wish it may.
1892:                                                    [Exit Queen.]
1893:   Pol. Ophelia, walk you here.- Gracious, so please you,
1894:     We will bestow ourselves.- [To Ophelia] Read on this book,
1895:     That show of such an exercise may colour
1896:     Your loneliness.- We are oft to blame in this,
1897:     'Tis too much prov'd, that with devotion's visage
1898:     And pious action we do sugar o'er  
1899:     The Devil himself.
1900:   King. [aside] O, 'tis too true!
1901:     How smart a lash that speech doth give my conscience!
1902:     The harlot's cheek, beautied with plast'ring art,
1903:     Is not more ugly to the thing that helps it
1904:     Than is my deed to my most painted word.
1905:     O heavy burthen!
1906:   Pol. I hear him coming. Let's withdraw, my lord.
1907:                                       Exeunt King and Polonius].
1908: 
1909:                            Enter Hamlet.
1910: 
1911:   Ham. To be, or not to be- that is the question:
1912:     Whether 'tis nobler in the mind to suffer
1913:     The slings and arrows of outrageous fortune
1914:     Or to take arms against a sea of troubles,
1915:     And by opposing end them. To die- to sleep-
1916:     No more; and by a sleep to say we end
1917:     The heartache, and the thousand natural shocks
1918:     That flesh is heir to. 'Tis a consummation  
1919:     Devoutly to be wish'd. To die- to sleep.
1920:     To sleep- perchance to dream: ay, there's the rub!
1921:     For in that sleep of death what dreams may come
1922:     When we have shuffled off this mortal coil,
1923:     Must give us pause. There's the respect
1924:     That makes calamity of so long life.
1925:     For who would bear the whips and scorns of time,
1926:     Th' oppressor's wrong, the proud man's contumely,
1927:     The pangs of despis'd love, the law's delay,
1928:     The insolence of office, and the spurns
1929:     That patient merit of th' unworthy takes,
1930:     When he himself might his quietus make
1931:     With a bare bodkin? Who would these fardels bear,
1932:     To grunt and sweat under a weary life,
1933:     But that the dread of something after death-
1934:     The undiscover'd country, from whose bourn
1935:     No traveller returns- puzzles the will,
1936:     And makes us rather bear those ills we have
1937:     Than fly to others that we know not of?
1938:     Thus conscience does make cowards of us all,  
1939:     And thus the native hue of resolution
1940:     Is sicklied o'er with the pale cast of thought,
1941:     And enterprises of great pith and moment
1942:     With this regard their currents turn awry
1943:     And lose the name of action.- Soft you now!
1944:     The fair Ophelia!- Nymph, in thy orisons
1945:     Be all my sins rememb'red.
1946:   Oph. Good my lord,
1947:     How does your honour for this many a day?
1948:   Ham. I humbly thank you; well, well, well.
1949:   Oph. My lord, I have remembrances of yours
1950:     That I have longed long to re-deliver.
1951:     I pray you, now receive them.
1952:   Ham. No, not I!
1953:     I never gave you aught.
1954:   Oph. My honour'd lord, you know right well you did,
1955:     And with them words of so sweet breath compos'd
1956:     As made the things more rich. Their perfume lost,
1957:     Take these again; for to the noble mind
1958:     Rich gifts wax poor when givers prove unkind.  
1959:     There, my lord.
1960:   Ham. Ha, ha! Are you honest?
1961:   Oph. My lord?
1962:   Ham. Are you fair?
1963:   Oph. What means your lordship?
1964:   Ham. That if you be honest and fair, your honesty should admit no
1965:     discourse to your beauty.
1966:   Oph. Could beauty, my lord, have better commerce than with honesty?
1967:   Ham. Ay, truly; for the power of beauty will sooner transform
1968:     honesty from what it is to a bawd than the force of honesty can
1969:     translate beauty into his likeness. This was sometime a paradox,
1970:     but now the time gives it proof. I did love you once.
1971:   Oph. Indeed, my lord, you made me believe so.
1972:   Ham. You should not have believ'd me; for virtue cannot so
1973:     inoculate our old stock but we shall relish of it. I loved you
1974:     not.
1975:   Oph. I was the more deceived.
1976:   Ham. Get thee to a nunnery! Why wouldst thou be a breeder of
1977:     sinners? I am myself indifferent honest, but yet I could accuse
1978:     me of such things that it were better my mother had not borne me.  
1979:     I am very proud, revengeful, ambitious; with more offences at my
1980:     beck than I have thoughts to put them in, imagination to give
1981:     them shape, or time to act them in. What should such fellows as I
1982:     do, crawling between earth and heaven? We are arrant knaves all;
1983:     believe none of us. Go thy ways to a nunnery. Where's your
1984:     father?
1985:   Oph. At home, my lord.
1986:   Ham. Let the doors be shut upon him, that he may play the fool
1987:     nowhere but in's own house. Farewell.
1988:   Oph. O, help him, you sweet heavens!
1989:   Ham. If thou dost marry, I'll give thee this plague for thy dowry:
1990:     be thou as chaste as ice, as pure as snow, thou shalt not escape
1991:     calumny. Get thee to a nunnery. Go, farewell. Or if thou wilt
1992:     needs marry, marry a fool; for wise men know well enough what
1993:     monsters you make of them. To a nunnery, go; and quickly too.
1994:     Farewell.
1995:   Oph. O heavenly powers, restore him!
1996:   Ham. I have heard of your paintings too, well enough. God hath
1997:     given you one face, and you make yourselves another. You jig, you
1998:     amble, and you lisp; you nickname God's creatures and make your  
1999:     wantonness your ignorance. Go to, I'll no more on't! it hath made
2000:     me mad. I say, we will have no moe marriages. Those that are
2001:     married already- all but one- shall live; the rest shall keep as
2002:     they are. To a nunnery, go.                            Exit.
2003:   Oph. O, what a noble mind is here o'erthrown!
2004:     The courtier's, scholar's, soldier's, eye, tongue, sword,
2005:     Th' expectancy and rose of the fair state,
2006:     The glass of fashion and the mould of form,
2007:     Th' observ'd of all observers- quite, quite down!
2008:     And I, of ladies most deject and wretched,
2009:     That suck'd the honey of his music vows,
2010:     Now see that noble and most sovereign reason,
2011:     Like sweet bells jangled, out of tune and harsh;
2012:     That unmatch'd form and feature of blown youth
2013:     Blasted with ecstasy. O, woe is me
2014:     T' have seen what I have seen, see what I see!
2015: 
2016:                    Enter King and Polonius.
2017: 
2018:   King. Love? his affections do not that way tend;  
2019:     Nor what he spake, though it lack'd form a little,
2020:     Was not like madness. There's something in his soul
2021:     O'er which his melancholy sits on brood;
2022:     And I do doubt the hatch and the disclose
2023:     Will be some danger; which for to prevent,
2024:     I have in quick determination
2025:     Thus set it down: he shall with speed to England
2026:     For the demand of our neglected tribute.
2027:     Haply the seas, and countries different,
2028:     With variable objects, shall expel
2029:     This something-settled matter in his heart,
2030:     Whereon his brains still beating puts him thus
2031:     From fashion of himself. What think you on't?
2032:   Pol. It shall do well. But yet do I believe
2033:     The origin and commencement of his grief
2034:     Sprung from neglected love.- How now, Ophelia?
2035:     You need not tell us what Lord Hamlet said.
2036:     We heard it all.- My lord, do as you please;
2037:     But if you hold it fit, after the play
2038:     Let his queen mother all alone entreat him  
2039:     To show his grief. Let her be round with him;
2040:     And I'll be plac'd so please you, in the ear
2041:     Of all their conference. If she find him not,
2042:     To England send him; or confine him where
2043:     Your wisdom best shall think.
2044:   King. It shall be so.
2045:     Madness in great ones must not unwatch'd go.         Exeunt.
2046:

2049: 
2050: Scene II.
2051: Elsinore. hall in the Castle.
2052: 
2053: Enter Hamlet and three of the Players.
2054: 
2055:   Ham. Speak the speech, I pray you, as I pronounc'd it to you,
2056:     trippingly on the tongue. But if you mouth it, as many of our
2057:     players do, I had as live the town crier spoke my lines. Nor do
2058:     not saw the air too much with your hand, thus, but use all
2059:     gently; for in the very torrent, tempest, and (as I may say)
2060:     whirlwind of your passion, you must acquire and beget a
2061:     temperance that may give it smoothness. O, it offends me to the
2062:     soul to hear a robustious periwig-pated fellow tear a passion to
2063:     tatters, to very rags, to split the cars of the groundlings, who
2064:     (for the most part) are capable of nothing but inexplicable dumb
2065:     shows and noise. I would have such a fellow whipp'd for o'erdoing
2066:     Termagant. It out-herods Herod. Pray you avoid it.
2067:   Player. I warrant your honour.
2068:   Ham. Be not too tame neither; but let your own discretion be your
2069:     tutor. Suit the action to the word, the word to the action; with
2070:     this special observance, that you o'erstep not the modesty of
2071:     nature: for anything so overdone is from the purpose of playing,  
2072:     whose end, both at the first and now, was and is, to hold, as
2073:     'twere, the mirror up to nature; to show Virtue her own feature,
2074:     scorn her own image, and the very age and body of the time his
2075:     form and pressure. Now this overdone, or come tardy off, though
2076:     it make the unskilful laugh, cannot but make the judicious
2077:     grieve; the censure of the which one must in your allowance
2078:     o'erweigh a whole theatre of others. O, there be players that I
2079:     have seen play, and heard others praise, and that highly (not to
2080:     speak it profanely), that, neither having the accent of
2081:     Christians, nor the gait of Christian, pagan, nor man, have so
2082:     strutted and bellowed that I have thought some of Nature's
2083:     journeymen had made men, and not made them well, they imitated
2084:     humanity so abominably.
2085:   Player. I hope we have reform'd that indifferently with us, sir.
2086:   Ham. O, reform it altogether! And let those that play your clowns
2087:     speak no more than is set down for them. For there be of them
2088:     that will themselves laugh, to set on some quantity of barren
2089:     spectators to laugh too, though in the mean time some necessary
2090:     question of the play be then to be considered. That's villanous
2091:     and shows a most pitiful ambition in the fool that uses it. Go  
2092:     make you ready.
2093:                                                  Exeunt Players.
2094: 
2095:             Enter Polonius, Rosencrantz, and Guildenstern.
2096: 
2097:     How now, my lord? Will the King hear this piece of work?
2098:   Pol. And the Queen too, and that presently.
2099:   Ham. Bid the players make haste, [Exit Polonius.] Will you two
2100:     help to hasten them?
2101:   Both. We will, my lord.                       Exeunt they two.
2102:   Ham. What, ho, Horatio!
2103: 
2104:                       Enter Horatio.
2105: 
2106:   Hor. Here, sweet lord, at your service.
2107:   Ham. Horatio, thou art e'en as just a man
2108:     As e'er my conversation cop'd withal.
2109:   Hor. O, my dear lord!
2110:   Ham. Nay, do not think I flatter;
2111:     For what advancement may I hope from thee,  
2112:     That no revenue hast but thy good spirits
2113:     To feed and clothe thee? Why should the poor be flatter'd?
2114:     No, let the candied tongue lick absurd pomp,
2115:     And crook the pregnant hinges of the knee
2116:     Where thrift may follow fawning. Dost thou hear?
2117:     Since my dear soul was mistress of her choice
2118:     And could of men distinguish, her election
2119:     Hath scald thee for herself. For thou hast been
2120:     As one, in suff'ring all, that suffers nothing;
2121:     A man that Fortune's buffets and rewards
2122:     Hast ta'en with equal thanks; and blest are those
2123:     Whose blood and judgment are so well commingled
2124:     That they are not a pipe for Fortune's finger
2125:     To sound what stop she please. Give me that man
2126:     That is not passion's slave, and I will wear him
2127:     In my heart's core, ay, in my heart of heart,
2128:     As I do thee. Something too much of this I
2129:     There is a play to-night before the King.
2130:     One scene of it comes near the circumstance,
2131:     Which I have told thee, of my father's death.  
2132:     I prithee, when thou seest that act afoot,
2133:     Even with the very comment of thy soul
2134:     Observe my uncle. If his occulted guilt
2135:     Do not itself unkennel in one speech,
2136:     It is a damned ghost that we have seen,
2137:     And my imaginations are as foul
2138:     As Vulcan's stithy. Give him heedful note;
2139:     For I mine eyes will rivet to his face,
2140:     And after we will both our judgments join
2141:     In censure of his seeming.
2142:   Hor. Well, my lord.
2143:     If he steal aught the whilst this play is playing,
2144:     And scape detecting, I will pay the theft.
2145: 
2146:     Sound a flourish. [Enter Trumpets and Kettledrums. Danish
2147:     march. [Enter King, Queen, Polonius, Ophelia, Rosencrantz,
2148:       Guildenstern, and other Lords attendant, with the Guard
2149:                        carrying torches.
2150: 
2151:   Ham. They are coming to the play. I must be idle.  
2152:     Get you a place.
2153:   King. How fares our cousin Hamlet?
2154:   Ham. Excellent, i' faith; of the chameleon's dish. I eat the air,
2155:     promise-cramm'd. You cannot feed capons so.
2156:   King. I have nothing with this answer, Hamlet. These words are not
2157:     mine.
2158:   Ham. No, nor mine now. [To Polonius] My lord, you play'd once
2159:     i' th' university, you say?
2160:   Pol. That did I, my lord, and was accounted a good actor.
2161:   Ham. What did you enact?
2162:   Pol. I did enact Julius Caesar; I was kill'd i' th' Capitol; Brutus
2163:     kill'd me.
2164:   Ham. It was a brute part of him to kill so capital a calf there. Be
2165:     the players ready.
2166:   Ros. Ay, my lord. They stay upon your patience.
2167:   Queen. Come hither, my dear Hamlet, sit by me.
2168:   Ham. No, good mother. Here's metal more attractive.
2169:   Pol. [to the King] O, ho! do you mark that?
2170:   Ham. Lady, shall I lie in your lap?
2171:                                   [Sits down at Ophelia's feet.]  
2172:   Oph. No, my lord.
2173:   Ham. I mean, my head upon your lap?
2174:   Oph. Ay, my lord.
2175:   Ham. Do you think I meant country matters?
2176:   Oph. I think nothing, my lord.
2177:   Ham. That's a fair thought to lie between maids' legs.
2178:   Oph. What is, my lord?
2179:   Ham. Nothing.
2180:   Oph. You are merry, my lord.
2181:   Ham. Who, I?
2182:   Oph. Ay, my lord.
2183:   Ham. O God, your only jig-maker! What should a man do but be merry?
2184:     For look you how cheerfully my mother looks, and my father died
2185:     within 's two hours.
2186:   Oph. Nay 'tis twice two months, my lord.
2187:   Ham. So long? Nay then, let the devil wear black, for I'll have a
2188:     suit of sables. O heavens! die two months ago, and not forgotten
2189:     yet? Then there's hope a great man's memory may outlive his life
2190:     half a year. But, by'r Lady, he must build churches then; or else
2191:     shall he suffer not thinking on, with the hobby-horse, whose  
2192:     epitaph is 'For O, for O, the hobby-horse is forgot!'
2193: 
2194:                Hautboys play. The dumb show enters.
2195: 
2196:     Enter a King and a Queen very lovingly; the Queen embracing
2197:     him and he her. She kneels, and makes show of protestation
2198:     unto him. He takes her up, and declines his head upon her
2199:     neck. He lays him down upon a bank of flowers. She, seeing
2200:     him asleep, leaves him. Anon comes in a fellow, takes off his
2201:     crown, kisses it, pours poison in the sleeper's ears, and
2202:     leaves him. The Queen returns, finds the King dead, and makes
2203:     passionate action. The Poisoner with some three or four Mutes,
2204:     comes in again, seem to condole with her. The dead body is
2205:     carried away. The Poisoner wooes the Queen with gifts; she
2206:     seems harsh and unwilling awhile, but in the end accepts
2207:     his love.
2208:                                                          Exeunt.
2209: 
2210:   Oph. What means this, my lord?
2211:   Ham. Marry, this is miching malhecho; it means mischief.  
2212:   Oph. Belike this show imports the argument of the play.
2213: 
2214:                       Enter Prologue.
2215: 
2216:   Ham. We shall know by this fellow. The players cannot keep counsel;
2217:     they'll tell all.
2218:   Oph. Will he tell us what this show meant?
2219:   Ham. Ay, or any show that you'll show him. Be not you asham'd to
2220:     show, he'll not shame to tell you what it means.
2221:   Oph. You are naught, you are naught! I'll mark the play.
2222: 
2223:     Pro. For us, and for our tragedy,
2224:       Here stooping to your clemency,
2225:       We beg your hearing patiently.                     [Exit.]
2226: 
2227:   Ham. Is this a prologue, or the posy of a ring?
2228:   Oph. 'Tis brief, my lord.
2229:   Ham. As woman's love.
2230: 
2231:               Enter [two Players as] King and Queen.  
2232: 
2233:     King. Full thirty times hath Phoebus' cart gone round
2234:       Neptune's salt wash and Tellus' orbed ground,
2235:       And thirty dozed moons with borrowed sheen
2236:       About the world have times twelve thirties been,
2237:       Since love our hearts, and Hymen did our hands,
2238:       Unite comutual in most sacred bands.
2239:     Queen. So many journeys may the sun and moon
2240:       Make us again count o'er ere love be done!
2241:       But woe is me! you are so sick of late,
2242:       So far from cheer and from your former state.
2243:       That I distrust you. Yet, though I distrust,
2244:       Discomfort you, my lord, it nothing must;
2245:       For women's fear and love holds quantity,
2246:       In neither aught, or in extremity.
2247:       Now what my love is, proof hath made you know;
2248:       And as my love is siz'd, my fear is so.
2249:       Where love is great, the littlest doubts are fear;
2250:       Where little fears grow great, great love grows there.
2251:     King. Faith, I must leave thee, love, and shortly too;  
2252:       My operant powers their functions leave to do.
2253:       And thou shalt live in this fair world behind,
2254:       Honour'd, belov'd, and haply one as kind
2255:       For husband shalt thou-
2256:     Queen. O, confound the rest!
2257:       Such love must needs be treason in my breast.
2258:       When second husband let me be accurst!
2259:       None wed the second but who killed the first.
2260: 
2261:   Ham. [aside] Wormwood, wormwood!
2262: 
2263:     Queen. The instances that second marriage move
2264:       Are base respects of thrift, but none of love.
2265:       A second time I kill my husband dead
2266:       When second husband kisses me in bed.
2267:     King. I do believe you think what now you speak;
2268:       But what we do determine oft we break.
2269:       Purpose is but the slave to memory,
2270:       Of violent birth, but poor validity;
2271:       Which now, like fruit unripe, sticks on the tree,  
2272:       But fill unshaken when they mellow be.
2273:       Most necessary 'tis that we forget
2274:       To pay ourselves what to ourselves is debt.
2275:       What to ourselves in passion we propose,
2276:       The passion ending, doth the purpose lose.
2277:       The violence of either grief or joy
2278:       Their own enactures with themselves destroy.
2279:       Where joy most revels, grief doth most lament;
2280:       Grief joys, joy grieves, on slender accident.
2281:       This world is not for aye, nor 'tis not strange
2282:       That even our loves should with our fortunes change;
2283:       For 'tis a question left us yet to prove,
2284:       Whether love lead fortune, or else fortune love.
2285:       The great man down, you mark his favourite flies,
2286:       The poor advanc'd makes friends of enemies;
2287:       And hitherto doth love on fortune tend,
2288:       For who not needs shall never lack a friend,
2289:       And who in want a hollow friend doth try,
2290:       Directly seasons him his enemy.
2291:       But, orderly to end where I begun,  
2292:       Our wills and fates do so contrary run
2293:       That our devices still are overthrown;
2294:       Our thoughts are ours, their ends none of our own.
2295:       So think thou wilt no second husband wed;
2296:       But die thy thoughts when thy first lord is dead.
2297:     Queen. Nor earth to me give food, nor heaven light,
2298:       Sport and repose lock from me day and night,
2299:       To desperation turn my trust and hope,
2300:       An anchor's cheer in prison be my scope,
2301:       Each opposite that blanks the face of joy
2302:       Meet what I would have well, and it destroy,
2303:       Both here and hence pursue me lasting strife,
2304:       If, once a widow, ever I be wife!
2305: 
2306:   Ham. If she should break it now!
2307: 
2308:     King. 'Tis deeply sworn. Sweet, leave me here awhile.
2309:       My spirits grow dull, and fain I would beguile
2310:       The tedious day with sleep.
2311:     Queen. Sleep rock thy brain,  
2312:                                                     [He] sleeps.
2313:       And never come mischance between us twain!
2314: Exit.
2315: 
2316:   Ham. Madam, how like you this play?
2317:   Queen. The lady doth protest too much, methinks.
2318:   Ham. O, but she'll keep her word.
2319:   King. Have you heard the argument? Is there no offence in't?
2320:   Ham. No, no! They do but jest, poison in jest; no offence i' th'
2321:     world.
2322:   King. What do you call the play?
2323:   Ham. 'The Mousetrap.' Marry, how? Tropically. This play is the
2324:     image of a murther done in Vienna. Gonzago is the duke's name;
2325:     his wife, Baptista. You shall see anon. 'Tis a knavish piece of
2326:     work; but what o' that? Your Majesty, and we that have free
2327:     souls, it touches us not. Let the gall'd jade winch; our withers
2328:     are unwrung.
2329: 
2330:                          Enter Lucianus.
2331:   
2332:     This is one Lucianus, nephew to the King.
2333:   Oph. You are as good as a chorus, my lord.
2334:   Ham. I could interpret between you and your love, if I could see
2335:     the puppets dallying.
2336:   Oph. You are keen, my lord, you are keen.
2337:   Ham. It would cost you a groaning to take off my edge.
2338:   Oph. Still better, and worse.
2339:   Ham. So you must take your husbands.- Begin, murtherer. Pox, leave
2340:     thy damnable faces, and begin! Come, the croaking raven doth
2341:     bellow for revenge.
2342: 
2343:     Luc. Thoughts black, hands apt, drugs fit, and time agreeing;
2344:       Confederate season, else no creature seeing;
2345:       Thou mixture rank, of midnight weeds collected,
2346:       With Hecate's ban thrice blasted, thrice infected,
2347:       Thy natural magic and dire property
2348:       On wholesome life usurp immediately.
2349:                                    Pours the poison in his ears.
2350: 
2351:   Ham. He poisons him i' th' garden for's estate. His name's Gonzago.  
2352:     The story is extant, and written in very choice Italian. You
2353:     shall see anon how the murtherer gets the love of Gonzago's wife.
2354:   Oph. The King rises.
2355:   Ham. What, frighted with false fire?
2356:   Queen. How fares my lord?
2357:   Pol. Give o'er the play.
2358:   King. Give me some light! Away!
2359:   All. Lights, lights, lights!
2360:                               Exeunt all but Hamlet and Horatio.
2361:   Ham.   Why, let the strucken deer go weep,
2362:           The hart ungalled play;
2363:          For some must watch, while some must sleep:
2364:           Thus runs the world away.
2365:     Would not this, sir, and a forest of feathers- if the rest of my
2366:     fortunes turn Turk with me-with two Provincial roses on my raz'd
2367:     shoes, get me a fellowship in a cry of players, sir?
2368:   Hor. Half a share.
2369:   Ham.   A whole one I!
2370:          For thou dost know, O Damon dear,
2371:            This realm dismantled was  
2372:          Of Jove himself; and now reigns here
2373:            A very, very- pajock.
2374:   Hor. You might have rhym'd.
2375:   Ham. O good Horatio, I'll take the ghost's word for a thousand
2376:     pound! Didst perceive?
2377:   Hor. Very well, my lord.
2378:   Ham. Upon the talk of the poisoning?
2379:   Hor. I did very well note him.
2380:   Ham.   Aha! Come, some music! Come, the recorders!
2381:          For if the King like not the comedy,
2382:          Why then, belike he likes it not, perdy.
2383:     Come, some music!
2384: 
2385:                 Enter Rosencrantz and Guildenstern.
2386: 
2387:   Guil. Good my lord, vouchsafe me a word with you.
2388:   Ham. Sir, a whole history.
2389:   Guil. The King, sir-
2390:   Ham. Ay, sir, what of him?
2391:   Guil. Is in his retirement, marvellous distemper'd.  
2392:   Ham. With drink, sir?
2393:   Guil. No, my lord; rather with choler.
2394:   Ham. Your wisdom should show itself more richer to signify this to
2395:     the doctor; for me to put him to his purgation would perhaps
2396:     plunge him into far more choler.
2397:   Guil. Good my lord, put your discourse into some frame, and start
2398:     not so wildly from my affair.
2399:   Ham. I am tame, sir; pronounce.
2400:   Guil. The Queen, your mother, in most great affliction of spirit
2401:     hath sent me to you.
2402:   Ham. You are welcome.
2403:   Guil. Nay, good my lord, this courtesy is not of the right breed.
2404:     If it shall please you to make me a wholesome answer, I will do
2405:     your mother's commandment; if not, your pardon and my return
2406:     shall be the end of my business.
2407:   Ham. Sir, I cannot.
2408:   Guil. What, my lord?
2409:   Ham. Make you a wholesome answer; my wit's diseas'd. But, sir, such
2410:     answer is I can make, you shall command; or rather, as you say,
2411:     my mother. Therefore no more, but to the matter! My mother, you  
2412:     say-
2413:   Ros. Then thus she says: your behaviour hath struck her into
2414:     amazement and admiration.
2415:   Ham. O wonderful son, that can so stonish a mother! But is there no
2416:     sequel at the heels of this mother's admiration? Impart.
2417:   Ros. She desires to speak with you in her closet ere you go to bed.
2418:   Ham. We shall obey, were she ten times our mother. Have you any
2419:     further trade with us?
2420:   Ros. My lord, you once did love me.
2421:   Ham. And do still, by these pickers and stealers!
2422:   Ros. Good my lord, what is your cause of distemper? You do surely
2423:     bar the door upon your own liberty, if you deny your griefs to
2424:     your friend.
2425:   Ham. Sir, I lack advancement.
2426:   Ros. How can that be, when you have the voice of the King himself
2427:     for your succession in Denmark?
2428:   Ham. Ay, sir, but 'while the grass grows'- the proverb is something
2429:     musty.
2430: 
2431:                      Enter the Players with recorders.  
2432: 
2433:     O, the recorders! Let me see one. To withdraw with you- why do
2434:     you go about to recover the wind of me, as if you would drive me
2435:     into a toil?
2436:   Guil. O my lord, if my duty be too bold, my love is too unmannerly.
2437:   Ham. I do not well understand that. Will you play upon this pipe?
2438:   Guil. My lord, I cannot.
2439:   Ham. I pray you.
2440:   Guil. Believe me, I cannot.
2441:   Ham. I do beseech you.
2442:   Guil. I know, no touch of it, my lord.
2443:   Ham. It is as easy as lying. Govern these ventages with your
2444:     fingers and thumbs, give it breath with your mouth, and it will
2445:     discourse most eloquent music. Look you, these are the stops.
2446:   Guil. But these cannot I command to any utt'rance of harmony. I
2447:     have not the skill.
2448:   Ham. Why, look you now, how unworthy a thing you make of me! You
2449:     would play upon me; you would seem to know my stops; you would
2450:     pluck out the heart of my mystery; you would sound me from my
2451:     lowest note to the top of my compass; and there is much music,  
2452:     excellent voice, in this little organ, yet cannot you make it
2453:     speak. 'Sblood, do you think I am easier to be play'd on than a
2454:     pipe? Call me what instrument you will, though you can fret me,
2455:     you cannot play upon me.
2456: 
2457:                         Enter Polonius.
2458: 
2459:     God bless you, sir!
2460:   Pol. My lord, the Queen would speak with you, and presently.
2461:   Ham. Do you see yonder cloud that's almost in shape of a camel?
2462:   Pol. By th' mass, and 'tis like a camel indeed.
2463:   Ham. Methinks it is like a weasel.
2464:   Pol. It is back'd like a weasel.
2465:   Ham. Or like a whale.
2466:   Pol. Very like a whale.
2467:   Ham. Then will I come to my mother by-and-by.- They fool me to the
2468:     top of my bent.- I will come by-and-by.
2469:   Pol. I will say so.                                      Exit.
2470:   Ham. 'By-and-by' is easily said.- Leave me, friends.
2471:                                         [Exeunt all but Hamlet.]  
2472:     'Tis now the very witching time of night,
2473:     When churchyards yawn, and hell itself breathes out
2474:     Contagion to this world. Now could I drink hot blood
2475:     And do such bitter business as the day
2476:     Would quake to look on. Soft! now to my mother!
2477:     O heart, lose not thy nature; let not ever
2478:     The soul of Nero enter this firm bosom.
2479:     Let me be cruel, not unnatural;
2480:     I will speak daggers to her, but use none.
2481:     My tongue and soul in this be hypocrites-
2482:     How in my words somever she be shent,
2483:     To give them seals never, my soul, consent!             Exit.
2484: 
2485: 
2486: 
2487: 
2488: Scene III.
2489: A room in the Castle.
2490: 
2491: Enter King, Rosencrantz, and Guildenstern.
2492: 
2493:   King. I like him not, nor stands it safe with us
2494:     To let his madness range. Therefore prepare you;
2495:     I your commission will forthwith dispatch,
2496:     And he to England shall along with you.
2497:     The terms of our estate may not endure
2498:     Hazard so near us as doth hourly grow
2499:     Out of his lunacies.
2500:   Guil. We will ourselves provide.
2501:     Most holy and religious fear it is
2502:     To keep those many many bodies safe
2503:     That live and feed upon your Majesty.
2504:   Ros. The single and peculiar life is bound
2505:     With all the strength and armour of the mind
2506:     To keep itself from noyance; but much more
2507:     That spirit upon whose weal depends and rests
2508:     The lives of many. The cesse of majesty
2509:     Dies not alone, but like a gulf doth draw  
2510:     What's near it with it. It is a massy wheel,
2511:     Fix'd on the summit of the highest mount,
2512:     To whose huge spokes ten thousand lesser things
2513:     Are mortis'd and adjoin'd; which when it falls,
2514:     Each small annexment, petty consequence,
2515:     Attends the boist'rous ruin. Never alone
2516:     Did the king sigh, but with a general groan.
2517:   King. Arm you, I pray you, to th', speedy voyage;
2518:     For we will fetters put upon this fear,
2519:     Which now goes too free-footed.
2520:   Both. We will haste us.
2521:                                                Exeunt Gentlemen.
2522: 
2523:                    Enter Polonius.
2524: 
2525:   Pol. My lord, he's going to his mother's closet.
2526:     Behind the arras I'll convey myself
2527:     To hear the process. I'll warrant she'll tax him home;
2528:     And, as you said, and wisely was it said,
2529:     'Tis meet that some more audience than a mother,  
2530:     Since nature makes them partial, should o'erhear
2531:     The speech, of vantage. Fare you well, my liege.
2532:     I'll call upon you ere you go to bed
2533:     And tell you what I know.
2534:   King. Thanks, dear my lord.
2535:                                                 Exit [Polonius].
2536:     O, my offence is rank, it smells to heaven;
2537:     It hath the primal eldest curse upon't,
2538:     A brother's murther! Pray can I not,
2539:     Though inclination be as sharp as will.
2540:     My stronger guilt defeats my strong intent,
2541:     And, like a man to double business bound,
2542:     I stand in pause where I shall first begin,
2543:     And both neglect. What if this cursed hand
2544:     Were thicker than itself with brother's blood,
2545:     Is there not rain enough in the sweet heavens
2546:     To wash it white as snow? Whereto serves mercy
2547:     But to confront the visage of offence?
2548:     And what's in prayer but this twofold force,
2549:     To be forestalled ere we come to fall,  
2550:     Or pardon'd being down? Then I'll look up;
2551:     My fault is past. But, O, what form of prayer
2552:     Can serve my turn? 'Forgive me my foul murther'?
2553:     That cannot be; since I am still possess'd
2554:     Of those effects for which I did the murther-
2555:     My crown, mine own ambition, and my queen.
2556:     May one be pardon'd and retain th' offence?
2557:     In the corrupted currents of this world
2558:     Offence's gilded hand may shove by justice,
2559:     And oft 'tis seen the wicked prize itself
2560:     Buys out the law; but 'tis not so above.
2561:     There is no shuffling; there the action lies
2562:     In his true nature, and we ourselves compell'd,
2563:     Even to the teeth and forehead of our faults,
2564:     To give in evidence. What then? What rests?
2565:     Try what repentance can. What can it not?
2566:     Yet what can it when one cannot repent?
2567:     O wretched state! O bosom black as death!
2568:     O limed soul, that, struggling to be free,
2569:     Art more engag'd! Help, angels! Make assay.  
2570:     Bow, stubborn knees; and heart with strings of steel,
2571:     Be soft as sinews of the new-born babe!
2572:     All may be well.                                  He kneels.
2573: 
2574:                          Enter Hamlet.
2575: 
2576:   Ham. Now might I do it pat, now he is praying;
2577:     And now I'll do't. And so he goes to heaven,
2578:     And so am I reveng'd. That would be scann'd.
2579:     A villain kills my father; and for that,
2580:     I, his sole son, do this same villain send
2581:     To heaven.
2582:     Why, this is hire and salary, not revenge!
2583:     He took my father grossly, full of bread,
2584:     With all his crimes broad blown, as flush as May;
2585:     And how his audit stands, who knows save heaven?
2586:     But in our circumstance and course of thought,
2587:     'Tis heavy with him; and am I then reveng'd,
2588:     To take him in the purging of his soul,
2589:     When he is fit and seasoned for his passage?  
2590:     No.
2591:     Up, sword, and know thou a more horrid hent.
2592:     When he is drunk asleep; or in his rage;
2593:     Or in th' incestuous pleasure of his bed;
2594:     At gaming, swearing, or about some act
2595:     That has no relish of salvation in't-
2596:     Then trip him, that his heels may kick at heaven,
2597:     And that his soul may be as damn'd and black
2598:     As hell, whereto it goes. My mother stays.
2599:     This physic but prolongs thy sickly days.              Exit.
2600:   King. [rises] My words fly up, my thoughts remain below.
2601:     Words without thoughts never to heaven go.             Exit.
2602: 
2603: 
2604: 
2605: 
2606: Scene IV.
2607: The Queen's closet.
2608: 
2609: Enter Queen and Polonius.
2610: 
2611:   Pol. He will come straight. Look you lay home to him.
2612:     Tell him his pranks have been too broad to bear with,
2613:     And that your Grace hath screen'd and stood between
2614:     Much heat and him. I'll silence me even here.
2615:     Pray you be round with him.
2616:   Ham. (within) Mother, mother, mother!
2617:   Queen. I'll warrant you; fear me not. Withdraw; I hear him coming.
2618:                               [Polonius hides behind the arras.]
2619: 
2620:                           Enter Hamlet.
2621: 
2622:   Ham. Now, mother, what's the matter?
2623:   Queen. Hamlet, thou hast thy father much offended.
2624:   Ham. Mother, you have my father much offended.
2625:   Queen. Come, come, you answer with an idle tongue.
2626:   Ham. Go, go, you question with a wicked tongue.
2627:   Queen. Why, how now, Hamlet?  
2628:   Ham. What's the matter now?
2629:   Queen. Have you forgot me?
2630:   Ham. No, by the rood, not so!
2631:     You are the Queen, your husband's brother's wife,
2632:     And (would it were not so!) you are my mother.
2633:   Queen. Nay, then I'll set those to you that can speak.
2634:   Ham. Come, come, and sit you down. You shall not budge I
2635:     You go not till I set you up a glass
2636:     Where you may see the inmost part of you.
2637:   Queen. What wilt thou do? Thou wilt not murther me?
2638:     Help, help, ho!
2639:   Pol. [behind] What, ho! help, help, help!
2640:   Ham. [draws] How now? a rat? Dead for a ducat, dead!
2641:             [Makes a pass through the arras and] kills Polonius.
2642:   Pol. [behind] O, I am slain!
2643:   Queen. O me, what hast thou done?
2644:   Ham. Nay, I know not. Is it the King?
2645:   Queen. O, what a rash and bloody deed is this!
2646:   Ham. A bloody deed- almost as bad, good mother,
2647:     As kill a king, and marry with his brother.  
2648:   Queen. As kill a king?
2649:   Ham. Ay, lady, it was my word.
2650:                          [Lifts up the arras and sees Polonius.]
2651:     Thou wretched, rash, intruding fool, farewell!
2652:     I took thee for thy better. Take thy fortune.
2653:     Thou find'st to be too busy is some danger.
2654:     Leave wringing of your hinds. Peace! sit you down
2655:     And let me wring your heart; for so I shall
2656:     If it be made of penetrable stuff;
2657:     If damned custom have not braz'd it so
2658:     That it is proof and bulwark against sense.
2659:   Queen. What have I done that thou dar'st wag thy tongue
2660:     In noise so rude against me?
2661:   Ham. Such an act
2662:     That blurs the grace and blush of modesty;
2663:     Calls virtue hypocrite; takes off the rose
2664:     From the fair forehead of an innocent love,
2665:     And sets a blister there; makes marriage vows
2666:     As false as dicers' oaths. O, such a deed
2667:     As from the body of contraction plucks  
2668:     The very soul, and sweet religion makes
2669:     A rhapsody of words! Heaven's face doth glow;
2670:     Yea, this solidity and compound mass,
2671:     With tristful visage, as against the doom,
2672:     Is thought-sick at the act.
2673:   Queen. Ay me, what act,
2674:     That roars so loud and thunders in the index?
2675:   Ham. Look here upon th's picture, and on this,
2676:     The counterfeit presentment of two brothers.
2677:     See what a grace was seated on this brow;
2678:     Hyperion's curls; the front of Jove himself;
2679:     An eye like Mars, to threaten and command;
2680:     A station like the herald Mercury
2681:     New lighted on a heaven-kissing hill:
2682:     A combination and a form indeed
2683:     Where every god did seem to set his seal
2684:     To give the world assurance of a man.
2685:     This was your husband. Look you now what follows.
2686:     Here is your husband, like a mildew'd ear
2687:     Blasting his wholesome brother. Have you eyes?  
2688:     Could you on this fair mountain leave to feed,
2689:     And batten on this moor? Ha! have you eyes
2690:     You cannot call it love; for at your age
2691:     The heyday in the blood is tame, it's humble,
2692:     And waits upon the judgment; and what judgment
2693:     Would step from this to this? Sense sure you have,
2694:     Else could you not have motion; but sure that sense
2695:     Is apoplex'd; for madness would not err,
2696:     Nor sense to ecstacy was ne'er so thrall'd
2697:     But it reserv'd some quantity of choice
2698:     To serve in such a difference. What devil was't
2699:     That thus hath cozen'd you at hoodman-blind?
2700:     Eyes without feeling, feeling without sight,
2701:     Ears without hands or eyes, smelling sans all,
2702:     Or but a sickly part of one true sense
2703:     Could not so mope.
2704:     O shame! where is thy blush? Rebellious hell,
2705:     If thou canst mutine in a matron's bones,
2706:     To flaming youth let virtue be as wax
2707:     And melt in her own fire. Proclaim no shame  
2708:     When the compulsive ardour gives the charge,
2709:     Since frost itself as actively doth burn,
2710:     And reason panders will.
2711:   Queen. O Hamlet, speak no more!
2712:     Thou turn'st mine eyes into my very soul,
2713:     And there I see such black and grained spots
2714:     As will not leave their tinct.
2715:   Ham. Nay, but to live
2716:     In the rank sweat of an enseamed bed,
2717:     Stew'd in corruption, honeying and making love
2718:     Over the nasty sty!
2719:   Queen. O, speak to me no more!
2720:     These words like daggers enter in mine ears.
2721:     No more, sweet Hamlet!
2722:   Ham. A murtherer and a villain!
2723:     A slave that is not twentieth part the tithe
2724:     Of your precedent lord; a vice of kings;
2725:     A cutpurse of the empire and the rule,
2726:     That from a shelf the precious diadem stole
2727:     And put it in his pocket!  
2728:   Queen. No more!
2729: 
2730:                 Enter the Ghost in his nightgown.
2731: 
2732:   Ham. A king of shreds and patches!-
2733:     Save me and hover o'er me with your wings,
2734:     You heavenly guards! What would your gracious figure?
2735:   Queen. Alas, he's mad!
2736:   Ham. Do you not come your tardy son to chide,
2737:     That, laps'd in time and passion, lets go by
2738:     Th' important acting of your dread command?
2739:     O, say!
2740:   Ghost. Do not forget. This visitation
2741:     Is but to whet thy almost blunted purpose.
2742:     But look, amazement on thy mother sits.
2743:     O, step between her and her fighting soul
2744:     Conceit in weakest bodies strongest works.
2745:     Speak to her, Hamlet.
2746:   Ham. How is it with you, lady?
2747:   Queen. Alas, how is't with you,  
2748:     That you do bend your eye on vacancy,
2749:     And with th' encorporal air do hold discourse?
2750:     Forth at your eyes your spirits wildly peep;
2751:     And, as the sleeping soldiers in th' alarm,
2752:     Your bedded hairs, like life in excrements,
2753:     Start up and stand an end. O gentle son,
2754:     Upon the beat and flame of thy distemper
2755:     Sprinkle cool patience! Whereon do you look?
2756:   Ham. On him, on him! Look you how pale he glares!
2757:     His form and cause conjoin'd, preaching to stones,
2758:     Would make them capable.- Do not look upon me,
2759:     Lest with this piteous action you convert
2760:     My stern effects. Then what I have to do
2761:     Will want true colour- tears perchance for blood.
2762:   Queen. To whom do you speak this?
2763:   Ham. Do you see nothing there?
2764:   Queen. Nothing at all; yet all that is I see.
2765:   Ham. Nor did you nothing hear?
2766:   Queen. No, nothing but ourselves.
2767:   Ham. Why, look you there! Look how it steals away!  
2768:     My father, in his habit as he liv'd!
2769:     Look where he goes even now out at the portal!
2770:                                                      Exit Ghost.
2771:   Queen. This is the very coinage of your brain.
2772:     This bodiless creation ecstasy
2773:     Is very cunning in.
2774:   Ham. Ecstasy?
2775:     My pulse as yours doth temperately keep time
2776:     And makes as healthful music. It is not madness
2777:     That I have utt'red. Bring me to the test,
2778:     And I the matter will reword; which madness
2779:     Would gambol from. Mother, for love of grace,
2780:     Lay not that flattering unction to your soul
2781:     That not your trespass but my madness speaks.
2782:     It will but skin and film the ulcerous place,
2783:     Whiles rank corruption, mining all within,
2784:     Infects unseen. Confess yourself to heaven;
2785:     Repent what's past; avoid what is to come;
2786:     And do not spread the compost on the weeds
2787:     To make them ranker. Forgive me this my virtue;  
2788:     For in the fatness of these pursy times
2789:     Virtue itself of vice must pardon beg-
2790:     Yea, curb and woo for leave to do him good.
2791:   Queen. O Hamlet, thou hast cleft my heart in twain.
2792:   Ham. O, throw away the worser part of it,
2793:     And live the purer with the other half,
2794:     Good night- but go not to my uncle's bed.
2795:     Assume a virtue, if you have it not.
2796:     That monster, custom, who all sense doth eat
2797:     Of habits evil, is angel yet in this,
2798:     That to the use of actions fair and good
2799:     He likewise gives a frock or livery,
2800:     That aptly is put on. Refrain to-night,
2801:     And that shall lend a kind of easiness
2802:     To the next abstinence; the next more easy;
2803:     For use almost can change the stamp of nature,
2804:     And either [master] the devil, or throw him out
2805:     With wondrous potency. Once more, good night;
2806:     And when you are desirous to be blest,
2807:     I'll blessing beg of you.- For this same lord,  
2808:     I do repent; but heaven hath pleas'd it so,
2809:     To punish me with this, and this with me,
2810:     That I must be their scourge and minister.
2811:     I will bestow him, and will answer well
2812:     The death I gave him. So again, good night.
2813:     I must be cruel, only to be kind;
2814:     Thus bad begins, and worse remains behind.
2815:     One word more, good lady.
2816:   Queen. What shall I do?
2817:   Ham. Not this, by no means, that I bid you do:
2818:     Let the bloat King tempt you again to bed;
2819:     Pinch wanton on your cheek; call you his mouse;
2820:     And let him, for a pair of reechy kisses,
2821:     Or paddling in your neck with his damn'd fingers,
2822:     Make you to ravel all this matter out,
2823:     That I essentially am not in madness,
2824:     But mad in craft. 'Twere good you let him know;
2825:     For who that's but a queen, fair, sober, wise,
2826:     Would from a paddock, from a bat, a gib
2827:     Such dear concernings hide? Who would do so?  
2828:     No, in despite of sense and secrecy,
2829:     Unpeg the basket on the house's top,
2830:     Let the birds fly, and like the famous ape,
2831:     To try conclusions, in the basket creep
2832:     And break your own neck down.
2833:   Queen. Be thou assur'd, if words be made of breath,
2834:     And breath of life, I have no life to breathe
2835:     What thou hast said to me.
2836:   Ham. I must to England; you know that?
2837:   Queen. Alack,
2838:     I had forgot! 'Tis so concluded on.
2839:   Ham. There's letters seal'd; and my two schoolfellows,
2840:     Whom I will trust as I will adders fang'd,
2841:     They bear the mandate; they must sweep my way
2842:     And marshal me to knavery. Let it work;
2843:     For 'tis the sport to have the enginer
2844:     Hoist with his own petar; and 't shall go hard
2845:     But I will delve one yard below their mines
2846:     And blow them at the moon. O, 'tis most sweet
2847:     When in one line two crafts directly meet.  
2848:     This man shall set me packing.
2849:     I'll lug the guts into the neighbour room.-
2850:     Mother, good night.- Indeed, this counsellor
2851:     Is now most still, most secret, and most grave,
2852:     Who was in life a foolish peating knave.
2853:     Come, sir, to draw toward an end with you.
2854:     Good night, mother.
2855:                   [Exit the Queen. Then] Exit Hamlet, tugging in
2856:                                                        Polonius.

2862: ACT IV. Scene I.
2863: Elsinore. A room in the Castle.
2864: 
2865: Enter King and Queen, with Rosencrantz and Guildenstern.
2866: 
2867:   King. There's matter in these sighs. These profound heaves
2868:     You must translate; 'tis fit we understand them.
2869:     Where is your son?
2870:   Queen. Bestow this place on us a little while.
2871:                           [Exeunt Rosencrantz and Guildenstern.]
2872:     Ah, mine own lord, what have I seen to-night!
2873:   King. What, Gertrude? How does Hamlet?
2874:   Queen. Mad as the sea and wind when both contend
2875:     Which is the mightier. In his lawless fit
2876:     Behind the arras hearing something stir,
2877:     Whips out his rapier, cries 'A rat, a rat!'
2878:     And in this brainish apprehension kills
2879:     The unseen good old man.
2880:   King. O heavy deed!
2881:     It had been so with us, had we been there.
2882:     His liberty is full of threats to all-
2883:     To you yourself, to us, to every one.  
2884:     Alas, how shall this bloody deed be answer'd?
2885:     It will be laid to us, whose providence
2886:     Should have kept short, restrain'd, and out of haunt
2887:     This mad young man. But so much was our love
2888:     We would not understand what was most fit,
2889:     But, like the owner of a foul disease,
2890:     To keep it from divulging, let it feed
2891:     Even on the pith of life. Where is he gone?
2892:   Queen. To draw apart the body he hath kill'd;
2893:     O'er whom his very madness, like some ore
2894:     Among a mineral of metals base,
2895:     Shows itself pure. He weeps for what is done.
2896:   King. O Gertrude, come away!
2897:     The sun no sooner shall the mountains touch
2898:     But we will ship him hence; and this vile deed
2899:     We must with all our majesty and skill
2900:     Both countenance and excuse. Ho, Guildenstern!
2901: 
2902:              Enter Rosencrantz and Guildenstern.
2903:   
2904:     Friends both, go join you with some further aid.
2905:     Hamlet in madness hath Polonius slain,
2906:     And from his mother's closet hath he dragg'd him.
2907:     Go seek him out; speak fair, and bring the body
2908:     Into the chapel. I pray you haste in this.
2909:                           Exeunt [Rosencrantz and Guildenstern].
2910:     Come, Gertrude, we'll call up our wisest friends
2911:     And let them know both what we mean to do
2912:     And what's untimely done. [So haply slander-]
2913:     Whose whisper o'er the world's diameter,
2914:     As level as the cannon to his blank,
2915:     Transports his poisoned shot- may miss our name
2916:     And hit the woundless air.- O, come away!
2917:     My soul is full of discord and dismay.
2918:                                                          Exeunt.

2923: Scene II.
2924: Elsinore. A passage in the Castle.
2925: 
2926: Enter Hamlet.
2927: 
2928:   Ham. Safely stow'd.
2929:   Gentlemen. (within) Hamlet! Lord Hamlet!
2930:   Ham. But soft! What noise? Who calls on Hamlet? O, here they come.
2931: 
2932:                Enter Rosencrantz and Guildenstern.
2933: 
2934:   Ros. What have you done, my lord, with the dead body?
2935:   Ham. Compounded it with dust, whereto 'tis kin.
2936:   Ros. Tell us where 'tis, that we may take it thence
2937:     And bear it to the chapel.
2938:   Ham. Do not believe it.
2939:   Ros. Believe what?
2940:   Ham. That I can keep your counsel, and not mine own. Besides, to be
2941:     demanded of a sponge, what replication should be made by the son
2942:     of a king?
2943:   Ros. Take you me for a sponge, my lord?
2944:   Ham. Ay, sir; that soaks up the King's countenance, his rewards,  
2945:     his authorities. But such officers do the King best service in
2946:     the end. He keeps them, like an ape, in the corner of his jaw;
2947:     first mouth'd, to be last Swallowed. When he needs what you have
2948:     glean'd, it is but squeezing you and, sponge, you shall be dry
2949:     again.
2950:   Ros. I understand you not, my lord.
2951:   Ham. I am glad of it. A knavish speech sleeps in a foolish ear.
2952:   Ros. My lord, you must tell us where the body is and go with us to
2953:     the King.
2954:   Ham. The body is with the King, but the King is not with the body.
2955:     The King is a thing-
2956:   Guil. A thing, my lord?
2957:   Ham. Of nothing. Bring me to him. Hide fox, and all after.
2958:                                                          Exeunt.
2959: 
2960: 
2961: 
2962: 
2963: Scene III.
2964: Elsinore. A room in the Castle.
2965: 
2966: Enter King.
2967: 
2968:   King. I have sent to seek him and to find the body.
2969:     How dangerous is it that this man goes loose!
2970:     Yet must not we put the strong law on him.
2971:     He's lov'd of the distracted multitude,
2972:     Who like not in their judgment, but their eyes;
2973:     And where 'tis so, th' offender's scourge is weigh'd,
2974:     But never the offence. To bear all smooth and even,
2975:     This sudden sending him away must seem
2976:     Deliberate pause. Diseases desperate grown
2977:     By desperate appliance are reliev'd,
2978:     Or not at all.
2979: 
2980:                     Enter Rosencrantz.
2981: 
2982:     How now O What hath befall'n?
2983:   Ros. Where the dead body is bestow'd, my lord,
2984:     We cannot get from him.  
2985:   King. But where is he?
2986:   Ros. Without, my lord; guarded, to know your pleasure.
2987:   King. Bring him before us.
2988:   Ros. Ho, Guildenstern! Bring in my lord.
2989: 
2990:         Enter Hamlet and Guildenstern [with Attendants].
2991: 
2992:   King. Now, Hamlet, where's Polonius?
2993:   Ham. At supper.
2994:   King. At supper? Where?
2995:   Ham. Not where he eats, but where he is eaten. A certain
2996:     convocation of politic worms are e'en at him. Your worm is your
2997:     only emperor for diet. We fat all creatures else to fat us, and
2998:     we fat ourselves for maggots. Your fat king and your lean beggar
2999:     is but variable service- two dishes, but to one table. That's the
3000:     end.
3001:   King. Alas, alas!
3002:   Ham. A man may fish with the worm that hath eat of a king, and eat
3003:     of the fish that hath fed of that worm.
3004:   King. What dost thou mean by this?  
3005:   Ham. Nothing but to show you how a king may go a progress through
3006:     the guts of a beggar.
3007:   King. Where is Polonius?
3008:   Ham. In heaven. Send thither to see. If your messenger find him not
3009:     there, seek him i' th' other place yourself. But indeed, if you
3010:     find him not within this month, you shall nose him as you go up
3011:     the stair, into the lobby.
3012:   King. Go seek him there. [To Attendants.]
3013:   Ham. He will stay till you come.
3014:                                             [Exeunt Attendants.]
3015:   King. Hamlet, this deed, for thine especial safety,-
3016:     Which we do tender as we dearly grieve
3017:     For that which thou hast done,- must send thee hence
3018:     With fiery quickness. Therefore prepare thyself.
3019:     The bark is ready and the wind at help,
3020:     Th' associates tend, and everything is bent
3021:     For England.
3022:   Ham. For England?
3023:   King. Ay, Hamlet.
3024:   Ham. Good.  
3025:   King. So is it, if thou knew'st our purposes.
3026:   Ham. I see a cherub that sees them. But come, for England!
3027:     Farewell, dear mother.
3028:   King. Thy loving father, Hamlet.
3029:   Ham. My mother! Father and mother is man and wife; man and wife is
3030:     one flesh; and so, my mother. Come, for England!
3031: Exit.
3032:   King. Follow him at foot; tempt him with speed aboard.
3033:     Delay it not; I'll have him hence to-night.
3034:     Away! for everything is seal'd and done
3035:     That else leans on th' affair. Pray you make haste.
3036:                             Exeunt Rosencrantz and Guildenstern]
3037:     And, England, if my love thou hold'st at aught,-
3038:     As my great power thereof may give thee sense,
3039:     Since yet thy cicatrice looks raw and red
3040:     After the Danish sword, and thy free awe
3041:     Pays homage to us,- thou mayst not coldly set
3042:     Our sovereign process, which imports at full,
3043:     By letters congruing to that effect,
3044:     The present death of Hamlet. Do it, England;  
3045:     For like the hectic in my blood he rages,
3046:     And thou must cure me. Till I know 'tis done,
3047:     Howe'er my haps, my joys were ne'er begun.             Exit.
3048: 
3049: 
3050:

3053: Scene IV.
3054: Near Elsinore.
3055: 
3056: Enter Fortinbras with his Army over the stage.
3057: 
3058:   For. Go, Captain, from me greet the Danish king.
3059:     Tell him that by his license Fortinbras
3060:     Craves the conveyance of a promis'd march
3061:     Over his kingdom. You know the rendezvous.
3062:     if that his Majesty would aught with us,
3063:     We shall express our duty in his eye;
3064:     And let him know so.
3065:   Capt. I will do't, my lord.
3066:   For. Go softly on.
3067:                                    Exeunt [all but the Captain].
3068: 
3069:        Enter Hamlet, Rosencrantz, [Guildenstern,] and others.
3070: 
3071:   Ham. Good sir, whose powers are these?
3072:   Capt. They are of Norway, sir.
3073:   Ham. How purpos'd, sir, I pray you?
3074:   Capt. Against some part of Poland.  
3075:   Ham. Who commands them, sir?
3076:   Capt. The nephew to old Norway, Fortinbras.
3077:   Ham. Goes it against the main of Poland, sir,
3078:     Or for some frontier?
3079:   Capt. Truly to speak, and with no addition,
3080:     We go to gain a little patch of ground
3081:     That hath in it no profit but the name.
3082:     To pay five ducats, five, I would not farm it;
3083:     Nor will it yield to Norway or the Pole
3084:     A ranker rate, should it be sold in fee.
3085:   Ham. Why, then the Polack never will defend it.
3086:   Capt. Yes, it is already garrison'd.
3087:   Ham. Two thousand souls and twenty thousand ducats
3088:     Will not debate the question of this straw.
3089:     This is th' imposthume of much wealth and peace,
3090:     That inward breaks, and shows no cause without
3091:     Why the man dies.- I humbly thank you, sir.
3092:   Capt. God b' wi' you, sir.                             [Exit.]
3093:   Ros. Will't please you go, my lord?
3094:   Ham. I'll be with you straight. Go a little before.  
3095:                                         [Exeunt all but Hamlet.]
3096:     How all occasions do inform against me
3097:     And spur my dull revenge! What is a man,
3098:     If his chief good and market of his time
3099:     Be but to sleep and feed? A beast, no more.
3100:     Sure he that made us with such large discourse,
3101:     Looking before and after, gave us not
3102:     That capability and godlike reason
3103:     To fust in us unus'd. Now, whether it be
3104:     Bestial oblivion, or some craven scruple
3105:     Of thinking too precisely on th' event,-
3106:     A thought which, quarter'd, hath but one part wisdom
3107:     And ever three parts coward,- I do not know
3108:     Why yet I live to say 'This thing's to do,'
3109:     Sith I have cause, and will, and strength, and means
3110:     To do't. Examples gross as earth exhort me.
3111:     Witness this army of such mass and charge,
3112:     Led by a delicate and tender prince,
3113:     Whose spirit, with divine ambition puff'd,
3114:     Makes mouths at the invisible event,  
3115:     Exposing what is mortal and unsure
3116:     To all that fortune, death, and danger dare,
3117:     Even for an eggshell. Rightly to be great
3118:     Is not to stir without great argument,
3119:     But greatly to find quarrel in a straw
3120:     When honour's at the stake. How stand I then,
3121:     That have a father klll'd, a mother stain'd,
3122:     Excitements of my reason and my blood,
3123:     And let all sleep, while to my shame I see
3124:     The imminent death of twenty thousand men
3125:     That for a fantasy and trick of fame
3126:     Go to their graves like beds, fight for a plot
3127:     Whereon the numbers cannot try the cause,
3128:     Which is not tomb enough and continent
3129:     To hide the slain? O, from this time forth,
3130:     My thoughts be bloody, or be nothing worth!            Exit.
