3136: Scene V.
3137: Elsinore. A room in the Castle.
3138: 
3139: Enter Horatio, Queen, and a Gentleman.
3140: 
3141:   Queen. I will not speak with her.
3142:   Gent. She is importunate, indeed distract.
3143:     Her mood will needs be pitied.
3144:   Queen. What would she have?
3145:   Gent. She speaks much of her father; says she hears
3146:     There's tricks i' th' world, and hems, and beats her heart;
3147:     Spurns enviously at straws; speaks things in doubt,
3148:     That carry but half sense. Her speech is nothing,
3149:     Yet the unshaped use of it doth move
3150:     The hearers to collection; they aim at it,
3151:     And botch the words up fit to their own thoughts;
3152:     Which, as her winks and nods and gestures yield them,
3153:     Indeed would make one think there might be thought,
3154:     Though nothing sure, yet much unhappily.
3155:   Hor. 'Twere good she were spoken with; for she may strew
3156:     Dangerous conjectures in ill-breeding minds.
3157:   Queen. Let her come in.  
3158:                                                [Exit Gentleman.]
3159:     [Aside] To my sick soul (as sin's true nature is)
3160:     Each toy seems Prologue to some great amiss.
3161:     So full of artless jealousy is guilt
3162:     It spills itself in fearing to be spilt.
3163: 
3164:                  Enter Ophelia distracted.
3165: 
3166:   Oph. Where is the beauteous Majesty of Denmark?
3167:   Queen. How now, Ophelia?
3168:   Oph. (sings)
3169:          How should I your true-love know
3170:            From another one?
3171:          By his cockle bat and' staff
3172:            And his sandal shoon.
3173: 
3174:   Queen. Alas, sweet lady, what imports this song?
3175:   Oph. Say you? Nay, pray You mark.
3176: 
3177:     (Sings) He is dead and gone, lady,  
3178:               He is dead and gone;
3179:             At his head a grass-green turf,
3180:               At his heels a stone.
3181: 
3182:     O, ho!
3183:   Queen. Nay, but Ophelia-
3184:   Oph. Pray you mark.
3185: 
3186:     (Sings) White his shroud as the mountain snow-
3187: 
3188:                     Enter King.
3189: 
3190:   Queen. Alas, look here, my lord!
3191:   Oph. (Sings)
3192:            Larded all with sweet flowers;
3193:          Which bewept to the grave did not go
3194:            With true-love showers.
3195: 
3196:   King. How do you, pretty lady?
3197:   Oph. Well, God dild you! They say the owl was a baker's daughter.  
3198:     Lord, we know what we are, but know not what we may be. God be at
3199:     your table!
3200:   King. Conceit upon her father.
3201:   Oph. Pray let's have no words of this; but when they ask, you what
3202:     it means, say you this:
3203: 
3204:     (Sings) To-morrow is Saint Valentine's day,
3205:               All in the morning bedtime,
3206:             And I a maid at your window,
3207:               To be your Valentine.
3208: 
3209:             Then up he rose and donn'd his clo'es
3210:               And dupp'd the chamber door,
3211:             Let in the maid, that out a maid
3212:               Never departed more.
3213: 
3214:   King. Pretty Ophelia!
3215:   Oph. Indeed, la, without an oath, I'll make an end on't!
3216: 
3217:     [Sings] By Gis and by Saint Charity,  
3218:               Alack, and fie for shame!
3219:             Young men will do't if they come to't
3220:               By Cock, they are to blame.
3221: 
3222:             Quoth she, 'Before you tumbled me,
3223:               You promis'd me to wed.'
3224: 
3225:     He answers:
3226: 
3227:             'So would I 'a' done, by yonder sun,
3228:               An thou hadst not come to my bed.'
3229: 
3230:   King. How long hath she been thus?
3231:   Oph. I hope all will be well. We must be patient; but I cannot
3232:     choose but weep to think they would lay him i' th' cold ground.
3233:     My brother shall know of it; and so I thank you for your good
3234:     counsel. Come, my coach! Good night, ladies. Good night, sweet
3235:     ladies. Good night, good night.                         Exit
3236:   King. Follow her close; give her good watch, I pray you.
3237:                                                  [Exit Horatio.]  
3238:     O, this is the poison of deep grief; it springs
3239:     All from her father's death. O Gertrude, Gertrude,
3240:     When sorrows come, they come not single spies.
3241:     But in battalions! First, her father slain;
3242:     Next, Your son gone, and he most violent author
3243:     Of his own just remove; the people muddied,
3244:     Thick and and unwholesome in their thoughts and whispers
3245:     For good Polonius' death, and we have done but greenly
3246:     In hugger-mugger to inter him; Poor Ophelia
3247:     Divided from herself and her fair-judgment,
3248:     Without the which we are Pictures or mere beasts;
3249:     Last, and as such containing as all these,
3250:     Her brother is in secret come from France;
3251:     And wants not buzzers to infect his ear
3252:     Feeds on his wonder, keep, himself in clouds,
3253:     With pestilent speeches of his father's death,
3254:     Wherein necessity, of matter beggar'd,
3255:     Will nothing stick Our person to arraign
3256:     In ear and ear. O my dear Gertrude, this,
3257:     Like to a murd'ring piece, in many places  
3258:     Give, me superfluous death.                  A noise within.
3259:   Queen. Alack, what noise is this?
3260:   King. Where are my Switzers? Let them guard the door.
3261: 
3262:                      Enter a Messenger.
3263: 
3264:     What is the matter?
3265:   Mess. Save Yourself, my lord:
3266:     The ocean, overpeering of his list,
3267:     Eats not the flats with more impetuous haste
3268:     Than Young Laertes, in a riotous head,
3269:     O'erbears Your offices. The rabble call him lord;
3270:     And, as the world were now but to begin,
3271:     Antiquity forgot, custom not known,
3272:     The ratifiers and props of every word,
3273:     They cry 'Choose we! Laertes shall be king!'
3274:     Caps, hands, and tongues applaud it to the clouds,
3275:     'Laertes shall be king! Laertes king!'
3276:                                                  A noise within.
3277:   Queen. How cheerfully on the false trail they cry!  
3278:     O, this is counter, you false Danish dogs!
3279:   King. The doors are broke.
3280: 
3281:                     Enter Laertes with others.
3282: 
3283:   Laer. Where is this king?- Sirs, staid you all without.
3284:   All. No, let's come in!
3285:   Laer. I pray you give me leave.
3286:   All. We will, we will!
3287:   Laer. I thank you. Keep the door.      [Exeunt his Followers.]
3288:     O thou vile king,
3289:     Give me my father!
3290:   Queen. Calmly, good Laertes.
3291:   Laer. That drop of blood that's calm proclaims me bastard;
3292:     Cries cuckold to my father; brands the harlot
3293:     Even here between the chaste unsmirched brows
3294:     Of my true mother.
3295:   King. What is the cause, Laertes,
3296:     That thy rebellion looks so giantlike?
3297:     Let him go, Gertrude. Do not fear our person.  
3298:     There's such divinity doth hedge a king
3299:     That treason can but peep to what it would,
3300:     Acts little of his will. Tell me, Laertes,
3301:     Why thou art thus incens'd. Let him go, Gertrude.
3302:     Speak, man.
3303:   Laer. Where is my father?
3304:   King. Dead.
3305:   Queen. But not by him!
3306:   King. Let him demand his fill.
3307:   Laer. How came he dead? I'll not be juggled with:
3308:     To hell, allegiance! vows, to the blackest devil
3309:     Conscience and grace, to the profoundest pit!
3310:     I dare damnation. To this point I stand,
3311:     That both the world, I give to negligence,
3312:     Let come what comes; only I'll be reveng'd
3313:     Most throughly for my father.
3314:   King. Who shall stay you?
3315:   Laer. My will, not all the world!
3316:     And for my means, I'll husband them so well
3317:     They shall go far with little.  
3318:   King. Good Laertes,
3319:     If you desire to know the certainty
3320:     Of your dear father's death, is't writ in Your revenge
3321:     That swoopstake you will draw both friend and foe,
3322:     Winner and loser?
3323:   Laer. None but his enemies.
3324:   King. Will you know them then?
3325:   Laer. To his good friends thus wide I'll ope my arms
3326:     And, like the kind life-rend'ring pelican,
3327:     Repast them with my blood.
3328:   King. Why, now You speak
3329:     Like a good child and a true gentleman.
3330:     That I am guiltless of your father's death,
3331:     And am most sensibly in grief for it,
3332:     It shall as level to your judgment pierce
3333:     As day does to your eye.
3334:                               A noise within: 'Let her come in.'
3335:   Laer. How now? What noise is that?
3336: 
3337:                       Enter Ophelia.  
3338: 
3339:     O heat, dry up my brains! Tears seven times salt
3340:     Burn out the sense and virtue of mine eye!
3341:     By heaven, thy madness shall be paid by weight
3342:     Till our scale turn the beam. O rose of May!
3343:     Dear maid, kind sister, sweet Ophelia!
3344:     O heavens! is't possible a young maid's wits
3345:     Should be as mortal as an old man's life?
3346:     Nature is fine in love, and where 'tis fine,
3347:     It sends some precious instance of itself
3348:     After the thing it loves.
3349: 
3350:   Oph. (sings)
3351:          They bore him barefac'd on the bier
3352:            (Hey non nony, nony, hey nony)
3353:          And in his grave rain'd many a tear.
3354: 
3355:     Fare you well, my dove!
3356:   Laer. Hadst thou thy wits, and didst persuade revenge,
3357:     It could not move thus.  
3358:   Oph. You must sing 'A-down a-down, and you call him a-down-a.' O,
3359:     how the wheel becomes it! It is the false steward, that stole his
3360:     master's daughter.
3361:   Laer. This nothing's more than matter.
3362:   Oph. There's rosemary, that's for remembrance. Pray you, love,
3363:     remember. And there is pansies, that's for thoughts.
3364:   Laer. A document in madness! Thoughts and remembrance fitted.
3365:   Oph. There's fennel for you, and columbines. There's rue for you,
3366:     and here's some for me. We may call it herb of grace o' Sundays.
3367:     O, you must wear your rue with a difference! There's a daisy. I
3368:     would give you some violets, but they wither'd all when my father
3369:     died. They say he made a good end.
3370: 
3371:     [Sings] For bonny sweet Robin is all my joy.
3372: 
3373:   Laer. Thought and affliction, passion, hell itself,
3374:     She turns to favour and to prettiness.
3375:   Oph. (sings)
3376:          And will he not come again?
3377:          And will he not come again?
3378:            No, no, he is dead;  
3379:            Go to thy deathbed;
3380:          He never will come again.
3381: 
3382:          His beard was as white as snow,
3383:          All flaxen was his poll.
3384:            He is gone, he is gone,
3385:            And we cast away moan.
3386:          God 'a'mercy on his soul!
3387: 
3388:     And of all Christian souls, I pray God. God b' wi', you.
3389: Exit.
3390:   Laer. Do you see this, O God?
3391:   King. Laertes, I must commune with your grief,
3392:     Or you deny me right. Go but apart,
3393:     Make choice of whom your wisest friends you will,
3394:     And they shall hear and judge 'twixt you and me.
3395:     If by direct or by collateral hand
3396:     They find us touch'd, we will our kingdom give,
3397:     Our crown, our life, and all that we call ours,
3398:     To you in satisfaction; but if not,  
3399:     Be you content to lend your patience to us,
3400:     And we shall jointly labour with your soul
3401:     To give it due content.
3402:   Laer. Let this be so.
3403:     His means of death, his obscure funeral-
3404:     No trophy, sword, nor hatchment o'er his bones,
3405:     No noble rite nor formal ostentation,-
3406:     Cry to be heard, as 'twere from heaven to earth,
3407:     That I must call't in question.
3408:   King. So you shall;
3409:     And where th' offence is let the great axe fall.
3410:     I pray you go with me.
3411:                                                           Exeunt
3412:

3417: Scene VI.
3418: Elsinore. Another room in the Castle.
3419: 
3420: Enter Horatio with an Attendant.
3421: 
3422:   Hor. What are they that would speak with me?
3423:   Servant. Seafaring men, sir. They say they have letters for you.
3424:   Hor. Let them come in.
3425:                                                [Exit Attendant.]
3426:     I do not know from what part of the world
3427:     I should be greeted, if not from Lord Hamlet.
3428: 
3429:                           Enter Sailors.
3430: 
3431:   Sailor. God bless you, sir.
3432:   Hor. Let him bless thee too.
3433:   Sailor. 'A shall, sir, an't please him. There's a letter for you,
3434:     sir,- it comes from th' ambassador that was bound for England- if
3435:     your name be Horatio, as I am let to know it is.
3436:   Hor. (reads the letter) 'Horatio, when thou shalt have overlook'd
3437:     this, give these fellows some means to the King. They have
3438:     letters for him. Ere we were two days old at sea, a pirate of  
3439:     very warlike appointment gave us chase. Finding ourselves too
3440:     slow of sail, we put on a compelled valour, and in the grapple I
3441:     boarded them. On the instant they got clear of our ship; so I
3442:     alone became their prisoner. They have dealt with me like thieves
3443:     of mercy; but they knew what they did: I am to do a good turn for
3444:     them. Let the King have the letters I have sent, and repair thou
3445:     to me with as much speed as thou wouldst fly death. I have words
3446:     to speak in thine ear will make thee dumb; yet are they much too
3447:     light for the bore of the matter. These good fellows will bring
3448:     thee where I am. Rosencrantz and Guildenstern hold their course
3449:     for England. Of them I have much to tell thee. Farewell.
3450:                             'He that thou knowest thine, HAMLET.'
3451: 
3452:     Come, I will give you way for these your letters,
3453:     And do't the speedier that you may direct me
3454:     To him from whom you brought them.                   Exeunt.

3460: Scene VII.
3461: Elsinore. Another room in the Castle.
3462: 
3463: Enter King and Laertes.
3464: 
3465:   King. Now must your conscience my acquittance seal,
3466:     And You must put me in your heart for friend,
3467:     Sith you have heard, and with a knowing ear,
3468:     That he which hath your noble father slain
3469:     Pursued my life.
3470:   Laer. It well appears. But tell me
3471:     Why you proceeded not against these feats
3472:     So crimeful and so capital in nature,
3473:     As by your safety, wisdom, all things else,
3474:     You mainly were stirr'd up.
3475:   King. O, for two special reasons,
3476:     Which may to you, perhaps, seein much unsinew'd,
3477:     But yet to me they are strong. The Queen his mother
3478:     Lives almost by his looks; and for myself,-
3479:     My virtue or my plague, be it either which,-
3480:     She's so conjunctive to my life and soul
3481:     That, as the star moves not but in his sphere,  
3482:     I could not but by her. The other motive
3483:     Why to a public count I might not go
3484:     Is the great love the general gender bear him,
3485:     Who, dipping all his faults in their affection,
3486:     Would, like the spring that turneth wood to stone,
3487:     Convert his gives to graces; so that my arrows,
3488:     Too slightly timber'd for so loud a wind,
3489:     Would have reverted to my bow again,
3490:     And not where I had aim'd them.
3491:   Laer. And so have I a noble father lost;
3492:     A sister driven into desp'rate terms,
3493:     Whose worth, if praises may go back again,
3494:     Stood challenger on mount of all the age
3495:     For her perfections. But my revenge will come.
3496:   King. Break not your sleeps for that. You must not think
3497:     That we are made of stuff so flat and dull
3498:     That we can let our beard be shook with danger,
3499:     And think it pastime. You shortly shall hear more.
3500:     I lov'd your father, and we love ourself,
3501:     And that, I hope, will teach you to imagine-  
3502: 
3503:                  Enter a Messenger with letters.
3504: 
3505:     How now? What news?
3506:   Mess. Letters, my lord, from Hamlet:
3507:     This to your Majesty; this to the Queen.
3508:   King. From Hamlet? Who brought them?
3509:   Mess. Sailors, my lord, they say; I saw them not.
3510:     They were given me by Claudio; he receiv'd them
3511:     Of him that brought them.
3512:   King. Laertes, you shall hear them.
3513:     Leave us.
3514:                                                  Exit Messenger.
3515:     [Reads]'High and Mighty,-You shall know I am set naked on your
3516:     kingdom. To-morrow shall I beg leave to see your kingly eyes;
3517:     when I shall (first asking your pardon thereunto) recount the
3518:     occasion of my sudden and more strange return.
3519:                                                      'HAMLET.'
3520:     What should this mean? Are all the rest come back?
3521:     Or is it some abuse, and no such thing?  
3522:   Laer. Know you the hand?
3523:   King. 'Tis Hamlet's character. 'Naked!'
3524:     And in a postscript here, he says 'alone.'
3525:     Can you advise me?
3526:   Laer. I am lost in it, my lord. But let him come!
3527:     It warms the very sickness in my heart
3528:     That I shall live and tell him to his teeth,
3529:     'Thus didest thou.'
3530:   King. If it be so, Laertes
3531:     (As how should it be so? how otherwise?),
3532:     Will you be rul'd by me?
3533:   Laer. Ay my lord,
3534:     So you will not o'errule me to a peace.
3535:   King. To thine own peace. If he be now return'd
3536:     As checking at his voyage, and that he means
3537:     No more to undertake it, I will work him
3538:     To exploit now ripe in my device,
3539:     Under the which he shall not choose but fall;
3540:     And for his death no wind
3541:     But even his mother shall uncharge the practice  
3542:     And call it accident.
3543:   Laer. My lord, I will be rul'd;
3544:     The rather, if you could devise it so
3545:     That I might be the organ.
3546:   King. It falls right.
3547:     You have been talk'd of since your travel much,
3548:     And that in Hamlet's hearing, for a quality
3549:     Wherein they say you shine, Your sun of parts
3550:     Did not together pluck such envy from him
3551:     As did that one; and that, in my regard,
3552:     Of the unworthiest siege.
3553:   Laer. What part is that, my lord?
3554:   King. A very riband in the cap of youth-
3555:     Yet needfull too; for youth no less becomes
3556:     The light and careless livery that it wears
3557:     Thin settled age his sables and his weeds,
3558:     Importing health and graveness. Two months since
3559:     Here was a gentleman of Normandy.
3560:     I have seen myself, and serv'd against, the French,
3561:     And they can well on horseback; but this gallant  
3562:     Had witchcraft in't. He grew unto his seat,
3563:     And to such wondrous doing brought his horse
3564:     As had he been incorps'd and demi-natur'd
3565:     With the brave beast. So far he topp'd my thought
3566:     That I, in forgery of shapes and tricks,
3567:     Come short of what he did.
3568:   Laer. A Norman was't?
3569:   King. A Norman.
3570:   Laer. Upon my life, Lamound.
3571:   King. The very same.
3572:   Laer. I know him well. He is the broach indeed
3573:     And gem of all the nation.
3574:   King. He made confession of you;
3575:     And gave you such a masterly report
3576:     For art and exercise in your defence,
3577:     And for your rapier most especially,
3578:     That he cried out 'twould be a sight indeed
3579:     If one could match you. The scrimers of their nation
3580:     He swore had neither motion, guard, nor eye,
3581:     If you oppos'd them. Sir, this report of his  
3582:     Did Hamlet so envenom with his envy
3583:     That he could nothing do but wish and beg
3584:     Your sudden coming o'er to play with you.
3585:     Now, out of this-
3586:   Laer. What out of this, my lord?
3587:   King. Laertes, was your father dear to you?
3588:     Or are you like the painting of a sorrow,
3589:     A face without a heart,'
3590:   Laer. Why ask you this?
3591:   King. Not that I think you did not love your father;
3592:     But that I know love is begun by time,
3593:     And that I see, in passages of proof,
3594:     Time qualifies the spark and fire of it.
3595:     There lives within the very flame of love
3596:     A kind of wick or snuff that will abate it;
3597:     And nothing is at a like goodness still;
3598:     For goodness, growing to a plurisy,
3599:     Dies in his own too-much. That we would do,
3600:     We should do when we would; for this 'would' changes,
3601:     And hath abatements and delays as many  
3602:     As there are tongues, are hands, are accidents;
3603:     And then this 'should' is like a spendthrift sigh,
3604:     That hurts by easing. But to the quick o' th' ulcer!
3605:     Hamlet comes back. What would you undertake
3606:     To show yourself your father's son in deed
3607:     More than in words?
3608:   Laer. To cut his throat i' th' church!
3609:   King. No place indeed should murther sanctuarize;
3610:     Revenge should have no bounds. But, good Laertes,
3611:     Will you do this? Keep close within your chamber.
3612:     Will return'd shall know you are come home.
3613:     We'll put on those shall praise your excellence
3614:     And set a double varnish on the fame
3615:     The Frenchman gave you; bring you in fine together
3616:     And wager on your heads. He, being remiss,
3617:     Most generous, and free from all contriving,
3618:     Will not peruse the foils; so that with ease,
3619:     Or with a little shuffling, you may choose
3620:     A sword unbated, and, in a pass of practice,
3621:     Requite him for your father.  
3622:   Laer. I will do't!
3623:     And for that purpose I'll anoint my sword.
3624:     I bought an unction of a mountebank,
3625:     So mortal that, but dip a knife in it,
3626:     Where it draws blood no cataplasm so rare,
3627:     Collected from all simples that have virtue
3628:     Under the moon, can save the thing from death
3629:     This is but scratch'd withal. I'll touch my point
3630:     With this contagion, that, if I gall him slightly,
3631:     It may be death.
3632:   King. Let's further think of this,
3633:     Weigh what convenience both of time and means
3634:     May fit us to our shape. If this should fall,
3635:     And that our drift look through our bad performance.
3636:     'Twere better not assay'd. Therefore this project
3637:     Should have a back or second, that might hold
3638:     If this did blast in proof. Soft! let me see.
3639:     We'll make a solemn wager on your cunnings-
3640:     I ha't!
3641:     When in your motion you are hot and dry-  
3642:     As make your bouts more violent to that end-
3643:     And that he calls for drink, I'll have prepar'd him
3644:     A chalice for the nonce; whereon but sipping,
3645:     If he by chance escape your venom'd stuck,
3646:     Our purpose may hold there.- But stay, what noise,
3647: 
3648:                            Enter Queen.
3649: 
3650:     How now, sweet queen?
3651:   Queen. One woe doth tread upon another's heel,
3652:     So fast they follow. Your sister's drown'd, Laertes.
3653:   Laer. Drown'd! O, where?
3654:   Queen. There is a willow grows aslant a brook,
3655:     That shows his hoar leaves in the glassy stream.
3656:     There with fantastic garlands did she come
3657:     Of crowflowers, nettles, daisies, and long purples,
3658:     That liberal shepherds give a grosser name,
3659:     But our cold maids do dead men's fingers call them.
3660:     There on the pendant boughs her coronet weeds
3661:     Clamb'ring to hang, an envious sliver broke,  
3662:     When down her weedy trophies and herself
3663:     Fell in the weeping brook. Her clothes spread wide
3664:     And, mermaid-like, awhile they bore her up;
3665:     Which time she chaunted snatches of old tunes,
3666:     As one incapable of her own distress,
3667:     Or like a creature native and indued
3668:     Unto that element; but long it could not be
3669:     Till that her garments, heavy with their drink,
3670:     Pull'd the poor wretch from her melodious lay
3671:     To muddy death.
3672:   Laer. Alas, then she is drown'd?
3673:   Queen. Drown'd, drown'd.
3674:   Laer. Too much of water hast thou, poor Ophelia,
3675:     And therefore I forbid my tears; but yet
3676:     It is our trick; nature her custom holds,
3677:     Let shame say what it will. When these are gone,
3678:     The woman will be out. Adieu, my lord.
3679:     I have a speech of fire, that fain would blaze
3680:     But that this folly douts it.                          Exit.
3681:   King. Let's follow, Gertrude.  
3682:     How much I had to do to calm his rage I
3683:     Now fear I this will give it start again;
3684:     Therefore let's follow.
3685:                                                          Exeunt.
3686:

3691: ACT V. Scene I.
3692: Elsinore. A churchyard.
3693: 
3694: Enter two Clowns, [with spades and pickaxes].
3695: 
3696:   Clown. Is she to be buried in Christian burial when she wilfully
3697:     seeks her own salvation?
3698:   Other. I tell thee she is; therefore make her grave straight.
3699:     The crowner hath sate on her, and finds it Christian burial.
3700:   Clown. How can that be, unless she drown'd herself in her own
3701:     defence?
3702:   Other. Why, 'tis found so.
3703:   Clown. It must be se offendendo; it cannot be else. For here lies
3704:     the point: if I drown myself wittingly, it argues an act; and an
3705:     act hath three branches-it is to act, to do, and to perform;
3706:     argal, she drown'd herself wittingly.
3707:   Other. Nay, but hear you, Goodman Delver!
3708:   Clown. Give me leave. Here lies the water; good. Here stands the
3709:     man; good. If the man go to this water and drown himself, it is,
3710:     will he nill he, he goes- mark you that. But if the water come to
3711:     him and drown him, he drowns not himself. Argal, he that is not
3712:     guilty of his own death shortens not his own life.  
3713:   Other. But is this law?
3714:   Clown. Ay, marry, is't- crowner's quest law.
3715:   Other. Will you ha' the truth an't? If this had not been a
3716:     gentlewoman, she should have been buried out o' Christian burial.
3717:   Clown. Why, there thou say'st! And the more pity that great folk
3718:     should have count'nance in this world to drown or hang themselves
3719:     more than their even-Christen. Come, my spade! There is no
3720:     ancient gentlemen but gard'ners, ditchers, and grave-makers. They
3721:     hold up Adam's profession.
3722:   Other. Was he a gentleman?
3723:   Clown. 'A was the first that ever bore arms.
3724:   Other. Why, he had none.
3725:   Clown. What, art a heathen? How dost thou understand the Scripture?
3726:     The Scripture says Adam digg'd. Could he dig without arms? I'll
3727:     put another question to thee. If thou answerest me not to the
3728:     purpose, confess thyself-
3729:   Other. Go to!
3730:   Clown. What is he that builds stronger than either the mason, the
3731:     shipwright, or the carpenter?
3732:   Other. The gallows-maker; for that frame outlives a thousand  
3733:     tenants.
3734:   Clown. I like thy wit well, in good faith. The gallows does well.
3735:     But how does it well? It does well to those that do ill. Now,
3736:     thou dost ill to say the gallows is built stronger than the
3737:     church. Argal, the gallows may do well to thee. To't again, come!
3738:   Other. Who builds stronger than a mason, a shipwright, or a
3739:     carpenter?
3740:   Clown. Ay, tell me that, and unyoke.
3741:   Other. Marry, now I can tell!
3742:   Clown. To't.
3743:   Other. Mass, I cannot tell.
3744: 
3745:                  Enter Hamlet and Horatio afar off.
3746: 
3747:   Clown. Cudgel thy brains no more about it, for your dull ass will
3748:     not mend his pace with beating; and when you are ask'd this
3749:     question next, say 'a grave-maker.' The houses he makes lasts
3750:     till doomsday. Go, get thee to Yaughan; fetch me a stoup of
3751:     liquor.
3752:                                             [Exit Second Clown.]  
3753: 
3754:                        [Clown digs and] sings.
3755: 
3756:        In youth when I did love, did love,
3757:          Methought it was very sweet;
3758:        To contract- O- the time for- a- my behove,
3759:          O, methought there- a- was nothing- a- meet.
3760: 
3761:   Ham. Has this fellow no feeling of his business, that he sings at
3762:     grave-making?
3763:   Hor. Custom hath made it in him a Property of easiness.
3764:   Ham. 'Tis e'en so. The hand of little employment hath the daintier
3765:     sense.
3766:   Clown. (sings)
3767:          But age with his stealing steps
3768:            Hath clawed me in his clutch,
3769:          And hath shipped me intil the land,
3770:            As if I had never been such.
3771:                                             [Throws up a skull.]
3772:   
3773:   Ham. That skull had a tongue in it, and could sing once. How the
3774:     knave jowls it to the ground,as if 'twere Cain's jawbone, that
3775:     did the first murther! This might be the pate of a Politician,
3776:     which this ass now o'erreaches; one that would circumvent God,
3777:     might it not?
3778:   Hor. It might, my lord.
3779:   Ham. Or of a courtier, which could say 'Good morrow, sweet lord!
3780:     How dost thou, good lord?' This might be my Lord Such-a-one, that
3781:     prais'd my Lord Such-a-one's horse when he meant to beg it- might
3782:     it not?
3783:   Hor. Ay, my lord.
3784:   Ham. Why, e'en so! and now my Lady Worm's, chapless, and knock'd
3785:     about the mazzard with a sexton's spade. Here's fine revolution,
3786:     and we had the trick to see't. Did these bones cost no more the
3787:     breeding but to play at loggets with 'em? Mine ache to think
3788:     on't.
3789:   Clown. (Sings)
3790:          A pickaxe and a spade, a spade,
3791:            For and a shrouding sheet;
3792:          O, a Pit of clay for to be made  
3793:            For such a guest is meet.
3794:                                       Throws up [another skull].
3795: 
3796:   Ham. There's another. Why may not that be the skull of a lawyer?
3797:     Where be his quiddits now, his quillets, his cases, his tenures,
3798:     and his tricks? Why does he suffer this rude knave now to knock
3799:     him about the sconce with a dirty shovel, and will not tell him
3800:     of his action of battery? Hum! This fellow might be in's time a
3801:     great buyer of land, with his statutes, his recognizances, his
3802:     fines, his double vouchers, his recoveries. Is this the fine of
3803:     his fines, and the recovery of his recoveries, to have his fine
3804:     pate full of fine dirt? Will his vouchers vouch him no more of
3805:     his purchases, and double ones too, than the length and breadth
3806:     of a pair of indentures? The very conveyances of his lands will
3807:     scarcely lie in this box; and must th' inheritor himself have no
3808:     more, ha?
3809:   Hor. Not a jot more, my lord.
3810:   Ham. Is not parchment made of sheepskins?
3811:   Hor. Ay, my lord, And of calveskins too.
3812:   Ham. They are sheep and calves which seek out assurance in that. I  
3813:     will speak to this fellow. Whose grave's this, sirrah?
3814:   Clown. Mine, sir.
3815: 
3816:     [Sings] O, a pit of clay for to be made
3817:               For such a guest is meet.
3818: 
3819:   Ham. I think it be thine indeed, for thou liest in't.
3820:   Clown. You lie out on't, sir, and therefore 'tis not yours.
3821:     For my part, I do not lie in't, yet it is mine.
3822:   Ham. Thou dost lie in't, to be in't and say it is thine. 'Tis for
3823:     the dead, not for the quick; therefore thou liest.
3824:   Clown. 'Tis a quick lie, sir; 'twill away again from me to you.
3825:   Ham. What man dost thou dig it for?
3826:   Clown. For no man, sir.
3827:   Ham. What woman then?
3828:   Clown. For none neither.
3829:   Ham. Who is to be buried in't?
3830:   Clown. One that was a woman, sir; but, rest her soul, she's dead.
3831:   Ham. How absolute the knave is! We must speak by the card, or
3832:     equivocation will undo us. By the Lord, Horatio, this three years
3833:     I have taken note of it, the age is grown so picked that the toe  
3834:     of the peasant comes so near the heel of the courtier he galls
3835:     his kibe.- How long hast thou been a grave-maker?
3836:   Clown. Of all the days i' th' year, I came to't that day that our
3837:     last king Hamlet overcame Fortinbras.
3838:   Ham. How long is that since?
3839:   Clown. Cannot you tell that? Every fool can tell that. It was the
3840:     very day that young Hamlet was born- he that is mad, and sent
3841:     into England.
3842:   Ham. Ay, marry, why was be sent into England?
3843:   Clown. Why, because 'a was mad. 'A shall recover his wits there;
3844:     or, if 'a do not, 'tis no great matter there.
3845:   Ham. Why?
3846:   Clown. 'Twill not he seen in him there. There the men are as mad as
3847:     he.
3848:   Ham. How came he mad?
3849:   Clown. Very strangely, they say.
3850:   Ham. How strangely?
3851:   Clown. Faith, e'en with losing his wits.
3852:   Ham. Upon what ground?
3853:   Clown. Why, here in Denmark. I have been sexton here, man and boy  
3854:     thirty years.
3855:   Ham. How long will a man lie i' th' earth ere he rot?
3856:   Clown. Faith, if 'a be not rotten before 'a die (as we have many
3857:     pocky corses now-a-days that will scarce hold the laying in, I
3858:     will last you some eight year or nine year. A tanner will last
3859:     you nine year.
3860:   Ham. Why he more than another?
3861:   Clown. Why, sir, his hide is so tann'd with his trade that 'a will
3862:     keep out water a great while; and your water is a sore decayer of
3863:     your whoreson dead body. Here's a skull now. This skull hath lien
3864:     you i' th' earth three-and-twenty years.
3865:   Ham. Whose was it?
3866:   Clown. A whoreson, mad fellow's it was. Whose do you think it was?
3867:   Ham. Nay, I know not.
3868:   Clown. A pestilence on him for a mad rogue! 'A pour'd a flagon of
3869:     Rhenish on my head once. This same skull, sir, was Yorick's
3870:     skull, the King's jester.
3871:   Ham. This?
3872:   Clown. E'en that.
3873:   Ham. Let me see. [Takes the skull.] Alas, poor Yorick! I knew him,  
3874:     Horatio. A fellow of infinite jest, of most excellent fancy. He
3875:     hath borne me on his back a thousand tunes. And now how abhorred
3876:     in my imagination it is! My gorge rises at it. Here hung those
3877:     lips that I have kiss'd I know not how oft. Where be your gibes
3878:     now? your gambols? your songs? your flashes of merriment that
3879:     were wont to set the table on a roar? Not one now, to mock your
3880:     own grinning? Quite chap- fall'n? Now get you to my lady's
3881:     chamber, and tell her, let her paint an inch thick, to this
3882:     favour she must come. Make her laugh at that. Prithee, Horatio,
3883:     tell me one thing.
3884:   Hor. What's that, my lord?
3885:   Ham. Dost thou think Alexander look'd o' this fashion i' th' earth?
3886:   Hor. E'en so.
3887:   Ham. And smelt so? Pah!
3888:                                           [Puts down the skull.]
3889:   Hor. E'en so, my lord.
3890:   Ham. To what base uses we may return, Horatio! Why may not
3891:     imagination trace the noble dust of Alexander till he find it
3892:     stopping a bunghole?
3893:   Hor. 'Twere to consider too curiously, to consider so.  
3894:   Ham. No, faith, not a jot; but to follow him thither with modesty
3895:     enough, and likelihood to lead it; as thus: Alexander died,
3896:     Alexander was buried, Alexander returneth into dust; the dust is
3897:     earth; of earth we make loam; and why of that loam (whereto he
3898:     was converted) might they not stop a beer barrel?
3899:     Imperious Caesar, dead and turn'd to clay,
3900:     Might stop a hole to keep the wind away.
3901:     O, that that earth which kept the world in awe
3902:     Should patch a wall t' expel the winter's flaw!
3903:     But soft! but soft! aside! Here comes the King-
3904: 
3905:     Enter [priests with] a coffin [in funeral procession], King,
3906:              Queen, Laertes, with Lords attendant.]
3907: 
3908:     The Queen, the courtiers. Who is this they follow?
3909:     And with such maimed rites? This doth betoken
3910:     The corse they follow did with desp'rate hand
3911:     Fordo it own life. 'Twas of some estate.
3912:     Couch we awhile, and mark.
3913:                                          [Retires with Horatio.]  
3914:   Laer. What ceremony else?
3915:   Ham. That is Laertes,
3916:     A very noble youth. Mark.
3917:   Laer. What ceremony else?
3918:   Priest. Her obsequies have been as far enlarg'd
3919:     As we have warranty. Her death was doubtful;
3920:     And, but that great command o'ersways the order,
3921:     She should in ground unsanctified have lodg'd
3922:     Till the last trumpet. For charitable prayers,
3923:     Shards, flints, and pebbles should be thrown on her.
3924:     Yet here she is allow'd her virgin crants,
3925:     Her maiden strewments, and the bringing home
3926:     Of bell and burial.
3927:   Laer. Must there no more be done?
3928:   Priest. No more be done.
3929:     We should profane the service of the dead
3930:     To sing a requiem and such rest to her
3931:     As to peace-parted souls.
3932:   Laer. Lay her i' th' earth;
3933:     And from her fair and unpolluted flesh  
3934:     May violets spring! I tell thee, churlish priest,
3935:     A minist'ring angel shall my sister be
3936:     When thou liest howling.
3937:   Ham. What, the fair Ophelia?
3938:   Queen. Sweets to the sweet! Farewell.
3939:                                              [Scatters flowers.]
3940:     I hop'd thou shouldst have been my Hamlet's wife;
3941:     I thought thy bride-bed to have deck'd, sweet maid,
3942:     And not have strew'd thy grave.
3943:   Laer. O, treble woe
3944:     Fall ten times treble on that cursed head
3945:     Whose wicked deed thy most ingenious sense
3946:     Depriv'd thee of! Hold off the earth awhile,
3947:     Till I have caught her once more in mine arms.
3948:                                              Leaps in the grave.
3949:     Now pile your dust upon the quick and dead
3950:     Till of this flat a mountain you have made
3951:     T' o'ertop old Pelion or the skyish head
3952:     Of blue Olympus.
3953:   Ham. [comes forward] What is he whose grief  
3954:     Bears such an emphasis? whose phrase of sorrow
3955:     Conjures the wand'ring stars, and makes them stand
3956:     Like wonder-wounded hearers? This is I,
3957:     Hamlet the Dane.                    [Leaps in after Laertes.
3958:   Laer. The devil take thy soul!
3959:                                             [Grapples with him].
3960:   Ham. Thou pray'st not well.
3961:     I prithee take thy fingers from my throat;
3962:     For, though I am not splenitive and rash,
3963:     Yet have I in me something dangerous,
3964:     Which let thy wisdom fear. Hold off thy hand!
3965:   King. Pluck thein asunder.
3966:   Queen. Hamlet, Hamlet!
3967:   All. Gentlemen!
3968:   Hor. Good my lord, be quiet.
3969:              [The Attendants part them, and they come out of the
3970:                                                          grave.]
3971:   Ham. Why, I will fight with him upon this theme
3972:     Until my eyelids will no longer wag.
3973:   Queen. O my son, what theme?  
3974:   Ham. I lov'd Ophelia. Forty thousand brothers
3975:     Could not (with all their quantity of love)
3976:     Make up my sum. What wilt thou do for her?
3977:   King. O, he is mad, Laertes.
3978:   Queen. For love of God, forbear him!
3979:   Ham. 'Swounds, show me what thou't do.
3980:     Woo't weep? woo't fight? woo't fast? woo't tear thyself?
3981:     Woo't drink up esill? eat a crocodile?
3982:     I'll do't. Dost thou come here to whine?
3983:     To outface me with leaping in her grave?
3984:     Be buried quick with her, and so will I.
3985:     And if thou prate of mountains, let them throw
3986:     Millions of acres on us, till our ground,
3987:     Singeing his pate against the burning zone,
3988:     Make Ossa like a wart! Nay, an thou'lt mouth,
3989:     I'll rant as well as thou.
3990:   Queen. This is mere madness;
3991:     And thus a while the fit will work on him.
3992:     Anon, as patient as the female dove
3993:     When that her golden couplets are disclos'd,  
3994:     His silence will sit drooping.
3995:   Ham. Hear you, sir!
3996:     What is the reason that you use me thus?
3997:     I lov'd you ever. But it is no matter.
3998:     Let Hercules himself do what he may,
3999:     The cat will mew, and dog will have his day.
4000: Exit.
4001:   King. I pray thee, good Horatio, wait upon him.
4002:                                                    Exit Horatio.
4003:     [To Laertes] Strengthen your patience in our last night's speech.
4004:     We'll put the matter to the present push.-
4005:     Good Gertrude, set some watch over your son.-
4006:     This grave shall have a living monument.
4007:     An hour of quiet shortly shall we see;
4008:     Till then in patience our proceeding be.
4009:                                                          Exeunt.
4010: 
4011: 
4012: 
4013: 
4014: Scene II.
4015: Elsinore. A hall in the Castle.
4016: 
4017: Enter Hamlet and Horatio.
4018: 
4019:   Ham. So much for this, sir; now shall you see the other.
4020:     You do remember all the circumstance?
4021:   Hor. Remember it, my lord!
4022:   Ham. Sir, in my heart there was a kind of fighting
4023:     That would not let me sleep. Methought I lay
4024:     Worse than the mutinies in the bilboes. Rashly-
4025:     And prais'd be rashness for it; let us know,
4026:     Our indiscretion sometime serves us well
4027:     When our deep plots do pall; and that should learn us
4028:     There's a divinity that shapes our ends,
4029:     Rough-hew them how we will-
4030:   Hor. That is most certain.
4031:   Ham. Up from my cabin,
4032:     My sea-gown scarf'd about me, in the dark
4033:     Grop'd I to find out them; had my desire,
4034:     Finger'd their packet, and in fine withdrew
4035:     To mine own room again; making so bold  
4036:     (My fears forgetting manners) to unseal
4037:     Their grand commission; where I found, Horatio
4038:     (O royal knavery!), an exact command,
4039:     Larded with many several sorts of reasons,
4040:     Importing Denmark's health, and England's too,
4041:     With, hoo! such bugs and goblins in my life-
4042:     That, on the supervise, no leisure bated,
4043:     No, not to stay the finding of the axe,
4044:     My head should be struck off.
4045:   Hor. Is't possible?
4046:   Ham. Here's the commission; read it at more leisure.
4047:     But wilt thou bear me how I did proceed?
4048:   Hor. I beseech you.
4049:   Ham. Being thus benetted round with villanies,
4050:     Or I could make a prologue to my brains,
4051:     They had begun the play. I sat me down;
4052:     Devis'd a new commission; wrote it fair.
4053:     I once did hold it, as our statists do,
4054:     A baseness to write fair, and labour'd much
4055:     How to forget that learning; but, sir, now  
4056:     It did me yeoman's service. Wilt thou know
4057:     Th' effect of what I wrote?
4058:   Hor. Ay, good my lord.
4059:   Ham. An earnest conjuration from the King,
4060:     As England was his faithful tributary,
4061:     As love between them like the palm might flourish,
4062:     As peace should still her wheaten garland wear
4063:     And stand a comma 'tween their amities,
4064:     And many such-like as's of great charge,
4065:     That, on the view and knowing of these contents,
4066:     Without debatement further, more or less,
4067:     He should the bearers put to sudden death,
4068:     Not shriving time allow'd.
4069:   Hor. How was this seal'd?
4070:   Ham. Why, even in that was heaven ordinant.
4071:     I had my father's signet in my purse,
4072:     which was the model of that Danish seal;
4073:     Folded the writ up in the form of th' other,
4074:     Subscrib'd it, gave't th' impression, plac'd it safely,
4075:     The changeling never known. Now, the next day  
4076:     Was our sea-fight; and what to this was sequent
4077:     Thou know'st already.
4078:   Hor. So Guildenstern and Rosencrantz go to't.
4079:   Ham. Why, man, they did make love to this employment!
4080:     They are not near my conscience; their defeat
4081:     Does by their own insinuation grow.
4082:     'Tis dangerous when the baser nature comes
4083:     Between the pass and fell incensed points
4084:     Of mighty opposites.
4085:   Hor. Why, what a king is this!
4086:   Ham. Does it not, thinks't thee, stand me now upon-
4087:     He that hath kill'd my king, and whor'd my mother;
4088:     Popp'd in between th' election and my hopes;
4089:     Thrown out his angle for my Proper life,
4090:     And with such coz'nage- is't not perfect conscience
4091:     To quit him with this arm? And is't not to be damn'd
4092:     To let this canker of our nature come
4093:     In further evil?
4094:   Hor. It must be shortly known to him from England
4095:     What is the issue of the business there.  
4096:   Ham. It will be short; the interim is mine,
4097:     And a man's life is no more than to say 'one.'
4098:     But I am very sorry, good Horatio,
4099:     That to Laertes I forgot myself,
4100:     For by the image of my cause I see
4101:     The portraiture of his. I'll court his favours.
4102:     But sure the bravery of his grief did put me
4103:     Into a tow'ring passion.
4104:   Hor. Peace! Who comes here?
4105: 
4106:                  Enter young Osric, a courtier.
4107: 
4108:   Osr. Your lordship is right welcome back to Denmark.
4109:   Ham. I humbly thank you, sir. [Aside to Horatio] Dost know this
4110:     waterfly?
4111:   Hor. [aside to Hamlet] No, my good lord.
4112:   Ham. [aside to Horatio] Thy state is the more gracious; for 'tis a
4113:     vice to know him. He hath much land, and fertile. Let a beast be
4114:     lord of beasts, and his crib shall stand at the king's mess. 'Tis
4115:     a chough; but, as I say, spacious in the possession of dirt.  
4116:   Osr. Sweet lord, if your lordship were at leisure, I should impart
4117:     a thing to you from his Majesty.
4118:   Ham. I will receive it, sir, with all diligence of spirit. Put your
4119:     bonnet to his right use. 'Tis for the head.
4120:   Osr. I thank your lordship, it is very hot.
4121:   Ham. No, believe me, 'tis very cold; the wind is northerly.
4122:   Osr. It is indifferent cold, my lord, indeed.
4123:   Ham. But yet methinks it is very sultry and hot for my complexion.
4124:   Osr. Exceedingly, my lord; it is very sultry, as 'twere- I cannot
4125:     tell how. But, my lord, his Majesty bade me signify to you that
4126:     he has laid a great wager on your head. Sir, this is the matter-
4127:   Ham. I beseech you remember.
4128:                            [Hamlet moves him to put on his hat.]
4129:   Osr. Nay, good my lord; for mine ease, in good faith. Sir, here is
4130:     newly come to court Laertes; believe me, an absolute gentleman,
4131:     full of most excellent differences, of very soft society and
4132:     great showing. Indeed, to speak feelingly of him, he is the card
4133:     or calendar of gentry; for you shall find in him the continent of
4134:     what part a gentleman would see.
4135:   Ham. Sir, his definement suffers no perdition in you; though, I  
4136:     know, to divide him inventorially would dozy th' arithmetic of
4137:     memory, and yet but yaw neither in respect of his quick sail.
4138:     But, in the verity of extolment, I take him to be a soul of great
4139:     article, and his infusion of such dearth and rareness as, to make
4140:     true diction of him, his semblable is his mirror, and who else
4141:     would trace him, his umbrage, nothing more.
4142:   Osr. Your lordship speaks most infallibly of him.
4143:   Ham. The concernancy, sir? Why do we wrap the gentleman in our more
4144:     rawer breath
4145:   Osr. Sir?
4146:   Hor [aside to Hamlet] Is't not possible to understand in another
4147:     tongue? You will do't, sir, really.
4148:   Ham. What imports the nomination of this gentleman
4149:   Osr. Of Laertes?
4150:   Hor. [aside] His purse is empty already. All's golden words are
4151:     spent.
4152:   Ham. Of him, sir.
4153:   Osr. I know you are not ignorant-
4154:   Ham. I would you did, sir; yet, in faith, if you did, it would not
4155:     much approve me. Well, sir?  
4156:   Osr. You are not ignorant of what excellence Laertes is-
4157:   Ham. I dare not confess that, lest I should compare with him in
4158:     excellence; but to know a man well were to know himself.
4159:   Osr. I mean, sir, for his weapon; but in the imputation laid on him
4160:     by them, in his meed he's unfellowed.
4161:   Ham. What's his weapon?
4162:   Osr. Rapier and dagger.
4163:   Ham. That's two of his weapons- but well.
4164:   Osr. The King, sir, hath wager'd with him six Barbary horses;
4165:     against the which he has impon'd, as I take it, six French
4166:     rapiers and poniards, with their assigns, as girdle, hangers, and
4167:     so. Three of the carriages, in faith, are very dear to fancy,
4168:     very responsive to the hilts, most delicate carriages, and of
4169:     very liberal conceit.
4170:   Ham. What call you the carriages?
4171:   Hor. [aside to Hamlet] I knew you must be edified by the margent
4172:     ere you had done.
4173:   Osr. The carriages, sir, are the hangers.
4174:   Ham. The phrase would be more germane to the matter if we could
4175:     carry cannon by our sides. I would it might be hangers till then.  
4176:     But on! Six Barbary horses against six French swords, their
4177:     assigns, and three liberal-conceited carriages: that's the French
4178:     bet against the Danish. Why is this all impon'd, as you call it?
4179:   Osr. The King, sir, hath laid that, in a dozen passes between
4180:     yourself and him, he shall not exceed you three hits; he hath
4181:     laid on twelve for nine, and it would come to immediate trial
4182:     if your lordship would vouchsafe the answer.
4183:   Ham. How if I answer no?
4184:   Osr. I mean, my lord, the opposition of your person in trial.
4185:   Ham. Sir, I will walk here in the hall. If it please his Majesty,
4186:     it is the breathing time of day with me. Let the foils be
4187:     brought, the gentleman willing, and the King hold his purpose,
4188:     I will win for him if I can; if not, I will gain nothing but my
4189:     shame and the odd hits.
4190:   Osr. Shall I redeliver you e'en so?
4191:   Ham. To this effect, sir, after what flourish your nature will.
4192:   Osr. I commend my duty to your lordship.
4193:   Ham. Yours, yours. [Exit Osric.] He does well to commend it
4194:     himself; there are no tongues else for's turn.
4195:   Hor. This lapwing runs away with the shell on his head.  
4196:   Ham. He did comply with his dug before he suck'd it. Thus has he,
4197:     and many more of the same bevy that I know the drossy age dotes
4198:     on, only got the tune of the time and outward habit of encounter-
4199:     a kind of yesty collection, which carries them through and
4200:     through the most fann'd and winnowed opinions; and do but blow
4201:     them to their trial-the bubbles are out,
4202: 
4203:                             Enter a Lord.
4204: 
4205:   Lord. My lord, his Majesty commended him to you by young Osric, who
4206:     brings back to him, that you attend him in the hall. He sends to
4207:     know if your pleasure hold to play with Laertes, or that you will
4208:     take longer time.
4209:   Ham. I am constant to my purposes; they follow the King's pleasure.
4210:     If his fitness speaks, mine is ready; now or whensoever, provided
4211:     I be so able as now.
4212:   Lord. The King and Queen and all are coming down.
4213:   Ham. In happy time.
4214:   Lord. The Queen desires you to use some gentle entertainment to
4215:     Laertes before you fall to play.  
4216:   Ham. She well instructs me.
4217:                                                     [Exit Lord.]
4218:   Hor. You will lose this wager, my lord.
4219:   Ham. I do not think so. Since he went into France I have been in
4220:     continual practice. I shall win at the odds. But thou wouldst not
4221:     think how ill all's here about my heart. But it is no matter.
4222:   Hor. Nay, good my lord -
4223:   Ham. It is but foolery; but it is such a kind of gaingiving as
4224:     would perhaps trouble a woman.
4225:   Hor. If your mind dislike anything, obey it. I will forestall their
4226:     repair hither and say you are not fit.
4227:   Ham. Not a whit, we defy augury; there's a special providence in
4228:     the fall of a sparrow. If it be now, 'tis not to come', if it be
4229:     not to come, it will be now; if it be not now, yet it will come:
4230:     the readiness is all. Since no man knows aught of what he leaves,
4231:     what is't to leave betimes? Let be.
4232: 
4233:     Enter King, Queen, Laertes, Osric, and Lords, with other
4234:               Attendants with foils and gauntlets.
4235:                A table and flagons of wine on it.  
4236: 
4237:   King. Come, Hamlet, come, and take this hand from me.
4238:                     [The King puts Laertes' hand into Hamlet's.]
4239:   Ham. Give me your pardon, sir. I have done you wrong;
4240:     But pardon't, as you are a gentleman.
4241:     This presence knows,
4242:     And you must needs have heard, how I am punish'd
4243:     With sore distraction. What I have done
4244:     That might your nature, honour, and exception
4245:     Roughly awake, I here proclaim was madness.
4246:     Was't Hamlet wrong'd Laertes? Never Hamlet.
4247:     If Hamlet from himself be taken away,
4248:     And when he's not himself does wrong Laertes,
4249:     Then Hamlet does it not, Hamlet denies it.
4250:     Who does it, then? His madness. If't be so,
4251:     Hamlet is of the faction that is wrong'd;
4252:     His madness is poor Hamlet's enemy.
4253:     Sir, in this audience,
4254:     Let my disclaiming from a purpos'd evil
4255:     Free me so far in your most generous thoughts  
4256:     That I have shot my arrow o'er the house
4257:     And hurt my brother.
4258:   Laer. I am satisfied in nature,
4259:     Whose motive in this case should stir me most
4260:     To my revenge. But in my terms of honour
4261:     I stand aloof, and will no reconcilement
4262:     Till by some elder masters of known honour
4263:     I have a voice and precedent of peace
4264:     To keep my name ungor'd. But till that time
4265:     I do receive your offer'd love like love,
4266:     And will not wrong it.
4267:   Ham. I embrace it freely,
4268:     And will this brother's wager frankly play.
4269:     Give us the foils. Come on.
4270:   Laer. Come, one for me.
4271:   Ham. I'll be your foil, Laertes. In mine ignorance
4272:     Your skill shall, like a star i' th' darkest night,
4273:     Stick fiery off indeed.
4274:   Laer. You mock me, sir.
4275:   Ham. No, by this bad.  
4276:   King. Give them the foils, young Osric. Cousin Hamlet,
4277:     You know the wager?
4278:   Ham. Very well, my lord.
4279:     Your Grace has laid the odds o' th' weaker side.
4280:   King. I do not fear it, I have seen you both;
4281:     But since he is better'd, we have therefore odds.
4282:   Laer. This is too heavy; let me see another.
4283:   Ham. This likes me well. These foils have all a length?
4284:                                                 Prepare to play.
4285:   Osr. Ay, my good lord.
4286:   King. Set me the stoups of wine upon that table.
4287:     If Hamlet give the first or second hit,
4288:     Or quit in answer of the third exchange,
4289:     Let all the battlements their ordnance fire;
4290:     The King shall drink to Hamlet's better breath,
4291:     And in the cup an union shall he throw
4292:     Richer than that which four successive kings
4293:     In Denmark's crown have worn. Give me the cups;
4294:     And let the kettle to the trumpet speak,
4295:     The trumpet to the cannoneer without,  
4296:     The cannons to the heavens, the heaven to earth,
4297:     'Now the King drinks to Hamlet.' Come, begin.
4298:     And you the judges, bear a wary eye.
4299:   Ham. Come on, sir.
4300:   Laer. Come, my lord.                                They play.
4301:   Ham. One.
4302:   Laer. No.
4303:   Ham. Judgment!
4304:   Osr. A hit, a very palpable hit.
4305:   Laer. Well, again!
4306:   King. Stay, give me drink. Hamlet, this pearl is thine;
4307:     Here's to thy health.
4308:                [Drum; trumpets sound; a piece goes off [within].
4309:     Give him the cup.
4310:   Ham. I'll play this bout first; set it by awhile.
4311:     Come. (They play.) Another hit. What say you?
4312:   Laer. A touch, a touch; I do confess't.
4313:   King. Our son shall win.
4314:   Queen. He's fat, and scant of breath.
4315:     Here, Hamlet, take my napkin, rub thy brows.  
4316:     The Queen carouses to thy fortune, Hamlet.
4317:   Ham. Good madam!
4318:   King. Gertrude, do not drink.
4319:   Queen. I will, my lord; I pray you pardon me.          Drinks.
4320:   King. [aside] It is the poison'd cup; it is too late.
4321:   Ham. I dare not drink yet, madam; by-and-by.
4322:   Queen. Come, let me wipe thy face.
4323:   Laer. My lord, I'll hit him now.
4324:   King. I do not think't.
4325:   Laer. [aside] And yet it is almost against my conscience.
4326:   Ham. Come for the third, Laertes! You but dally.
4327:     pray You Pass with your best violence;
4328:     I am afeard You make a wanton of me.
4329:   Laer. Say you so? Come on.                               Play.
4330:   Osr. Nothing neither way.
4331:   Laer. Have at you now!
4332:                 [Laertes wounds Hamlet; then] in scuffling, they
4333:                     change rapiers, [and Hamlet wounds Laertes].
4334:   King. Part them! They are incens'd.
4335:   Ham. Nay come! again!                         The Queen falls.  
4336:   Osr. Look to the Queen there, ho!
4337:   Hor. They bleed on both sides. How is it, my lord?
4338:   Osr. How is't, Laertes?
4339:   Laer. Why, as a woodcock to mine own springe, Osric.
4340:     I am justly kill'd with mine own treachery.
4341:   Ham. How does the Queen?
4342:   King. She sounds to see them bleed.
4343:   Queen. No, no! the drink, the drink! O my dear Hamlet!
4344:     The drink, the drink! I am poison'd.                 [Dies.]
4345:   Ham. O villany! Ho! let the door be lock'd.
4346:     Treachery! Seek it out.
4347:                                                 [Laertes falls.]
4348:   Laer. It is here, Hamlet. Hamlet, thou art slain;
4349:     No medicine in the world can do thee good.
4350:     In thee there is not half an hour of life.
4351:     The treacherous instrument is in thy hand,
4352:     Unbated and envenom'd. The foul practice
4353:     Hath turn'd itself on me. Lo, here I lie,
4354:     Never to rise again. Thy mother's poison'd.
4355:     I can no more. The King, the King's to blame.  
4356:   Ham. The point envenom'd too?
4357:     Then, venom, to thy work.                    Hurts the King.
4358:   All. Treason! treason!
4359:   King. O, yet defend me, friends! I am but hurt.
4360:   Ham. Here, thou incestuous, murd'rous, damned Dane,
4361:     Drink off this potion! Is thy union here?
4362:     Follow my mother.                                 King dies.
4363:   Laer. He is justly serv'd.
4364:     It is a poison temper'd by himself.
4365:     Exchange forgiveness with me, noble Hamlet.
4366:     Mine and my father's death come not upon thee,
4367:     Nor thine on me!                                       Dies.
4368:   Ham. Heaven make thee free of it! I follow thee.
4369:     I am dead, Horatio. Wretched queen, adieu!
4370:     You that look pale and tremble at this chance,
4371:     That are but mutes or audience to this act,
4372:     Had I but time (as this fell sergeant, Death,
4373:     Is strict in his arrest) O, I could tell you-
4374:     But let it be. Horatio, I am dead;
4375:     Thou liv'st; report me and my cause aright  
4376:     To the unsatisfied.
4377:   Hor. Never believe it.
4378:     I am more an antique Roman than a Dane.
4379:     Here's yet some liquor left.
4380:   Ham. As th'art a man,
4381:     Give me the cup. Let go! By heaven, I'll ha't.
4382:     O good Horatio, what a wounded name
4383:     (Things standing thus unknown) shall live behind me!
4384:     If thou didst ever hold me in thy heart,
4385:     Absent thee from felicity awhile,
4386:     And in this harsh world draw thy breath in pain,
4387:     To tell my story.         [March afar off, and shot within.]
4388:     What warlike noise is this?
4389:   Osr. Young Fortinbras, with conquest come from Poland,
4390:     To the ambassadors of England gives
4391:     This warlike volley.
4392:   Ham. O, I die, Horatio!
4393:     The potent poison quite o'ercrows my spirit.
4394:     I cannot live to hear the news from England,
4395:     But I do prophesy th' election lights  
4396:     On Fortinbras. He has my dying voice.
4397:     So tell him, with th' occurrents, more and less,
4398:     Which have solicited- the rest is silence.             Dies.
4399:   Hor. Now cracks a noble heart. Good night, sweet prince,
4400:     And flights of angels sing thee to thy rest!
4401:                                                  [March within.]
4402:     Why does the drum come hither?
4403: 
4404:     Enter Fortinbras and English Ambassadors, with Drum,
4405:                   Colours, and Attendants.
4406: 
4407:   Fort. Where is this sight?
4408:   Hor. What is it you will see?
4409:     If aught of woe or wonder, cease your search.
4410:   Fort. This quarry cries on havoc. O proud Death,
4411:     What feast is toward in thine eternal cell
4412:     That thou so many princes at a shot
4413:     So bloodily hast struck.
4414:   Ambassador. The sight is dismal;
4415:     And our affairs from England come too late.  
4416:     The ears are senseless that should give us bearing
4417:     To tell him his commandment is fulfill'd
4418:     That Rosencrantz and Guildenstern are dead.
4419:     Where should We have our thanks?
4420:   Hor. Not from his mouth,
4421:     Had it th' ability of life to thank you.
4422:     He never gave commandment for their death.
4423:     But since, so jump upon this bloody question,
4424:     You from the Polack wars, and you from England,
4425:     Are here arriv'd, give order that these bodies
4426:     High on a stage be placed to the view;
4427:     And let me speak to the yet unknowing world
4428:     How these things came about. So shall You hear
4429:     Of carnal, bloody and unnatural acts;
4430:     Of accidental judgments, casual slaughters;
4431:     Of deaths put on by cunning and forc'd cause;
4432:     And, in this upshot, purposes mistook
4433:     Fall'n on th' inventors' heads. All this can I
4434:     Truly deliver.
4435:   Fort. Let us haste to hear it,  
4436:     And call the noblest to the audience.
4437:     For me, with sorrow I embrace my fortune.
4438:     I have some rights of memory in this kingdom
4439:     Which now, to claim my vantage doth invite me.
4440:   Hor. Of that I shall have also cause to speak,
4441:     And from his mouth whose voice will draw on more.
4442:     But let this same be presently perform'd,
4443:     Even while men's minds are wild, lest more mischance
4444:     On plots and errors happen.
4445:   Fort. Let four captains
4446:     Bear Hamlet like a soldier to the stage;
4447:     For he was likely, had he been put on,
4448:     To have prov'd most royally; and for his passage
4449:     The soldiers' music and the rites of war
4450:     Speak loudly for him.
4451:     Take up the bodies. Such a sight as this
4452:     Becomes the field but here shows much amiss.
4453:     Go, bid the soldiers shoot.
4454:             Exeunt marching; after the which a peal of ordnance
4455:                                                    are shot off.  
4456: 
4457: 
4458: THE END
